Then pour a small amount of hot water into the tube: keep your hand steady and turn it back with compression on your hand for a little while, so that the edge of the tube imprints itself in the root of the clavus: and let the patient wait for a short time after the burning of the hot water, about one hour.
Then let him go, for the nail is taken out with its roots.
Do this with each one after another, until you come to all those that are in the body.
Then care for them after they have been taken out, with that which generates flesh from ointments.
¶ On the manner of cauterizing for tremors.
When a tremor occurs to someone from cold in the nerve, or from quartan fever, or another cause: then it is necessary that you cauterize him with four cauterizations, or five on the vertebrae of his back, and between every vertebra you cauterize, and on his chest you cauterize, and above his stomach you cauterize.
For the tremor is calmed by an oily cautery, and speedily comes the health of the affliction of cold.
¶ On the manner of cauterizing for Albutar ( or Albathir).
Chapter 57.
And sometimes foul things are expelled from the body, that is, pustules, which are formed from corrupted gross materials.
It is therefore necessary at first, when they appear, that they be cauterized over the entire head of each pustule with a subtle cautery made from myrtle wood, whose extremity has been set on fire. Or with the root of aristolochia, long. Or with a lentil cautery.
And sometimes they are cauterized with ademanthal, at the beginning of their expulsion according to this manner: and they do not increase: and the excess is broken up making them healthy again from it.
However it is necessary that it be done after evacuation with bloodletting.
¶ On the manner of cauterizing for a flow of blood from an artery into a wound.
A bleeding occurs from an artery which has been cut at the wound, which happens externally, or at the opening of a boil, or the burning of a limb with a cautery, and similar things: and it becomes difficult to sever him.
So when this happens to someone, then hasten with your hand to the opening of the artery and place your index finger on top of it: and squeeze it a lot until blood is pressed under your finger and nothing comes out from it.
Then place small and large oil-based cauteries in the fire, many in number: and blow on them until they become very hot.
Then take one, small or large, according to the wound and place where the artery has been disrupted, and place the cautery on the vein; after which you quickly remove your finger: and hold the cautery until the bleeding stops.
But if it is expelled when you lift your finger from the opening of the artery and extinguish the cautery, then take another cautery quickly from those that are prepared by the fire.
So do not cease to do that with one after the other, until the bleeding stops.
And beware, lest you burn the nerve which is there, and another evil occur on the sick person.
And know that when blood flows from an artery, then it is not possible to stop the bleeding: especially since the artery is large, unless with one of four methods. Or with a cautery, as we said. Or by removal, when it has not been removed. For when it is extracted, its ends contract and the bleeding stops. Or it will be tied with a strong binding thread. Or medicines of their own property to stop the blood are placed on it and a compress with a binding constriction is applied.
Whoever administers his incision with a suture, and stitches with cloth or the arrangement of things pressing upon one another, and similar things, does not benefit him at all, unless rarely.
If however it happens to someone that neither a doctor nor medicine is present: then let him begin with the positioning of the index finger on top of the opening of his vein, as we have mentioned, and constriction thereof very strongly, until the blood from the upper part of the wound becomes stuck and above the artery. And let the finger not be moved, on top of it with cold water from a very icy place always, until the blood congeals and thickens, and is cut.
And meanwhile let him consider in himself what he needs from cautery or medicine.
On the incision, perforation, and bloodletting in wounds, and similar things.
We have already said in the First Chapter, that every affliction in which a burning cautery and a medicine are suitable to be applied together, and its occasions, its causes, and its instruments, and its forms of cauteries.
And I have placed there the chapters from the top to the foot.
And I shall proceed in this chapter by the same way, so that relief may be given to him who is seeking it, and his inquiry may be fulfilled.
And before I begin it, my sons, it is necessary that you learn what in this chapter is from fear of the same as in the first chapter about cautery.
And because of this it is necessary that there be caution in it more severe, since in the operation which is in this chapter, evacuation often occurs with blood, with which life stands at the opening of a vein, or an incision on an aposteme, or the opening of an exit wound, or the treatment of a wound, or the extraction of an arrow, or an incision over a stone, and similar things: from all of which a tremor and fear arise, and death occurs in many of them.
And I warn you, that it may not fall upon you this thing of doubt, for sometimes falls upon you in this art the manners of men with manners of infirmities. For from them is he who is angry with his sickness, and death is light to him: because of the vehemence of that which he finds from his infirmity and the length of his malice. And when sickness is from fear that signifies death.
And from them is he who gives his physician and enriches you with him, hoping for health, and his sickness is deadly.
Therefore it is not fitting that you should agree to him who comes to you of this sort, whose manner is such. And let your caution be more vehement than your greed and your avarice. And do not presume over anything from him, unless after certain knowledge which verifies to you that thing to which comes a praiseworthy outcome.
And take care in the treatment of all your sick patients, with prognosis and indication of that to which it is fitting for them to be saved.
For in this indeed is aid to you towards the acquisition of reputation and glory, and memory and praise.
May God inspire you, O sons, with his understanding, and not take away from you direction and inspiration: for it is in his hand.
And I myself have arranged this Chapter distinguishing by chapters, according to what preceded in the chapter on Cauterization, from head to feet: so that it may be alleviated for you the thing which you seek from it and wherein you are in need.
About the cure of water that is accumulated in the heads of boys.
Chapter I.
Know that this illness often happens to boys at birth: or when the midwife constricts the boy's head without gentleness.
And whenever it happens even from a hidden cause which is not known.
And I myself have not seen this affliction in any others except in children, and death has hastened for all whom I saw. Therefore it seemed to me to abandon the treatment of it.
And I myself have seen a boy whose head was full of water, and his head swelled every day until he could not sit up on account of the magnitude of his head. And the moisture increased until he died.
And this moisture indeed either accumulates between the skin and bone, or it accumulates under the bone above the ear.
And the operation on him, if there is moisture between the skin and bone and an abscess is small, then you must make a cut in the middle of the head with one incision from side to side: and let the length of the incision be as long as two knots, until the moisture runs.
And this is the shape of a spatula.
If therefore the moisture is more increased and the abscess is greater, then make two incisions like these.
And if the moisture is under the bone, and its sign is that you see the folds of the head open on all sides, and the water presses down when you press it with your hand to the inside, and it does not conceal itself from you: then you must make three incisions in the middle of the head, in this manner.
And after making the incision draw out all the moisture. Then tie the incisions with bandages and lint. Then bind it over with wine and oil from above until the fifth day.
Then untie the bandage and care for the wound with threads and ointments.
And do not neglect to bind the head with ligatures. And feed the weak person with all dry food of little moisture until the limb is comforted and healed.
And another method of making an incision is, that you consider where it appears in what magnitude the swelling is, and the collection of water.
Because sometimes it is more on the back of the head, or on the forehead, or on the right side, or on the left.
So ascend with an incision to where you see the swelling and collection of water, and cut it as far as is possible for you.
And observe so that you do not cut the artery and a flow of blood occurs, and the patient dies from that hemorrhage with the evacuation of moisture.
Concerning the incision of arteries which are behind the ears.
Chapter II.
When there occurs a swelling to the eyes or to the chest, and it is inflamed, and medical treatment does not bring relief: then the last treatment for this is an incision of these two arteries.
You must therefore when you wish to cut them, so that the sick person may scratch his head. Then rub the place with a rough cloth, so that the arteries may appear. Then the sick person should bind his neck with any of his cloths. Then look where you may feel the pulse in the vein. And those places of both are the places that are behind the ears depressed: and they are rarely hidden, except in some people.
Then mark with wax on both, and cut both with a spatula from both sides carefully, the incision arriving at the mouth, and let it be that way by the width of the head.
And if you wish, insert the spatula under the artery, and cut it upwards with the incision.
And let the length of the incision be as two joined fingers.
For when the vein is cut, blood issues forth with a pulse-like emission, which leaps within frequently.
If however the artery does not appear to sense, then it is necessary that measurements be taken from the ear of the space of three fingers. Then mark with encaustic and search until the mouth.
And what needs to be let out of blood is six inches, and perhaps you let out more or less from it. The whole thing according to the quantity that appears to you from the weakness of the patient and the filling of his artery.
Then consider towards the wound. Then if something remains at the bone from seepage, then cut it so that a warm gangrene may not occur; then tie the wound with ligatures of linen cloth.
Then care for him with an ointment until he heals.
Concerning the extraction of arteries which are in wounds.
Chapter III.
When an old hemicrania or acute catarrah occurs in man on account of acidic humidities and heat in the arteries of wounds, or old soda which is gentle and similar to these: and it is treated with the modes of medical cure, and no benefit is conferred by this: then we are experienced in these afflictions that extraction of the artery from the wound, or cauterization of them, as we have narrated.
And the method of the operation in their extraction is that the patient must shave the hairs which are in the wound, and extend to the artery appearing in the wound. For it itself is shown to you from its pulse, and it rarely conceals itself unless in a few men or in great coldness.
If it conceals itself from you, then the patient should tie his head with any of his cloths; afterwards scrub the place with a rough cloth. Or warm the place with hot water until it appears to you in clear appearance. Then take a spatula which I formed a little while ago. Then peel off with him the skin with ease until you reach the artery. Then insert a probe into it and draw it upwards until you extract it from the skin and make it escape from all the tunics that are under it.
But if however the artery is thin, then wrap it around with the extremity of the probe. Then cut out from it a part according to the size in which its extremities stretch and contract, so that there may not be a flow of blood. For if it is not cut and if it is not severed, the blood does not rise up completely.
Then drain out six ounces of blood up to three.
Which if the artery is great, then it is necessary that you bind it in two places with thread double, strong, from silk: or from cord of hippopotamus, so that the putrefaction does not rush to it before the consolidation of the wound, and a flow of blood occurs.
Then cut away the superfluity which is between the two ligatures. Do that at that hour, or after another hour.
And if you wish, cauterize it with a cautery up to the bone, with a knife-like cautery, until its extremities are cut and remain in place as is the same work.
And it is better work, unless the sick person has a fever or a warm complexion. Because the cautery is from things that help to dry up humors. So it is a better treatment.
And it is necessary after the removal of arteries, that the place be filled with old catgut and well-made poultices be put on top.
And after the suppuration it should be treated with dry medicines that make flesh to grow and with liniments until it heals.
Which if in the meantime while you are operating a flow of blood occurs from the artery, then hasten to cut it off with a cautery, or that you fill the place with zege and bind your hand until the blood is cut off. If there is nothing among these remedies present for you, then place on top of the place of your finger until the blood coagulates. And bind up the place with water of very cold heat, until the sharpness ceases and becomes tightened as it should.
And from that which is lighter and easier in the extraction of the artery, this is, that you cauterize the vein with a cautery having two knives: and let the sharpness of the two knives be similar to that of an emerald. However they are much less in sharpness than two knives. Because if they are sharp, like two knives, the cold rushes to both quickly: and they do not cut the flesh with speed. Therefore since there is a little thickness in them, heat of fire remains in them and the flesh is cut quickly with this therefore it is better than any work, and easier, and more readily done.
And this is the shape of the cautery.
And let the space that is between the two knives be the thickness of a finger, after you mark two places with encaustic, and lay them both burning very hot until they reach the bone, and cut the vein in two places: so that what is between its extremities is lengthened. For they are not completely closed with this cautery.
Chapter IV.
Concerning the care for a constant flow of sharp tears from the eyes.
When there is a constant flow of tears and the course of them comes from the veins that are over the outer part of the head: and no care of medical cure comes to them with medicines, and you see the face of the infirm person already red, and they feel on their forehead a wandering, as if it were the walking of ants: and their eyes are emaciated, moist, so that the hairs around them have been eaten away by corrosion: and their eyelids are heated from the sharpness of the tears: then the ancients needed in curing them this operation, and they called it the cure with a needle.
And you should order the patient to shave the hairs that are on his face. Then you should cut three incisions on the face equally spaced according to the length of the face: and the length of each incision should be almost two fingers long, and one incision should be equally distant from the middle of the nose in the center of the forehead, and the second incision should be according to the length a little above the movement of the arm, and the third equally distant on the other side: and beware of cutting the arteries which are on both sides. And stretch out your hand along the continuation of the two jaws, and let the space of all the incisions be the length of three joined fingers. And have a piece of sponge prepared for you, or many dry cloths with which to stanch blood.
Then insert the spatula of the two sharp-edged ends, whose form will soon follow. From the incision that is on the side of the eyebrow, as far as the second incision: and scrape off with it all the skin that is between the two incisions, along with the Siffak, which is above the eye. Then do this also from the middle incision as far as the second incision. Then pull out this spatula and insert into the first incision again another instrument which is called a sharp knife, from one side smooth, not sharp on the other side. And this is its shape.
And place the sharp edge of it against the attached flesh along with the skin, and the smooth side against the bone: and push it until it reaches the middle incision: and cut off with it all the veins that go from the head to the eyes: except that the incision continue up to what appears on the skin.
Then do this in the middle incision as far as the other incision.
And when an equal quantity of blood has flowed out, press out the places from clots of congealed blood.
Then place linen from old clay on every split and put over them poultices soaked in wine, oil or vinegar. And be careful that no hot abscess may occur.
And on the third day dissolve the ligament and administer a strong infusion with warm water. Then treat it with balsam ointment and other things by which wounds are healed; until it heals.
Chapter 5.
On the cure of sharp tears and catarrhs to the eyes from internal parts of the head.
When sharp, acute, constant catarrhs occur to someone, and you see that their eyes are emaciated, small, and already weakened in appearance: and the eyelids are ulcerated, and hairs fall from them, and there is a sharp, piercing pain in the inner part of the head, causing sneezing: then take note of these accidents, that those materials and catarrhs do not come, unless from many veins.
And their better cure is this. And it is, to have the patient shave his forehead. Then make an incision in it, one wound in the middle of the forehead, or another a little to the side, and start with the left side to the right: and let the incision be up to the bone: and extend your hand from the elbows of the temples which move at mastication: until when the bone is uncovered: and dry out all the blood with a sponge.
Then separate between the two lips of the wound with old clay or with linens from linen. Then bind it on top with poultices and soak the poultices with wine and oil, so that no hot abscess may occur.
And when you dissolve it and see that the hot abscess is already decreasing: then it is necessary to rub the bone until flesh begins to grow there.
Then the treatment of this with a regimen of excitation that makes flesh grow: as if you take two parts from wheat flour and four parts from colophony, and prepare from them an ointment, and it is administered at the birth of flesh in these wounds.
Chapter 6.
Regarding its cure which falls into the ear.
All that falls into the ear is one of four kinds: either a mineral stone, or similar to a stone, as iron and glass; or it is from a plant, such as chickpeas and beans; or it is a flowing thing, as water and vinegar and things like them; or some animal.
When therefore a stone falls into the ear, or a kind of stone from among those that do not add or grow within the ear, expose the ear to the sun: if then you see the stone, then distill something from violet oil, or siseum [sesame] into the ear. Then administer an extraction of it [the stone] and make him sneeze with a condiment, and stop up his nostrils near the arrival of sneezing, after you have placed fragments of cloths or wool around the ear, and stretched out the ear upwards.
For often it exits with this cure.
But if it does not exit, then administer an extraction of it with gesti, that is, fine pomegranate rinds; its form is this.
And so if it exits with the gesti, it is good.
And if not, then administer an extraction of it with a thin blind thread of fine silk.
And if it does not exit through that [gesti or thread], then make a tube of air and insert the end of the tube into the ear opening very much, and stop up what is around the tube with softened wax with oil, so that there is no passage for wind except the tube: then draw it out with your own breath, using strong suction.
Therefore it frequently comes out with him as we have said. But if it does not come out this way then take from the bird lime or from vetch, that is a prepared gluten with which birds are caught, a small amount: and place it at the end of the beam after you have wrapped over it a cloth placed decently: then insert it into the ear canal with ease, after you have dried the ear of moisture.
Therefore if it does not come out with all that we have said, then hasten to cut into it, before a warm abscess or spasms occur.
And the manner of making an incision is, that as a barbers cut the hair on the head first, and draw from it according to its strength. Then have the patient sit between your hands, and turn his ear upwards, and make a small incision in the root of the ear near the fat there in a depressed place itself, in the shape of a crescent moon, until you come to the bone: then remove it with that which is possible from instruments.
Then close the incision with your hand quickly and take care of it until it heals.
But if there is a thing falling into the ear from one grain which grows and swells: then administer its removal.
But if it does not obey you to come out, then take a thin, subtle spatula according to this shape.
And administer with it the removal of that kind of thing from grains which fall into the ear, and do not do this when you think that grain is already moistened by the vapor of the ear, until you put in many tweezers: then draw them out with a hook or with a subtle forceps or by suction, as we said, for the removal of it is eased.
But the water which enters into the ear ought to be used by the patient with sneezing after it has been seasoned first, and already filled his ear with a somewhat hot old wine: and he is lying on top of that ear in which is the water. If therefore it comes out through that one, it is good.
And if not: then take many stones, as long as a finger, thin, soft, and heat them with fire for a little while, and the sick person should insert one of them into his ear hole, and let him urinate over his foot from that side, and strike with another stone on top of the one in his ear. Therefore he should not stop doing that with stones and stones until all the water comes out.
And now water comes out so that you take from a papyrus or a reed one, and insert the end of it into his ear, and burn the other end with fire until much of it is burned. Then repeat the reed again and do that many times until all the water comes out, and then draw it out with a pipe as was mentioned in the stone.
But in the extraction of the living creature entering into it, if it is small in body, such as a louse, and like to it, take care of it just as I said in the division.
But if it is of a large body that appears to the senses: then administer its extraction with forceps and tweezers: and its removal is easier for all that are held in the ear.
But in the extraction of the worm generated in the ear, when you cure it with that which we said in the division, and in the treatment of distillations, and your care does not help: then it is necessary to look at the ear in the sun. If therefore something appears there from worms, then draw that out with forceps or with very fine tweezers.
But if nothing appears there, then take a pipe, of which this is the shape, tight below, wide above: and insert the fine end into the ear according to what the sick person can tolerate: then suck with it strong suction. Do that many times, until all worms come out.
If they do not obey you to come out, then a small tube with honey, as I said on stone.
And if they do not come out with what we said, then administer the distillations which ancient ones have experienced in the removal of worms: and you will find it in the treatise on distillations.
And let your discharge be that which you pour oil and medicines into the ear with this instrument, whose form is depicted here. As you see, make it from silver or bronze, lower part tightened, with a small hole at the bottom, wider above.
Let it be so that by pushing what is in the concavity of the tube, it comes out from well-made bronze. And if you wish, take a reed and wrap in it cotton in a decent wrapping: then pour oil or juice or what you will from those medicines into the tube: and it itself be in the ear: then insert the reed with the cotton on top, and press out by hand with temperate compression until oil flows into the concavity of hearing, and feels it inside the sick person: and that which is poured into the ear, be already warmed up a little with fire: and beware lest what is distilled into it is very cold: for medicines do not tolerate that.
Chapter VII.
Concerning the care of the occurrence of goldsmith's burn.
And sometimes babies come out through their mothers' wombs unruptured.
And it also happens to some people that an obstruction occurs in the ear canal from a wound or a boil in it.
And this obstruction sometimes occurs in the deep part of the ear canal which cannot be seen, and sometimes it happens to the outer part of the ear canal which can be seen.
Those things that occur deep within the ear, and which the sight does not comprehend, are often difficult to heal.
And as for that which results from the sight, it is necessary to place the ear of the infirm person in the sun and look at it. Then if you see a visible blockage, open it with a small thin spatula whose shape is this: its extremity should have a small depth, where there is an acute part, and the rest of the spatule should be two smooth sides so that they do not harm the ear.
Therefore if the blockage is from flesh grown in the ear passage: then take hold of it with a small hook and cut it out with violent facility, until all the flesh is removed.
And if the blockage is deep within the ear: then take a small thin pointed probe and warm it with fire for a little while. Then insert it into the ear passage.
And if you feel the blockage with it, as the infirm person feels a heaviness of hearing, try to open it with facility: and beware lest you wound the nerves of the ear.
Then place a lint in the ear according to the size of the opening that you enveloped with Egyptian ointment. Do this for several days, until you are sure of the wound's consolidation.
Or take lint and immerse it in rose water, and powder saffron over it, and use it.
And if you see the ear already to be inflamed: then it is necessary for you to remove the lint and permit it with another cloth which you will wrap in cerate that has been made with rose oil, until the swelling cools down. Then care for it until it heals.
And if a bleeding occurs: then submerge a sponge or cloths in cold water and place them over it: and use the remaining cure which stops the flow of blood as described in the treatment of bleedings.
Concerning the care of the eyelids' corners.
Chapter Eight.
These swellings that occur on the eyelids of the eye are sometimes moist and sometimes dry.
Therefore it is necessary to tie them with thread or with a needle, and to cut them off from their roots. Then place powdered vinegar on the places.
But if there is bleeding from them, then cauterize them with a delicate lentil-shaped cautery.
And it is better to cauterize them after an incision. For they often recur when something remains from their roots. And when they are cauterized with fire, the roots burn and do not recur.
Concerning the care of the cataracts of the eyelids of the eye.
Chapter Nine.
Often something occurs in the eyelids which is similar in strength and hardness to a cataract, and on account of this it is called a Grandium: and it is a thick gathering of moisture in the upper eyelid and the lower one.
Therefore the operation in them is to see then if it is a Grandium proceeding from it which appears on the eyelid and moves in every direction, and its nature is easy.
Then therefore find an incision over it by breadth. Then peel it off from every part with ease until it comes out. Then hang it with a needle and cut it off.
But if its removal is not prepared for you, unless after you have made an incision into the eyelid a little bit, then it will harm the sick person somehow.
Which if the incision is great, then sew it up with suture and take care of it until it heals.
And if it is small, no harm will come to you from it. The ointment restores it and makes it flesh again.
And if the stone is leaning towards the inner ear, towards the internal surface, then turn back the eyelid and hang the stone with a hook; besides which you will need an incision and press it from every side.
But if the eyelid is stretched by the incision, it will not harm it in any way.
Then wash the eye with salt water after the stone has been cut out, and take care of the place with that which makes it flesh again, until the sick person is healed.
Concerning the treatment for a swelling of the eyelids.
Chapter 10.
Axirnath is a fatty swelling that arises in the folds of the upper eyelid, and it most commonly occurs in children: and it burdens their eyes, and cataracts occur in them at morning time, and they are unable to look upon the light of the sun, therefore because tears flow towards them, and on this account you see them reclining on their faces always, and sleeping on them, and their eyelids are moist under their eyebrows which have already been overlaid by swelling: and indeed the prominence of Axirnath appears to the eye, and when you press the place with your fingers you feel Axirnath between them.
And the manner of operating on it is, that the sick person should lay their head in your lap.
Then take a linen cloth and prepare in it lint, and make from this lint a circle according to the size that contains the Axirnath all around. Then place him on it and press with your fingers all around so that the Axirnath is gathered together in the middle of the circle.
Then prick into the center of that moisture with a splinter, making incisions through its breadth, and let it not be an incision deeper than is done by bloodletting.
You should find the skin to be intact, until you reach Azarnath; for indeed it proceeds from a fissure according to the shape of white fatty tissue.
Therefore draw it out with a cloth which you have infiltrated between your fingers on the outside; and turn your hand to the right and left until it disperses: and beware that you do not add anything in the fissure, lest you reach the white part of the eye and harm it.
But if however Azarnath does not appear to you at the beginning of the fissure, then you should add a little into the fissure with ease until it proceeds. Then draw it out, as we have said.
Then soak the cloths in vinegar and water, and place them on the place, and bind it with bandages.
And there are men who rub salt and place it in the concavity of the fissure, so that what remains of moisture may dissolve.
Then care for it, until it heals.
But if a hot abscess should occur in the place, then cure it with soothing plasters until it heals.
Concerning the ways of Azarnath of the eye, that is, when the eyelid becomes soft, the eye becomes inflamed as it should not.
Chapter XI.
When hair sprouts out between the eyelashes, in addition to the natural ones, and become old: then they harm the eye and cause various sufferings, as constant tears, softening of the eyelids, and a growth, until it comes to be the cause of destruction of the eye.
And indeed Azarnath of the eye is cured according to four methods. Either by cauterization with fire. Or with sharp medicine, according to what is set forth in the chapter on cauterization. This is healed with an incision and stitching. Or by canals, according to what I say.
It is necessary that you place the head of the sick person on your shoulder. Then turn the eyelid of the eye with your left hand.
Therefore if it turns, it is well. And if not, introduce the needle into it where there is a thread from the lower part of the eyelid, and make the needle pass through with the thread on top, and let it be near the hair itself, that is, near the edge of the eyelid where the hairs are born: and pull the thread to the upper parts with the eyelid, and turn it with a stick.
Then find in the inner part of the eyelid under the hairs with the spatula running from the larger tear duct to the smaller one.
Then pull out the thread and place a small dust of ashes, or cloth, under the eyelid; then mark above the eyelid with wax, so that it be like the figure of a myrtle leaf.
However, it is necessary that the figure be made according to the size and elevation of the eyelid, since it varies greatly among men.
For some of them are those who need that a great quantity be cut from the eyelid, according to the quantity which is softened by the eyelid.
Then with the spatula find above two lines which you marked, and start from the larger tear duct to the smaller one, and let there be a fissure one near the natural hairs, just as is the thickness of the stick.
Then insert the needle into one of the two angles of the skin. Then peel it off entirely. Then gather the two eyelids together with a stitch of needle and fine linen thread; and wipe away the blood, and make what overflows from the threads above the eyebrows adhere with some cauterizing things, if you wish to do so: and if not, don't bother.
Then let loose the suture and threads until three days, or four: then care for that.
And if you wish, let the wound go without a suture, and care for it with that which dries and tightens. For the eyelid is raised by the sealing of the wound and its gathering together: but the future one will be better.
This method of treatment the ancient Ataksir taught.
However, there is concern for the weak one, and it is from good surgery, and there is no fear in it.
And another method is in Ataksir also: and it is, to make signs on the eyelid a figure, which is like the figure of a myrtle leaf, as we have narrated.
Then raise the eyelid with three separate or joined hooks, according to this shape which follows, then cut off what is superfluous with small scissors, according to this shape, with equal cutting.
But if it is not possible for you to hold the hook and it does not come out even: then take a needle with thread on it, and insert it in the middle of the shape: and insert another thread near the larger tear channel, and the second thread near the smaller tear channel: and gather together between your fingers the threads with equality.
Then raise your hand with equal elevation: and raise the skin above which is marked out, as we have narrated.
Then gather together the two lips of the wound with a suture: and care for it, until it heals.
If, however, a suppurative formation occurs at your incision, or your cut: bind that suppurative formation with cerate and those similar things, from soothing ointments.
And sometimes it happens to the eyelid also, that its hairs turn.
Therefore you must use in it what we said concerning a cut: but to sew both of them is more artificial. On the care of an elephant wound with a thorn.
It is so, that you will turn back the eyelid and cut open the split that is inside, as I have told. Then make two thin sticks or canes by art, the length of which is according to the length of the eyelid and their width less than half that of a reed: and you have now cut them at both ends on both sides where you hold the threads.
Then bring both sticks together with subtlety over the excess from the eyelid of the eye and tie the two canes tightly on both sides with a strong knot, and leave it for some days. For the bound skin will die, become blackened, and rot until it falls of its own accord.
If therefore it is delayed, then cut it with scissors. Then care for it until it heals.
When therefore it becomes firm, the eyelid is raised and the hairs do not prick.
And this is the form of the two canes which you will make according to this shape and quantity in length and width.
Chapter Twelve.
On the elevation of pricking hairs in the eye with a needle.
The operation is this: you will take a thin needle and insert into it a fine thread of soft linen. Then bring together the ends of it and tie both with a very small knot, and let the length of the threads be one finger.
Then compose another fine thread under it in shortness, and tie its ends if you wish.
Then let the sick person put his head in your arm and be near the sun so that the operation may be declared; because it is a subtle or minute operation.
Then insert the needle with the Alinxotati under the root of the eyelid and make it penetrate into the eyelid quickly until you draw out from above a natural hair.
Then pull out the arrow until it is above the level of the body. And insert that arrow into the body. If there be one, or two, or three, not more.
Then pull your hand along with it until the arrow comes out through the body into the eyelid together with its own natural thread.
But if the thread is pulled out and the arrow does not come out with it, pull the thread downwards along with the string that you made in it, until the thread comes out through the upper hole and you see it with the sight of the eye. Then pull the thread and the string and take both out, and close the eye and let it remain closed for two or three days, until the thread adheres to its place and new flesh grows over it.
And if the thread is short, then either join a long one to it from the natural hairs and lift them up together; or let it be until it lengthens over time, and then lift it up.
Chapter 13.
When this Alxa'tratus happens to the eye, it is called Leporina.
And this Alxa'tratus is either natural or accidental.
The accidental therefore comes from the closure of a wound, or incision, or cautery, and similar things to these.
And the mode of operation for it is that you should find the closure itself, and separate its lids, and place a linen thread between them, and tie it until it heals.
And you should not use things that dry out and constrict for its treatment, because if you do it will make the Alxatrati worse than it was. But rather use things that soften, such as opium: and mix with water in which althea seeds have been cooked: and a diachylon ointment that has been liquefied with some oil: and wrap it around with the linen, and treat it with this.
And the treasure of your cure is that you strive with all effort, so that it may not harden, according to its original shape.
And indeed this operation which we said is nothing but a certain correction of the Alxatras [?], so that the shape of the place does not return entirely as it was.
On the care for Alxatras which occurs in the lower eyelid, and it is a wrinkling or contraction upwards to the right and left.
Chapter XIII.
This Alxatras which occurs below is truly called Alxatras. And it is natural and accidental.
And the accidental one occurs from a wound, or incision, or cautery, and similar things.
And the way of operating in it is, to take an awl, in which there is a double thread, and dye it in flesh, and make it penetrate from the left tear duct to the right tear duct, until the thread reaches both ends of the flesh.
Then stretch the skin upwards with the awl and cut it with a wide spatulum. If then the shape of the eyelid returns as is fitting, it is good.
And if not, take a needle and place it in the incision, and convert with it the eyelid: and make two cuts on the inner side of the eyelid, and let the ends of the two cuts be from both angles of the incision, until they come together and form an angle: until when they are gathered it will be their figure, and it is the Greek letter lambda.
Then remove that flesh to the extent of its sharp lower side, from what follows the eyelid. Then gather the separated parts: or sew them with a thread of wool.
Then treat them with those things which we said, about softening medicines, and liniments, until they are healed.
And if an eyelash has fallen out due to a cut, and stitching, or cautery: then it is necessary that you seek out the flat cut under the eyelid skin, even according to what happened before. Then separate between two lids with a lint or linen cloth, as we have said.
And the conclusion of the speech about the cure of Alxatrai, when it is above or below, is that the operation should be done according to the form of Alxatrai.
For indeed it has many differences in form and a wise craftsman prepares the tool with whatever means is possible to him until he makes the figure return to its natural form or close to it, and the infirm person himself, as becomes him from the operation and instruments in various dispositions.
Chapter 15.
About the sticking together of the eyelid with the conjunctiva, or cornea.
Sometimes this consolidation happens to many people in such a way that the upper eyelid sticks together with the white of the eye and prevents it from performing its own natural operations.
Therefore it is necessary that you insert the tip of a rod under the eyelid and raise it upwards, or extend it with a hook: then cut the adhesion with a thin spatula, which should not be sharp but slightly dull, so as not to disturb the patient during the operation and remove the spatula and hurt the eye with its sharpness. And let your incision be like you scrape off that adhesion easily, until when the eyelid returns according to its natural form and the whole adhesion is healed.
Then pour salt water into the eye with fresh water or distill a drop into it from a flask made from something dissolved in vinegar.
Then separate between the eyelid and eye with a lint or linen cloth, and place over the eye a woolen poultice soaked in egg white, and after three days use a flask and ointments until it is healed.
On cutting the nail and the elevation of a cutaneous excrescence of tears.
Chapter XVI.
Know that the nail is in two ways. Or it is fleshy, and is similar to skin, hard and thin. Or it is not fleshy, similar to frozen moisture; which when touched with an iron file, or you attempt to take hold of it with a nail, it breaks off and the nail does not stick to it.
And both kinds do not begin to grow, unless from the larger tear of the eye; until it gradually covers the sight, and prevents light, and motion of the eye.
And the manner of procedure in cutting it is: that the sick man places his head on your shoulder. Then open his eyes, and lift up his eyelid with your hand. Then gather the nail with a double-edged needle, and extend it upwards. Then take a needle and introduce into it a thread of horsehair or cow hair, or goat's hair: and let the thread be strong, and double the end of the needle for a little while, and fix it in the middle of the nail; and penetrate it with the needle, and tie the nail with the thread, and extend it upwards; and excise with the thread the side of the nail that follows the pupil, as if you were sewing it with the thread up to its end.
Then cut the remaining part at the root of the tear with a very fine spatula, or with small scissors; and let the natural tear flesh be left, so that there may not occur from it a continuous flow of tears.
And sometimes the nail is extended with the needle alone, or with thread, as we said. Then it should be excised with a light spatula, whose shape is this.
And beware lest the spatula touch the corneal coat and a rupture occur in it, and the pupil be perforated.
Then after making an incision, throw some crushed salt into the eye, or something made from sifted alum, and let it be until another day. Then care for him with what is necessary until he is healed.
But if the nail is not inflamed and you cannot insert an awl into it nor a hook fastens to it, then this kind of nail is often cured with medicines and heals as I narrated in the division.
But if you wish to cut it, then open the eye of the sick person and take a small, thin, weak, sharp knife, according to this shape. And scrape the nail from above with delicacy. But if you see that it becomes cracked and broken and the scraper sticks to it, then drop from your hand into the eye a thing made from sifted alum or red powder or crushed salt: and close the eye until another day.
Then repeat the same operation over it until everything is removed from it. Unless a contrary thing, a hot abscess, is in the eye: then indeed let it be and care for the abscess until it heals.
Then repeat the operation over it with a scraper, until it heals.
But the prominence of the eyelash, if it causes great harm to the eye, is suspended with a hook and an incision is made from it; and do not stop making the incision so that the flow of tears does not occur.
Then drop into the tear duct red powder or something made from alum until it heals.
About the incision of Alguarden and the flesh that forms in the eye.
Chapter XVII.
There is sometimes formed in the eyes of men a red flesh composition, or a growth that spreads until it covers the pupil, or approaches or tightens over the eyelids, and perhaps turns the eyelids outward, and it resembles a rose mallow.
And the operation for it is also this: that you place the sick person's head on your shoulder. Then open his eyes, and collect all that flesh together with the hook-shaped needles fitting for it; and hold that with a forceps or tweezers.
Then cut into the flesh first, until it is finished entirely with a razor. And beware of the eye, lest you hurt it during the operation; and let your cut be such as you are cutting with, or with a spatula, which we have mentioned in the incision of the nail: or with small forceps with which the sty is collected, according to how its shape will come out after this chapter.
When therefore your cut is completed and the entire growth removed, then fill the eye with pounded salt: or distill red powder into it and similar things from corrosive medicines; and place over the eye a poultice outside made with the white of an egg, in order that it may be secured from hot inflammation.
But if something remains of the growth and hot blood dominates over you and you fear a hot inflammation: then let go of the eye and cure it with what soothes an inflammation.
Then apply again on it with the operation, until it is healed.
And do similarly with added flesh which occurs in the eye from this kind.
However it is necessary that you refrain from treating any weak eye either naturally or accidentally by operating with iron; and do not insert your hand into any of these operations until you see that the member can tolerate that operation.
About the collection of a sty from the eye.
Chapter 18.
A sty is a red vein that grows over the eye and hinders its vision from operating properly; and weakens the eye with the length of days.
It is therefore necessary for you to consider first if the eye in which there is a sty is strong and not has any affliction other than the sty; then collect its sty.
And it is, that you may instruct the sick person, that they put their head into your arm.
Then suspend the veins with a single hook, or two according to your prudence; and let the hook be thin, like a fold, in this shape, or let there be two hooks graded in one body, in this shape.
Then collect with fine scissors those veins with delicacy, and wipe away the blood hour by hour, until you see those veins being removed from sight, and be dissolved: and beware lest you hurt the eye with the extremities of the forceps.
And let your operation be at midday before the sun, and be firm in your operation very much: so that you do not slip except those veins.
And when you have finished, then apply to the eye either green or red, so that its sharpness eats away what remains of the vein.
But if it is not possible for you to collect all of it in that hour, then apply a poultice to the eye with what soothes the pain of the eye: and leave it for some days, until the pain has ceased: and be protected from warm inflammation: then proceed over it in the same manner, until it heals.
And this is the shape of the forceps.
Of the reduction of a nasal swelling.
Chapter 19.
Doctors call the swelling of Arissat a fistula, which you cure with a cautery, or with burning medicine, according to its preceding account.
And if it does not heal, then there is no talent in it, unless you lay a thread over the abscess at its birth, and draw out all the moisture that is in it and pus, until the opening is revealed.
When therefore the opening is revealed, and you see corruption or blackness in it, then scrape it out with an instrument, whose shape is this.
And it is called Apical fracture.
Let it be made from tempered iron and let its head be round like an azir, which has been punctured with a razor's puncture or the puncture of a thin awl; and place it on top of the corrupted bone.
Then turn it back and forth between your fingers, and you press your hand upon it for a little while until you know that the corruption is now smoothed out: and do this several times.
Then restore the place with drying and astringent medicines.
And if therefore the place is consolidated and flesh grows upon it, and a cure is followed: and remains for forty days and does not recur, and is not inflamed, and nothing untoward occurs there: then you will know that it is now healed.
And if not, then it does not have a natural tendency, except to reduce the fistula back into the nostril, according to this shape.
And it is, as you would uncover a second bone with iron or sharp medicine: and when the bone has been uncovered, then take a trephine according to this shape, let its iron part be triangular; and its wood part be a thinned-out pyramid to the end, just as you see.
Then place it over the bone itself: and let it be near the eye: and extend your hand from the eye a little. Then turn your hand with the trephine until it penetrates the bone and feels weak: through this because it finds air coming out from it, when it holds its hand over its nose.
Therefore then restore the place with drying and astringent medicines, just as I have taught you.
And when flesh grows and the place hardens: then the material that was flowing out to the outside returns to the nose: so it is therefore less troublesome for the invalid.
On the reduction of an elevated eye.
Chapter 20.
When the eye itself is elevated and no injury or decrease in vision has occurred, then you must let blood from the patient; afterwards put poultices on his neck without incision, but with suction.
Then place over the eye a liniment made from acacia, and aloe, and oil of cinnamon, and wax. Then bind the eye tightly above with a poultice containing many powders.
And the manner of making the powders is this: take many soft cloths, and each cloth be doubled on itself four times; make many such things; then place one upon another, according as much as the eye requires; then bind tightly over them with a strong cloth.
And repeat the poultices with suction without incision.
Then take away the poultices and leave the eye tightly bound day and night.
But if the bandage becomes loose again, then it must be bound tightly; afterwards remove the bandage after a day and night, as we said.
Therefore if you see that the eye has returned to its natural covering, it is good; and if not, then repeat the plaster, powders, binding, and poultices until it heals.
On the incision of the eyelid's skin fold.
Chapter 21.
When a disruption occurs in the eyelid's skin fold, and it elevates and protrudes beyond the lashes like a grain of corn, and it disfigures the face of man because of it, and you want to cut it: then the operation on it is as I say, and is that you insert a point (or needle) into the root of the eyelid from below upwards.
Then insert another point into the place where there are two threads from the corner of the tear duct, and make it penetrate; and leave the first point in its position.
Then cut the spot of the son's eyelid, and tie with him a thread of five ouches around the edge of the eye: and draw it tight.
Then take out the needle, and place over the eye a thread soaked in alum on the white part of the eyelid: and let him go until the thread falls with its five ouches.
Then take care of the eye with it, that it comforts itself, until the wound heals.
And whensoever the thread is drawn tight from every part with a circle of cloth, until the eye swells greatly.
Then a fine awl will be pierced into it, so that it reaches the depth of the eye, and the fluid from the white part flows out, and the eye descends, and becomes at once clear; then bind him until he heals.
Of the cure of the Alcornutia (or Hordeolum).
Chapter XXII.
This affliction which is called Alcornutia, is nothing but a pus which accumulates in the eye, similar to water flowing down, and it is not itself.
And the manner of operation in this case is, that you make the patient sit upon a prepared seat, or upright.
Then take his head in your hands from both sides, and shake it until you see the pus come out towards the lower parts of your eyes. Then it will stick and not come down, and the light will be released: and let the patient see what it looks like as he saw before; and if it does not go down below, know that it is water.
But if the descent of the pus does not prepare itself as we said, then make the patient sit between your hands. Then take a fine awl and pierce with it above the white part of the eyelid for a little while at the continuation of the conjunctiva with the corneal tunic in the crown, until the pus comes out.
When it comes out, then let a warm water distill into the eye, in which you have mixed honey: or the water in which you have already boiled Egyptian linseed and honey: then care for him with the rest of the cure until he heals.
On the cure of water that descends into the eye, or a cataract.
Chapter 23.
We have already described the kind of water used for making an incision, and what is suitable for it; then take that same thing according to health.
Therefore it is necessary that you make the patient sit between your hands square and facing the sun, near the sunlight. And bind his sound eye tightly and tie him strongly. Then with your right hand take hold of the eyelid of his left eye if there is water in it; or his right eyelid if there is water in it.
Then take with your right hand the almaga, if the eye is on the left; or with your left hand, if it is on the right.
Then place the extremity of the almaga near the crown, by the thickness of the radius visible in the whiteness of the eye on the smaller tear duct. Then push the almaga with force and you turn your hand with it until it penetrates into the conjunctiva of the eye: and you feel that the almaga has already reached the empty space.
And it is necessary that the measure of the path of the almaga to depth be the measure of the space from the pupil to the end of the blackness and that is the crown of the eye. For you will see it indeed in the very pupil by the clear vision of the corneal tunic.
Then place the almaga up to the place where there is water. Then press it downward with a gentle force, one part after another.
If therefore the water descends immediately, then the patient will see it right away above which his vision opens up at once and the almaga is in his eye.
Then rest a little while. If therefore the water descends, then remove it secondly, apart from where the almaga comes out.
When therefore it is placed and does not rise: then extract the almond-shaped object with ease: and you revolve your hand slowly.
Then dissolve something from clear salt like a jewel in water, and wash the eye with it afterwards. Then place all the stuffing, or wool soaked in rose oil, and the white membrane of the eyelid outside the eye, and bandage with it a healthy eye.
But as for us, and those who are of our time, we place on top of it ground thyme with egg white.
Which if the almond-shaped object does not obey you for entering into the eye because of its hardness, since it is from men whose eye is very hard: then it behooves you to take a small spatula which is called Alberid, which has this shape.
Therefore pierce with it only the conjunctiva itself, and do not prolong your hand into the hole: and it is nothing but that you make a way to the place of the almond-shaped object subtle.
Then insert the almond-shaped object, as we have said.
And as a complement to your work, let a comfortable bed be prepared for the patient in a dark house: and prohibit all movements from him, and coughing: and place his food that which soothes his nature: and let him not move his head to the right, nor to the left at all.
And let the bandage be according to its arrangement until the third day.
Then dissolve it in that dark house, and try his sight: and make him see things. Then return the bandage until the seventh day.
And it is not necessary to do that in the hour of healing, or immediately after the incision: nay, it behoves you to remove it because water ascends quickly through a violent outlet.
If however a warm abscess occurs: then you must bind the eye for seven days, and correct it with that which calms an abscess until it is calmed; then without his will, and put on its face an oral mouthpiece by which its vision may be regulated over several days while he remains in that dark house.
Then he shall go out from the house gradually, and turn to his own places.
And know that in zeal a student is not excused from vision several times: then indeed he may dare to act on the operation.
And now indeed some have come to us from those who are from Alayrach, who said that in Alayrach a stomach was perforated, by which water is sucked.
Therefore truly I have not seen anyone on your earth who has done that: nor did I read it in any of the old books: and it is possible that it is new.
And these are the forms of species from a stomach, so that they may stand over him who ignores them.
They are to be made from bronze precisely: and their ends are to be triangular and pointed with the same subtlety.
Chapter XXIII.
On the care of Flesh formed on the nose.
Sometimes flesh grows on the nose of different kinds, from which is something that resembles a scorpion with many feet: and from it is hard cancerous flesh, stony in colour: and from it is soft flesh not dark coloured.
In that therefore which is from these soft flesh, not fraudulent nor cancerous, you must have the patient sit between your hands opposite to the sun, and open his nostrils, and throw a hook into those flesh parts: then pull them outwards, and cut with what you have grasped of it with a sharp pointed instrument on one side, until you know that all the flesh is already removed.
If something remained from it whose removal is not possible: then scrape that with one of the fine instruments with ease, until nothing remains of it. But if blood masters you, or an inflamed abscess occurs: then put a stop to it with what is necessary; or if it is from malignant ulcers, then hurry and cauterize it, until the blood is drained off and all flesh is removed.
Then vinegar, water, or wine should be poured into the nose after the incision. But if the nose is opened and moisture proceeds from it up to the palate, then you should know that he is already cured.
But if the moisture does not penetrate as it should, then you should know that there is born flesh within the upper bones of the nose to which the instrument did not reach with the incision.
So then it is necessary that you insert a linen thread with some thickness, and knot many knots in it: and place between each knot the length of a finger or less: and the patient will be able to send one end of the thread into the nose with a rod, or with what is possible for him after he has done this similar to a catheter: and pull out his stench, until it reaches the chest, and comes out over his throat.
And indeed children do this similar thing to the Attomy (catheter) often in schools: and it is an easy thing for whoever wants.
Then place the two ends of the thread, one end that comes out over the nose, and the other which remains in the nose. Then give him a meat-saw with the knots that are on the thread. Do this until you know that the flesh is already cut along with the threads of the thread.
Then draw out the thread and place into the nose after the removal of blood a liniment that you have mixed with Egyptian ointment: do this for three days or more, until the ointment dissolves all that remains of the flesh.
Then place at the end of the thing a tube of lead into the nose for several days until it heals.
But if it needs treatment that dries it out, use that one.
To make the head purging vessel by which olive oil and other medicines are distilled into the nose, make it from silver or copper, that it be like a small lantern, open as a shell is, and its channel similarly.
And if you wish to make a closed tube, like a reed.
And the shell of the instrument to be made for head purging should be full and hollowed out: which has a handle at its end, with which you will hold it when you heat in it oil or what you wish from juices and flowing things.
Chapter XXV.
A swelling often arises at the end of the nose and becomes large and grows with days until its appearance is foul.
And because of that it is necessary to burn it at the beginning of its appearance and to remove its roots entirely. Then place over the place either a cautery or a burning medicine which will take its place.
If therefore its cutting is delayed until it becomes large, then consider if it is stony, hard, dull in color, with little sensation: then do not touch it with iron because it is a cancerous ulcer.
For I have often seen that when a surgeon cut into an ulcus which was become malignant, and it happened to him who had it that there came upon him a great misfortune.
And if the ulcer is soft to touch, not dull in color, and you see that its cutting is possible throughout it, then evacuate the patient and cut into it without fear, without anxiety: and treat the place with what dries and tightens until it heals.
Chapter 26.
On the suturing of the nose, and lip, and ear.
Know that when a separation of continuity occurs in one of these cartilages, it is of little concern in the operation, unless in some men.
Therefore, when something happens to him that you should consider whether it is a recent wound with its own blood; and then gather the two edges of the wound together with a suture; thereafter take care of it until it heals.
And if the lips which have been separated by a break in continuity are already separated and each part has become sound, then you must scrape away all that part from its visible skin until it bleeds. Then gather the two lips together with a suture and draw them tight, and dust over it dragon's blood powder and ground galbanum; and place above the powder a fragment of powdered emplastrum diaphanicum, or another kind of consolidating plaster; and let him be bound for two days, or three. Then loosen it and change the medicine, and let him go until the strings are cut off by themselves. Then care for him with ointment, until he heals.
But the method of suturing is to gather together the break in continuity with needles, as we have narrated in the suturing of the belly: or gather it together with a thread, just as I have taught you.
Chapter 27.
On the removal of knots of the lips.
Indeed, many men experience hard pustules resembling grains of a herb, and some of them are smaller, and some greater.
Therefore you must turn the lip over and scrape over all the knot on it, and hang him up with a hook, and cut him off from every part.
Then fill the place after cutting with powdered zinc until the blood is congealed.
Then it should be applied with vinegar, and the place where there is a sting should be treated with it until the wounds are healed.
Chapter 28.
On making an incision of flesh born over the gum.
Often flesh grows over the gum which is called by the ancients abuluss.
Therefore it should be suspended with a hook, or held with forceps, and an incision made at its root, so that the pus may flow or the blood.
Then place over the place powdered zygium, or other stinging powders, drying.
But if that flesh returns after the cure, for it often does return, then make an incision in it a second time and cauterize it; for it will not return after the cautery.
Chapter 29.
On filing teeth with iron.
Sometimes they accumulate on the surfaces of teeth from inside and outside, and between the gum and rough, foul cores; sometimes they are denigrated and become yellow, green, until corruption reaches the gum and the teeth are fouled by it.
Therefore you should make the patient sit between your hands, and his head in your bosom, and file the molars and teeth in which the cores appear and are similar to sand: until nothing remains of them, and similarly do this for blackening, and greenishness, and yellowness, and other things until they are cleansed.
If therefore what is inside them from the beginning of filing is removed, it is good; and if not, then repeat the filing over them another day, a second time, and a third, until finally diligence reaches what you desire.
And know that the tooth needs files of diverse forms and many figures: according to what is prepared for your operation; therefore, the file with which the tooth is pared from the lower part is another than the file with which it is pared between the teeth, in other shapes.
And these razor shapes should be prepared for you which you see.
Chapter 30.
On the removal of toothache pain.
It is necessary for you to cure the tooth from its pain with every skill and delay not in extracting it. For it is not the case that its replacement can fill its place when it is extracted: since it is a high substance: until there is no excuse for extracting it, then it is necessary to extract it when the patient is forced, so that it may be firm and certified in you as a hurting tooth.
For often pain deceives the patient and he thinks that there is a sliver in the tooth and extracts it: then the pain does not subside until the patient's tooth is extracted.
For we have now seen this from the procedure of bloodletters.
When therefore it is certified to you that the tooth itself is aching, then it is necessary for you to scarify around the tooth with a spatula in which there is some virtue, until the gum dissolves from every part: and then move it with your fingers or with fine forceps little by little, until you shake it.
Then place the forceps then in it of good placement, and the head of the infirm person between your knees: which you have already held well so that it does not move: and then pull the tooth according to straightness, so that you do not break it.
If therefore it does not come out, then take some of those instruments and insert it under it from every part with ease, and try to move it as you did at first.
If however the tooth is perforated or corroded: then it is necessary for you to fill that hole with a cloth and tie it with the extremity of a thin splint, so that it does not break at the time of your constriction upon it with forceps.
And you should act carefully with a scarification around the gum from every part very much.
And preserve your strength so that you do not break it, and a part of it remains, and it returns over the sick person from that same malice which was greater in its first pain.
And beware lest you do that which ignorant bleeders do in their haste and boldness, and the removal of it, other than what we have told you to administer.
For many times they make great infirmities occur in men, the breaking of which is more difficult and some or all of their roots remain: or else they extract it with some bones of the jaw: as we have testified has happened many times.
Then it should be closed after its removal with wine, or with vinegar and salt.
But if a flow of blood occurs from the place (for it often does so) then apply some alum to it and fill in that place with it: and if not, cauterize it if you do not have alum.
The forms of the pincers should be fine and slender at their extremities, short-handled and thick so that they do not bend when pressed over a tooth: like the handles you see which are thick until you grasp them with them, then they do not bite themselves nor bend. The extremities should be short and made of iron or well-made bronze, straight. And in their extremities there should be teeth, some of which enter into others and grip with decent constriction and strength.
And sometimes the extremities are shaped like the beak of a bird called a stork, and they have strong gripping power.
Chapter 31.
On the removal of teeth and the extraction of broken jaw bones.
When something remains after the removal of a tooth that breaks, then you must put butter on the place for one or two days until the location is softened: then insert into it forceps or pincers, whose extremities resemble the beak of a bird called a stork. The form however of forceps is this.
Let their extremities be made like a file behind, or like scales.
So if he does not obey you to exit with these forceps, then it is necessary that you make an incision above the root, and strip all the flesh, and insert the small instrument called little pincers, whose form is this.
Let it be short of extremity, coarse a little bit, and not be wet, so that it does not break.
So if therefore the root goes out through it, it is good; and if not, it must be helped with these other instruments whose forms are these.
The form of the first triangular extremity, in which there is some coarseness.
The form of the sharp triangular extremity.
And with other instruments and tools which have preceded in the treatment of teeth.
And it is also helped with this instrument which resembles a large claw, of this form, triangular extremity twisted, in which there is some coarseness, so that it does not break; and let it not be wet.
And know that there are many instruments for the teeth; and similarly the other tools do not count, and a skilled craftsman, subtle in his art, finds an instrument by himself according to what each operation and illness signifies.
Because of the illnesses for which the ancients did not say that there was an instrument, on account of their diversity.
But if the bone breaks from the jaw or from other bones of the face, or it rots: then examine above it in its place with that which suits him among instruments and forceps for the extraction of roots, and help with this tool, whose form is this.
While sitting in something of a somewhat rough consistency, so that it may be tightened or filled with the mouth, and not escaping and wandering, until the mouth goes out: and restore the place with medicines fitting to it.
If however there is a small rot in the bone, then scrape it from its own rot, and care for it, until it be healed.
Chapter 32.
On the cutting of teeth that project over others.
When the projecting teeth do not grow according to their natural course, and become foul of form: and especially when it happens in women, and girls, namely the venal.
Therefore you should consider. For if the tooth has grown after another tooth, and does not belong there, or can be cut off, nor polished itself: then remove it: and if it is attached to another tooth, then cut it with this instrument, whose shape is this.
It has the form of a small beak, and is made from hard iron, very sharp at its end.
And let your cut be such that you may cut it on many days, and because of the hardness of the tooth, and so as not to disturb another from the teeth.
If however it is projecting exposed to its polishing, then polish it with a file of iron, whose shape is this.
Let it be all of iron, and its handle have a large number of sharp points, like a file with which needles are made: with which the tooth is polished gradually over many days with ease, lest the tooth should be disturbed and fall: then smooth it out, at last scrape it with certain rasps.
And if it is a tooth from which a part breaks and hurts the tongue: then you must file it also, until the roughness of that break is removed, and it is smoothed out: and flattened, so as not to hurt the tongue, nor corrupt the speech.
Chapter Thirty-Three.
On the treatment of teeth with golden wires.
When the anterior teeth are knocked loose and shaken by a blow or a fall, and an infirm person is unable to hold onto what they eat lest it fall out, and you have treated them with astringent medicines, but the treatment was unsuccessful, then the method for them is to be tied with a gold wire: because silver turns green and rots after days, but gold never changes.
And let the thread be of middle fineness in subtlety and thickness according to the amount that goes between the teeth.
And the form of the retention is this: take a thread and insert twice its length between two sound teeth; then weave it with both ends of the thread into the loose tooth, whether one or more are so, until you reach the other sound tooth. Then repeat the weaving until you reach the part from which you began; and bind your hand with subtlety and wisdom, until it is not moved at all; and let your constriction be such when you bind the thread, at the roots of the teeth, that it does not escape.
Then cut off the superfluous ends of the thread with scissors; and gather both together, and turn them with your fingers; and conceal them between the sound tooth and the loose tooth, so that they do not irritate the tongue; then let them remain thus tightly bound until it remains.
But if it breaks or is cut, bind them again with another thread, and it will stand thus for a whole time.
And this is the form of the teeth, and the figure of the retention of two sound teeth, and two loose teeth, as you see.
And sometimes it happens that one tooth, or two, return after a fall on each of their places, and are retained as we have narrated, and they remain; and only an experienced, skilled, and prudent craftsman can do this.
And sometimes a splint is made from bones of a kid: and it is formed like the shape of a tooth, and it is placed in the place, from which the tooth is supported and held fast, as we have said: and it remains, and serves with him for a long time.
Chapter 44.
Sometimes this ligament which occurs under the tongue is either natural with which man is born, or accidental from a wound that has already been consolidated.
And the operation on it is, that you open the mouth of the sick person in your arm and elevate his tongue; then cut through the above mentioned nerve-like ligament from side to side, until the tongue is released from its hold.
But if there is something hard and nodular in it, and that is from the consolidation of a wound: then throw into it a hook, and find through its breadth until you cut the ligament, and the knot will be released.
And beware lest there be a fissure in the deep flesh, and you cut an artery there, and bleeding occurs.
Then it should be covered with the track of the incision with rose water, or vinegar, or cold water.
Then place a linen poultice under the tongue which the sick person should hold all night, so that it will not become stiffened again.
But if bleeding occurs, then place powdered alum on top of the place. But if the blood overpowers you, then cauterize the place with a lentil-shaped cautery that is suited to it; then cure it with the remaining treatment until it heals.
Chapter 35.
Sometimes an abscess occurs under the tongue which resembles a small frog's bladder, preventing the tongue from performing its natural function: and it perhaps becomes so swollen that it fills the mouth.
And the operation for this is, that you open the mouth of the sick person before the sun: and examine the pustule.
If therefore you see it of a dark or black colour, hard, and the sick person does not feel it: then do not touch it, because it is a cancer.
And if it be inclining to whiteness, in which is moisture, then insert a needle into it and cut it with a sharp small spatula, and free it from every part. Which if blood flows over you in the hour of your operation, then place over it crushed alum until the bleeding ceases: then return to your operation, until you extract it with its supplement.
Then bind it with vinegar and salt: and care for it the rest of the cure fitting for it, until it heals.
Of the cure of a pustule on two Pomegranates (amigdalae).
Chapter 36.
And sometimes something occurs between the throat gland, that is, tonsils, which resemble goads, which occur outside: and are called two Pomegranates, which when you cure them with what we said above in a division, and they do not heal, then examine them.
If therefore it is a pustule hard, dark colour, little sensation: then do not touch it with iron.
And if it is of a red colour, and its root is thick: then do not touch it with iron, fearing a flow of blood, or even let it alone until it matures. And then either you will pierce it, or it will burst itself.
And if it is of white colour, round, and its root is thin: then you must cut it.
And the operation for this is, that you examine before your operation, if the pustule on it has cooled down quietly and completely, or diminished in some degree: then therefore make the sick person sit before the sun, and his head be in your bosom: and open his mouth, and let a servant take hold of it between his hands, and press his tongue downwards with an instrument, whose form is this.
Let it be made from silver, or bronze, which is as fine as a knife.
Since therefore you press with it your tongue, and a boil shows itself to you, and falls on it the view of yours: then take the hook, and thrust it into the kernel, and draw out from it as far as you can to the exterior, except that you pull with it something from the tunics.
Then cut it open with a tool, of whose form this follows. It is similar to forceps: however its extremities are curved, so that the opening of each one is directly opposite the other's point: whence be careful that it be made of tempered iron, or of bronze imbued.
But if this tool is not present, then cut it open with a pair of scissors, whose form follows here. Let it be sharp at one part, not sharp at the other.
And after you have cut one kernel, then cut another similarly in this same form of incision.
Then after the cutting, let the patient gargle with cold water, or with vinegar and water.
But if there occurs to him a flow of blood, let him gargle with water in which pomegranate rinds have been boiled: or leaves of myrtle and such things as are similar to them from styptics, until the bleeding ceases: then care for him until he heals.
And sometimes other boils appear in the throat, apart from the two tonsils. Cut them therefore according to what I said about cutting the two tonsils equally.
And I myself have already cured a woman from an abscess which had formed between her throat, that was drawing to blackness, and she had few senses, because it had almost closed the throat: and the woman was breathing through a narrow passage, and she had already forbidden her eating and drinking water: and she had already come to death if it had remained one day, or two days.
And the boil had risen so much that it had emitted two branches, until they came out over two nostrils.
