I therefore bound them hastily, and tied one of their hooks together. Then I pulled it, and a large portion came out from it.
Then I cut it off where it could be grasped through the nostril.
Then I did to him what had protruded from his other nostril.
Then I opened his mouth and pressed his tongue: and afterwards tied the hook into the boil: then I cut off from it a part of it, and there ran out from it only little blood: so therefore was the woman's throat cured, and she began to drink water immediately, and then took food.
So I did not cease to cut off from that boil many times for a long time: and the boil succeeded in its place which I was cutting, until it was prolonged too much and with it.
I therefore contrived, and cauterized the boil between the throat.
It therefore stood still from further increase: then I walked away from her part, and did not know what God had done with her afterwards.
¶ On making an incision in a boil of the uvula.
Chapter XXXVII.
When the catarrh has descended to the uvula and it boils and is long: then itself is called perpendicular: and if it is in the lower part thick, rounded, then itself is called uvva.
When it is cured with the thing which we said in the division, and the treatment does not help: and you see that the boil has cooled down from it, and is fine: then it is necessary to cut it.
And what is attached to it rounded and not in length, and is bloody, or of a fluid colour, or black, and there is no sense in it: then it is necessary to make a cut into it. In this case indeed there is fear over the ailing person.
It is necessary therefore when you see her according to the method I said of light and length, that you make the ailing person sit before the sun: and press her tongue with the tool about which I previously spoke: then insert the hook into one side and pull it down: and cut her with one of the two instruments which I mentioned in the incision of the two almonds.
And you must not cut into it except that which is added over the natural term without addition.
For if you cut into it more than that, you will harm the voice and speech.
Then after the incision use on it what we narrated in the incision of the two almonds, and care for her until she heals.
But if the ailing person is frightened by the incision, then it is necessary that you use your ingenuity in cauterizing him without fear and terror.
And the method of cauterizing her is not except with sharp water: and it is to place the head of the ailing person on your shoulder. Then press her tongue with the tool which we mentioned. Then take from the water which I told you to in the chapter of cauterizing and mix with it a coal not extinguished, and place it not dense, nor subtle, and fill the concavity of this tool, whose form is this.
Let the extremity of it to which the medicine is placed be hollowed out, like the concavity of a snail shell.
And place the instrument with the medicine on her own uvula: and let the ailing person lie on their side so that saliva may flow from his mouth from within the medicine itself, lest anything fall from it into his throat and harm him.
Then hold your hand with the medicine. And you from the first place it on the uvula a quantity of the time of half an hour, until you see that it is already darkened and the pain of the medicine has been assuaged.
And if you wish to take a splint, and wrap it over the end of the radius, and pour medicine into the splint, and insert the radius with the splint into the canal from above, until the splint adheres to the bone.
Do that many times, until you achieve what you desire by cauterizing the vein. Then release it: for indeed it dries out and falls after three days, or four.
Which if you need to renew the medicine, renew it: and after cauterizing wipe around the vein with a stick soaked in butter, and cleanse with that which is in its surroundings from the medicine: and bind it with cold water.
And it should be treated externally with ointments, and then internally with cataplasms, until it heals.
Therefore this method of treatment is more wholesome than an incision and farther away from fear.
And sometimes the vein is healed even with what is finer than a cauterizing iron and an incision: for it is healed by fumigating, in this manner. Take alum, and hyssop, and origanum, and rue, and saffron, and chamomile, and abrotanum, and similar things to them from herbs: and gather all these together, or some of them in a pot, and they are drowned with vinegar, and they boil: and the pot is plastered with clay decently: and there is a hole in the middle of the pot, over which an instrument with a concave shape will be composed according to this manner.
It should be made of silver, or bronze: and the extremity, in which is the powder, will be inserted into the mouth of the sick person, until the vapor ascends to the vein above the tube, until the vein is very obscured.
Then repeat over it many times, until it dries up.
And beware, lest you do this treatment at the beginning of an ulcer's arrival. For many wounds it adds a poultice in suppurating abscesses: and thou oughtest not to do that except at the decline of the fever of the abscess.
But if this instrument is not present to you, then take a stick of wood and compose it at the end with the rind of an egg, so that the tooth of the infirm one does not burn: for the rind of an egg prevents the vapor from burning the tooth: and this is from good healing with health.
Concerning the removal of splinters, and bones from the throat.
Chapter 38.
Often something adheres to the throat, a bone or a fishbone, or some other thing.
Therefore you should remove that which is appearing on what falls under sight, after you have pressed the tongue with an instrument in the sun, and it has been declared to you that it is in the throat.
And what does not appear to you and is hidden in the throat, it behoves that you should make the patient vomit before food has been digested in the stomach. For perhaps indeed something attached will come out through vomiting.
Or the infirm one swallows a piece of dried turnip, or the root of lettuce, or swallows a dry crumb of bread; or takes a piece of dry sea sponge and binds it up with a thread, then swallows it. When therefore it reaches the place where are the splinters or bone, and is drawn out, then it comes out.
If therefore it does not come out with what we said, then use an instrument of lead according to this shape.
Let it be thicker than a radius a little, and at the end of it let there be a curvature, that you may insert it into his throat with ease; and let itself have its head elevated upwards.
And beware of touching his epiglottis, lest coughing happen to him; and push with it the bone or splinter, or insert it himself with his hand.
It is true that an infirm person should intervene, that is to say, it is better: because of their knowledge about the place of the thing attached; and they push downward or draw their hand upward with a tool, all of this according to the quantity which has been prepared for them, until it emerges.
On the removal of the leech.
Chapter Thirty-Nine.
When the leech is being treated with what I said in the section on treatment with medications, and it does not adhere, then consider in the throat of the infirm person before the sun, or after the sun when you have pressed his tongue with the instrument which I told you about.
If therefore your gaze falls upon the leech, take it out with a small hook or with delicate fingers.
And if it is not possible with them [delicate fingers or hook], then take a concave tube and insert it into the throat of the infirm person until near the throat; then insert a glowing iron rod into the concavity of the tube. Do that many times, and let the infirm person abstain from water all day.
Then let him take a vessel full of cold water, and open his mouth in it; and let him wash his mouth with it, and not swallow anything from it; and let him move the water one hour after another with his own hand. For the leech immediately falls when it feels the water.
But if it does not come out with what we said, then fumigate the infirm person with cumin or with asafoetida with the instrument which I told you about for the fumigation of a uvula.
Do that many times, because it itself will fall.
And the mode of operation in fumigation is, that you take a pot in which are fresh prunes with fire; and the pot shall be covered with a cover, in whose middle there is an opening; and arrange the end of the instrument in that opening.
Then let him place a smoke there, and the patient put his mouth at the end of the tube, and close his mouth so that the smoke does not escape, until you know that the smoke has reached the bloodsucker. For the leech will immediately fall.
And if it does not fall, then repeat the smoke many times.
And let the patient be thirsty, and eat salt, and onions, and not drink water.
It is therefore necessary that he come out with this regimen.
And this is the shape of the instrument with which the leech is drawn from the throat when it falls on it in sight.
It resembles forceps, as you see: except that it has this curvature which is introduced into the throat. And its extremity resembles the beak of a bird which is called a stork, in which there is the roughness of a nail: when it has grasped something, it does not let it go altogether.
Of the opening of apostumes, and their cutting, and care for them generally.
Chapter 40.
The species of apostumes are many, of various kinds, according to the memory of them, that is, one and one which is mentioned in this book: and they differ in their perforation and cutting in two ways.
One of them is a type of aposteme that forms within itself, and because it gathers from moistures.
And the second way is because of the places in which it occurs on the body.
For an aposteme that occurs on the head is one thing from an aposteme that occurs in the anus: and an aposteme that occurs in a fleshy place is another from an aposteme that occurs at a joint: and each of them has its own judgment from experience.
And from apostemes are those which should not be perforated unless after the maturation of health in them and its completion: and from these are those which should be perforated while they are raw, and do not mature according to their completion.
As for boils which are situated near joints.
For since a boil which occurs near a joint and prolongs itself until what is surrounding it putrefies may possibly corrupt the tendons or nerves of that joint; wherefore there is a more probable cause of its adjacent member's contraction.
Or it is a boil on the main member itself; for if you delay making an incision until it matures, it will harm either that one or the adjacent main member.
Or it is near the anus, therefore make an incision on it unripe: and you should not cut into a fresh boil, not yet completed in digestion, except that which is near the anus, so that it does not putrefy deeply and extend to the inner parts of the anus, and form a fistula, or remain at its end and not heal.
You should know the hour of making an incision on a boil that has matured according to its completion; and it is at the station of relief from pain, abatement of fever, diminution of redness and pulsation, and sharpness of the head of the boil: and the other signs.
And you should make the same incision in the lower part of the boil and more prominent elevation; and let the incision be going along in length through the body, if the boils are on parts of the hands and feet, and places of arms, tendons, nerves, arteries, and at last in all equal locations where there is no reflection.
But in those which are reflected, make the incision along with it according to that member.
And when the boil is on fleshy places; then it is better to leave its incision until its digestion is confirmed, as we said according to completion.
For if you make an incision on it before that, the course of the virus is prolonged from it, and there are many filth and foulness, and perhaps its lips become hardened and its depth becomes greater.
And certain boils are pierced according to the breadth of the body in necessity; or according to what a limb needs.
And it is necessary to administer a small piercing in small boils and a wide piercing in large boils; or many cuttings according to the likeness of the size of the boil.
And there are boils from which it is necessary to cauterize the skin and cut when already it has become like a cloth and has turned at the end that is already dead such as those that occur in many cases, as an ulcus or a bubo.
And from them are those which are cut with a cutting having three angles. And from them is that from which it is cut like the figure of a myrtle leaf.
Like an inguinal boil.
And from them are those in which a round cut and a crescent cut are administered: and similar to these from cuts.
Indeed that which does not have a head, such as soft and superficial boils, it is necessary to pierce it with a shallow piercing, or only a flat one.
And it is necessary when the boil is large and has already gathered much matter to pierce it so that the pus does not form and you extract the whole pus at once; or take a part of it from it. Then cover the boil with an ointment until another day. Then extract something again from the pus again.
Do this many times according to the order, until it comes out: especially if the patient is weak in strength, or a pregnant woman, or a young child, or an elderly decrepit person: for the spirit of life often dissolves from one animal with the egress of pus, and perhaps the sick person will die, and you won't notice.
Therefore beware this chapter with great caution.
And after your piercing, by which you pierce boils, it is necessary to clean the wound and observe. If therefore there is a wound of a small boil, or it is a flat cut, then administer linseed from flax, or old wine. And if it is a large boil, and there are many perforations of the fissure, then you must introduce linseed into all the fissures, until they come together.
And if in the boil you have cut off a part of the skin, or hollowed it out around it: then you must fill it with old wine, or as when cloth is sewn, or wool is shorn from flax without moisture: and bind it until the third day.
Then throw it out, and take care of it with that which is necessary from an ointment, until it heals.
But if there occurs a flowing of blood at the time of your operation, then administer cold water and vinegar, after a cloth has been soaked in them: and place it repeatedly over the place of the flowing of blood.
But if the flowing of blood persists, then you must administer a dressing, and care, and powders, which I have narrated in many places of this our book, and from division.
But if it is at the time of winter, and the place of the boil is of many nerves: then you must pour in powders with wine and hot oil: and place them over the places.
And if it is in summer, and the places are fleshy: then you must place powders soaked in water and oil, or them in wine and oil, and all that is cold. Until when the third day comes, as we said, then it is necessary that you dissolve the boil, and wash it away, and administer to its cure what is fitting for it, until it heals.
This then is what is necessary from the knowledge of the cure of boils universally.
According to the method of distinction, I have already spoken about every abscess, how there is a way to its cure chapter by chapter individually.
Chapter 41.
On the cutting of abscesses which occur on the skin of the head.
Small abscesses occur on the skin of the head, and they are similar in appearance to boils, which are enclosed by pouches, that is, the skins, which contain them, like eggs hatched by a hen.
And their kinds are many.
Among them are those that are fatty; and among them are those which contain moisture similar to mud: and among them are those which contain moisture similar to porridge and puddings: and similar to them, and among them are those which are hard and stony.
And in the cutting of all of them, and their extraction there is no fear, as long as at their cutting does not conflict with an artery.
And the operation on cutting them is, that you experiment with them by means of an instrument, whose shape will be described afterwards, which is called Intromissum, until you know what it contains.
For if it is that which contains moisture, then cut it out with a superficial incision following its length, in this shape.
And begin with an incision from the line b to the line g. And when the moisture has been evacuated, then scrape out the cyst which contains that moisture, and let nothing be left in it.
For indeed many times it returns if anything remains in it.
Then immerse the poultice in Egyptian ointment, if it is available: and if not, in water and salt: and fill the wound with it, and leave it until another day. For indeed it will rot what remained from the cyst.
Then repeat the poultice on top of it with Egyptian ointment a second time, and a third time, and if you need to use more, do so until you are confident that nothing remains from the cyst. For then indeed you can cure the wound with ointment, until it is healed.
If the apostema contains a thick knot: then cut it according to this form, with an incision in the shape of a cross: and throw into the incision some salt between the two blades of the incision, and scrape off from all parts: and try your power in extracting the skin that contains it.
But if a vein makes difficulty for you, then do what I shall tell you afterwards.
And similarly act in an apostema, if it is stony, from the incision and its cure is the same, according to what we said.
And the cut over a stony apostema is easier: because there is little blood and moisture. And sometimes among these apostemata are some found on the head of certain people, in which there is no moisture at all.
For I cut over an apostema on the head of an old woman and found the apostema to be like a solid and hard stone, rough, white, which could not be broken: and if anyone struck it with something throwing, he would hurt himself.
But on other accidental apostemata occurring on the head, apart from these indeed which occur on the heads of children and near the roots of the ears, their cutting is a superficial cut.
Or make a perforation in them always from the lower part of them, so that the flow of pus may be eased to the lower parts.
Then cure those with what is suitable for them among the cures.
About cutting Officiale sores on the neck.
Chapter 42.
These apostemata often occur on the neck and under the armpits and thighs: and sometimes they occur in the rest of the body.
And on those that occur on the neck, there is one and many of them, and they are generated from one another: and every Officiale sore out of them is within its own skin, just as it is in knots and head apostemata, as we have described.
And the shapes of these strophulae are many. And from them there are lithic, and from them those which contain humidity, and from them the malignant ones, which do not obey cure.
Therefore, that one which you see of good disposition by touch, whose manifest color is like the skin's, and it moves to every part, and is not joined with the nerves of the neck, nor with the jugular vein, nor with the artery, nor is it deep: it must be cut off from the upper to the lower part of the body along line b. to line g., and scrape it completely: and extend two folds of skin with a hook or with many hooks if you need it, just as we said in head apostemats, and pull it out gradually.
And beware lest you cut the vein, or the nerve.
And let the spatula be not sharp at all: lest you cause yourself with the cut, or trouble the patient, and cut what is not necessary.
But if you cut the vein, or artery, and it prevents you from operating, then put some ground zegi, or certain powders that stop blood, into the wound, and bind the wound, and leave it until the sharpness of the apostemat ceases, and the wound softens: or rather let it begin to putrefy.
For blood is stopped by a cloth Therefore return to your operation until you complete it.
Then inquire with your index finger if anything remains of the other small strophulae: therefore cut them and clean them.
And if there is a large vein at the root of the strophulae: then it must be that you do not cut that strophula from its root: nay, but rather tie it with a doubled strong thread, and leave it until it falls on its own without harm.
Then fill the wound with a piece of old cord, which you have already immersed in Egyptian ointment. Then take care of it.
If a swelling develops around the wound, then it is necessary to close together the two lips of the wound; and sew it up immediately, as soon as you see that there is no excess remaining.
But if you see that there is excess remaining because of the size of the swelling, then it is necessary to apply yourself to cutting out the root of it, and pulling it out; and to administer a suture such as we said.
And what contains humidity among the swellings, pierce it with a superficial puncture; so that you may see the place of its maturity; and place the puncture below from what follows in the lower part of the body, as we said.
Then apply linseed with ointment to it after the puncture, and similar things to them, so that what remains from corruption may rot away; until when you see that the wound is now clean; then take care of it with a unguent born of flesh generation, until it heals.
Chapter 43.
About the cutting off of the epiglottis from an abscess occurring inside the throat.
The ancients said this cutting into the epiglottis and I have not seen anyone on our earth who has done it; and this is the writing of their speech.
But in those having dyspnea, it is necessary to avoid cutting into the epiglottis: because there will be no benefit through it; on account of which all the veins and lung are weak.
But in those who have a hot abscess in the mouth, or throat, or both tonsils, when there is no suffering in the canal, it is necessary to make an incision into the epiglottis, in order to flee from the destruction that comes from suffocation.
It is necessary therefore when we wish to do it, that we cut into the epiglottis under three or four circles of the epiglottis, with a small incision through breadth, in what is between two circles by quantity, so that the incision may be in the mucous membrane, not in the cartilage: and this indeed place is fitting for an incision, because it is private from flesh, and the vessels of blood are far from it.
If the surgeon is timid, then it is necessary that he extends the skin of the throat with a hook. Then he cuts the skin, until it reaches the canal; pus or blood flows out, if he sees anything from them.
Then he sutures the incision that we have described; and it is indicated over the cut of the canal because of the pus that comes out from it, with it being torn and the cutting of the voice.
And leave the wound open for some time.
So when however the hour is reached in which a closure is feared, gather together the two lips of the wound of the skin, and sew them up by themselves alone without cartilage.
Then administer medicines that make flesh grow, until it heals.
It is said by Albucasis. The sum of what we have narrated in this speech is that the explanation is not given unless it is seen that one of these abscesses has already obstructed the throat of the infirm person: and the infirm person is raised up to the point of death, and wants his breathing cut off: they go to cutting the Epiglottis so that the infirm person breathes over the place of the wound with some respiration and is saved from death: and for this reason they have commanded to leave the wound open until the evil of the illness is consumed, and it is an evil of three days, and as if itself: then therefore they have commanded to sew up the wound and cure it until it heals.
And that which I saw by myself is that one slave girl took a knife and sent it into her throat and cut with it the part of the lung's canal.
And I was called to heal her, and I found her bellowing like a mooing of the dead.
I therefore drew out from the wound and found the blood that had flowed out from the wound to be small. So I was assured that the very thing had not been cut off nor cauterized; and air was escaping from the wound.
I immediately sewed up and cared for the wound itself until it was healed; and nothing happened to the maid servant except that her voice became hoarse, and she returned after a few days to better condition.
Therefore I say, there is no fear in cutting into the epiglottis.
On the cutting of a hernia of the throat.
Chapter 44.
This hernia called apostema of the throat is a great swelling, according to the color of the body, and it occurs frequently in women.
And it is in two modes: either it is natural, or it is accidental.
Certainly there is no art in the natural kind.
The accidental kind, however, is in two modes: one of which is similar to a swelling with pustules. And the other mode is similar to an apostema that forms from the blockage of an artery. And in it there is fear, and one should not make a presumption with iron on it unless it is small; and you will test it, and investigate it with an instrument. If therefore you find it similar to a swelling with pustules, and it is not hanging from any veins; then cut it out, just as you would cut out a nodule; and extract it along with that which contains it in the sac, if it is in a sac: and if not, then thoroughly investigate all of it. Then care for the place with what is necessary from treatment, until it heals.
Chapter 45.
On the cutting of above the type of Nodules.
There are many types of nodules, and I have already mentioned all their kinds in the division; and it is necessary that I narrate here the difference between a nodule and an abscess, since it causes doubt.
I therefore say, that when the operation is warm, and fever, and great pains exist until the inflammation of excessiveness is stilled, and putrefaction is completed; then indeed fever and heat cease.
Since however there is no warmth, nor fever, nor pains, and they are contained by a sac of siplachin, which is their proper sack, and it agrees with the color of the body: and it begins like chickpeas, and becomes like blisters, greater and lesser.
And they are according to two kinds: either fatty: or containing moisture. And the colors of the moisture are many, as I said in the division.
It is therefore necessary, when arriving at the cure of a Nod, that you test him, and inquire into it first with an instrument which is called an Intromissum, according to what its form will be in the chapter that follows this.
And the method of inquiry for all pustules and Nods is, that you take this instrument, and insert it into a more moist place which you find in the pustule: and you turn your fingers with it slowly, until you know that the instrument has already pierced the skin. Then with your last hand according to the measurement of the size of the pustule.
Then withdraw the Intromissum, and examine that which comes out in its track.
If therefore moisture running out comes out, whatever the color is: then cut it off with a superficial incision, according to what I said in the other pustules.
And if no moisture comes out in the track of the Intromissum, then you will know that it is fatty: therefore cut above it with an incision having the form of a cross, according to this form, as I have taught you: and suspend it with hooks, and strip away the skin from all parts with ease: and protect the sac if you can over it, so that you may draw out the healthy sac along with the Nod.
If however the cyst bursts during the operation, and it is not possible to remove the healthy part of it, this happens often enough, then remove it piece by piece until nothing remains.
For if anything remains from it little or much, the Nodus most often returns.
But if it overpowers you and something remains from it little: then fill the wound with your completion, with certain sharp corrosive powders, and bind the wound, and place above it what sits a warm poultice, and cure it with the rest of the treatment, until it heals.
Therefore if the Nodus is great, then close its two lips over it, and cure it with that which becomes fleshed.
But if there is contrary pulsating or non-pulsating vein: then hasten and fill the place with crushed zegi, and leave it tightened for two days, or three, until the wound puttrefies and the boiling of blood subsides: then return to cutting what remains from the Nodus.
Chapter 46.
About the outer instruments for cutting and perforating.
These are the forms of Intromissories, and they are of three kinds.
From them there is a great one, and from them there is a middle one, and from them there is a small one.
They are made of iron with the four-angled ends smoothed out, so that they may hurry into the inflamed part.
And these are the forms of Perforators, and indeed they are, that is, piercing ones, and one of them is called Beris, that is, piercer, and they are of three kinds.
From them there is a great one, and a middle one, and a small one.
They are suitable for investigating through them abscesses, and ulcers, and fistulas, and boils from the bone that is between them.
And others are made round, smooth, soft, like large needles with which they upholster seats, namely from citron wood, or iron, or steel, or silver.
And those that are made of citron are the better ones.
And they are also made by experience from black lead, which is suitable for testing fistulas, the concavity of whose reflections cause them to be reflected with their own softness when reflected against these reflections.
And again there are three kinds. Because they are of a length, of an intermediate length, and of a short length, according to the magnitude of the fistula and its constriction.
The form of ligatures, and there are many kinds.
Because they are simple, that is to say those with one curvature; and there are three kinds as you see, Great, Middle, and Small.
And from them are blind ligatures: and there are three kinds.
And from them are ligatures having two curvatures, and there are three kinds: and from them are ligatures having three curvatures, and there are three kinds: and all these are necessary in their place.
And these are the forms of scalpels with which knots and apostemes are cut out and scraped away: and there are three kinds: because from them is great, middle, and small.
Their extremities with which a section is made are sharp, the other extremities are not sharp; and they should not be placed thus, unless it happens that with them relief is made in the scraping away of knots near fear of incision of a vein or nerve: and so an infirm person may be healed through them, and find tranquility for a little while from burning which he finds at the scraping away of an aposteme.
They are made of iron, and are similar to a Radium with which an injection is made: and at their broad extremity there are hidden points of spatula: and within them it flows inward and outward when you wish, as you see.
The form of bandages which are hidden among the fingers for piercing abscesses: and the sick do not perceive them; and there are three kinds, great, medium, and small.
The forms of astringent plasters with which bleeding is stopped: and there are three kinds, great, medium, and small.
They should be made from air or saffron, round, to a length somewhat, as you see them: and they should be thin.
And it is necessary that these kinds of astringent plasters be present to you, great and small: so that with them you can stop blood flow quickly in necessity, and then when medicine is not available.
However you should not use them in the stopping of blood everywhere in the body, and you should not use them except in fleshy places: such as the calves, thighs, arms, nipples, belly, groin, and similar ones, from fleshy moist limbs.
And sometimes they make from them small instruments similar to the rinds of figs, according to their shape: with which blood is stopped when it flows from a place of phlebotomy, or at an incision of a vein, or artery: and sometimes they are made round in this form.
Chapter 47.
About the care of the nipples of men which resemble those of women.
Sometimes the nipples of some men become inflamed at the completion of sleep (that is, when they start to urinate while sleeping) until they resemble those of women: and remain inflamed with bad swelling.
Therefore he who is afraid of that, should shave over the nipples with a lunar incision, according to this shape from the line, b, to the line, g. Then scrape away all the fatness. Then fill the wound with flesh medicine. Then gather together the two lips of the wound with a suture, and care for it, until it heals.
But if the nipple inclines towards the lower parts and becomes softened because of its size, as happens to women: then it must be cut into two upper sides with two cuts similar in shape to a crescent moon, each one being contained with the other at their ends until there is a longer line containing a shorter one, according to this form from line b. to line g. Then remove the skin that is between the two lips and take away the fat, and administer what we said about suturing and powders, which are necessary for healing, until it heals.
But if you do not complete the incision that is necessary for you to make, because of the disturbance of the patient or because of a flow of blood: then it must be filled with ointment immersed in a caustic, and leave it alone until it corrodes what remains of fat. Then care for it, until it heals.
About the piercing of an abscess, which occurs under the titillate (tittillus).
Chapter 48.
These abscesses which occur under the titillate are of a hard type like sponges that contain pus, and also those that contain moisture.
Therefore those that are of the type which contain moisture must be incised with a crescent-shaped cut, according to this form, from line b. to line g. And when all that is in the abscess has been evacuated by means of pus, then fill it with old dressing and leave it until another day.
Then the dressing is to be removed, and it is to be cared for with a suitable ointment, until it heals.
And if the abscess is of the type of solid nodules like sponges, then cut over it, as we said in cutting over equal sponges.
If however the wound extends so that it emits pus, then apply a caustic to it, as we said in what was said before.
Chapter Forty-nine.
On the cutting of an abscess which occurs on account of an artery and a vein.
When an artery is ruptured and the skin which is above it becomes hardened, then many times an abscess occurs therefrom; and similarly with veins, that an abscess and swelling occur in them.
And the signs by which it is known that an abscess and swelling are on account of an artery and a vein, are: because when an abscess is from an artery, it is long, accumulated in deep within the body; and when you press upon the abscess with your fingers, you feel as if there were a hissing sound.
And that which is from a vein is rounded in the body's surface.
And cutting into this abscess is a mistake, and especially when it is under the armpits, and groins, and neck, and in many places of the body; and it is very great. Therefore care must be taken to avoid cutting into it with iron, and what is from them even in the extremities, or on the head, must also be avoided.
Therefore into that which comes from it by reason of the expansion of the artery's opening, cut into the skin in length. Then open up the incision with a hook, afterwards the artery; and free it from its sheaths until it is revealed.
Then insert an awl under it and make it penetrate as far as the other side; and tie the artery with a double thread in two places, just as I taught you in the extraction of the two arteries which are in the temporal regions.
Then prick the place between the two ligatures with a small spatula, until the blood that is in it all comes out and the abscess is resolved.
Then administer a treatment that produces pus until the bonds fall apart. Afterwards treat it with ointments suitable for that until it heals.
If there is an abscess due to the cutting of a vein, then you must take from the abscess with your hand as much as possible along with the skin. Then insert a needle into the place which you took with your hand and make it penetrate, and let there be in it a thread twice its length, until you draw it out from the other side. Then tie it with the same abscess with a good bandage: as I told you to do with the grape, from two parts with two ends of thread.
But if you fear that the thread will be pulled out, then insert another needle with another thread under the entire abscess near the incision of the needle; press and draw your threads together in four places.
Then cut the abscess in its middle until what is inside it comes out; then cut away the superfluity of skin and let go that which was tied to it.
Then place over it a poultice which you immersed in wine and oil. Then administer the treatment that is made with liniments and ointments until it heals.
Chapter L.
Regarding an abscess that occurs from twisting of nerves.
Just as an abscess occurs in the artery and vein, similarly an abscess occurs in nerves: which comes from a blow or excessive labor and similar things: and it occurs very much in places of joints, or heel, and in every place where joints are moved: and it is a grave abscess.
It resembles the color of its surrounding body, and it is found in many dispositions without pain: and when it becomes inflamed with fever, one feels a weakness similar to lethargy in it.
And it is not a deep abscess, but rather under the skin, and itself moves to every part, and does not extend forward or in front, nor backward to the back.
Therefore what is connected in them should not be invaded with iron. Perhaps indeed a contraction will occur.
But in that which is connected to the head or forehead, find the skin with a spatula. Therefore if it is a small abscess, then hold it fast with forceps and cut it off from its root.
And if it is large, then hang it with a hook and lance it; then remove it, and sew up the wound with a suture; and take care of it until it heals.
Chapter 51.
On the cutting of an abscess that occurs in the abdomen.
There occur in some people, many times in their intestines and in other parts of their bodies, swellings which are called "Fungalia", by resemblance to mushrooms.
Whose root is slender, and the head is large, and the lips of them are turned back. And there is a small one among them, and there is also one that greatly grows.
And I myself have already seen a man, and in his abdomen were two thelils which were more like mushrooms than other things: there was no difference between them, white, with slender roots, and their lips were turned inward, and they were found, and moisture flowed from them always.
I therefore cut them and found that one weighed as much as nine ounces and eight, and the other as much as six; and the operation of cutting them is, that you consider then if the sick person is moist, and the color of the thelil is white, moist, slender root: then cut it with a wide spatula, and be present yourself, with cauterizing instruments in fire.
For often indeed much blood is expelled during the cutting of it; therefore hurry if blood overpowers you, then cauterize it.
If you see a fearful and timid patient afraid of cutting with iron, then take a thread made from decently formed lead, and bind it with him to the atthel which we mentioned earlier, and leave it for two days.
Then add some lead to the stricture. Therefore do not cease from binding the lead as often as it becomes loose, until it is cut off and falls away without effort.
But if the atthel is of thick root, then there is something to fear and dread in its cutting, especially within the belly.
However it is possible for you to cut half of it. Then cauterize it so that it does not recur.
Then care for him until the wound is healed.
And beware lest you presume to cut the atthel that is of foul colour, little sensation, and bad appearance: for it is a cancerous ulcer: and I come with reminder of Cancer after this.
Chapter 52.
¶ On the care for protrusion of the Navel.
There is a protrusion of the navel from many causes. Or from fissures of the sphincter which is over the belly: and an intestine comes out through it, or a part of the intestines, according to what happens in other ruptures. Or from blood that flows from the Vein, or from the Artery, as was previously mentioned. Or from a constricted and swollen vein in it.
If however it is because of a fissure of the sphincter and a protrusion of the intestines, then its colour is similar to the colour of the body: and it is smooth, without pain: and it has a different appearance. And if it is because of the protrusion of an intestine, then its condition with it is of a more vehement and greater difference. When therefore you press it with your fingers, it conceals itself; then it returns and perhaps a sound comes with it: and much glory is made about the entrance of the bath, and great effort.
If it is however because of humidity, it is soft: then it does not conceal itself when you press it with your hand, nor does it constrict or swell.
Which if it is because of blood, then with these signs an abscess appears to blackness from the swelling.
If however it is because of born flesh: then it is a severe abscess, large, hard, and remains according to its size.
If it is however because of inflammation, then the touch of it is soft.
And the operation on it is, that you will then consider, if the swelling of the navel is because of blood from an artery or vein, or inflammation: then it should be forbidden to cure it. For on that there is fear and dread, as I taught you in the chapter where I said abscesses because of an artery and vein.
Which if the swelling of the navel is because of the intestine or bladder: then it should be ordered to the patient, as much as possible, to hold his breath and stand standing upright. Then make signs with encaustum around the entire navel. Afterwards order him to be placed on his back between your hands. Then encircle with a spatula, wide and flat, around the navel area over the place you marked with encaustum. Afterwards stretch out the middle of the abscess upwards, with a large needle. Then bind it tightly around with strong thread, or with a cord of silk and firm sinew: and let the knot of the ligature be loose.
Then open the middle part of the abscess stretched out above the ligature. If therefore you have found it already captured by the ligament: then pull out the instrument and insert your index finger into it, and push the intestine to the inside of the abdomen. And if you find a tumour, then take it with a forceps and cut off its superfluity.
But if the artery or vein is against you: then encircle it much. Then return to your operation. And take two needles, and insert into them two strong threads; and insert the two needles into the ligature which you made around the tumour in the form of a cross, so that they penetrate.
Then bind the tumour in four places over the needles; and if you wish, remove the needles and leave the place, until the stretched flesh rots and falls by itself; or cut it when it becomes thin and rotten.
Then take care of it with what is necessary from ointments, until it heals.
But if the prominence of the navel is from raw flesh within it, or from moisture: then it should be concaved, as we said. And draw out the blood, or moisture, which you find in it.
Then take care of it with what consolidates the place.
Chapter 53.
Note on the treatment of a cancer.
We have already spoken in the division of the kinds of cancer, and how it is the way to its cure with medicines; and beware of it with its cure with iron, lest it ulcerate; and we said that the cancer generated in the womb, and the caution against its cure.
And the ancients said that sometimes cancer is in a place where its eradication is possible; such as cancer which is in the breasts, or in the hip, and those similar to them, located in parts from which it can be wholly extracted: especially when it is beginning and small.
When it is old and large, you must not approach it.
For I was unable to cure anyone from it, nor did I see another before me who had reached out to it.
And the operation for it, when it is located as we said, is, as mentioned before, to precede and dissolve the sickness from black bile sometimes. Then if you find it with a clear filling, bleed it.
Then prepare the sick person for preparation, in which it will be located during the operation.
Then place on it the thorns that fit it. Then hollow it out from every part with the skin, according to examination, until nothing remains of its roots: and let the blood flow, and do not cut it quickly, but rather press on the places and wipe away all the thick blood with your hand, or as best you can there with instruments.
But if a counterflow of thick blood from the artery or vein causes you trouble at the incision: then cauterize the vein until the bleeding stops. Then treat it with the remaining treatment, until it heals.
¶ On the cure of Hydropepsis.
Chapter LIII. Now we have discussed in the division the kinds of Hydropepsis, and how water gathers together, and the signs of every kind, and its cure with medicines.
And that which is cured with iron is not but one kind of ascites alone, and does not approach cure with iron the kinds of tympanitis nor of hypochondriac disease, for it is mortal.
When you are already cured of this kind of Hydropepsis which causes tympanitis with medicines, and your treatment has not helped: then consider. For if the sick person has already been weakened by a weakness, or is suffering from another illness besides Hydropepsis, such as coughing, or the dissolution of the intestines, and similar things to those, then beware of treating him with iron, for it is feared.
If however you see a sick person of robust virtue, and he is not suffering from any disease other than simple dropsy: and he is neither a boy nor an old man: then the manner of operating on him is, that you lift up the sick person standing between your hands, and have another attend after him express his belly with his hand forcing water down to the lower part of his navel.
Then take a thorned spatula, in this shape. There should be two sharp parts, long, with a sharp point like the spatula. However in it should be something of shortness gradually, so that you do not pass through with it up to the intestine during the operation and harm it.
Then examine. For if there is a generation of dropsy from the part of the intestines, then you must extend with a cut from the navel three finger lengths downwards straight over his navel.
If however the generation of dropsy is on account of liver disease, then let your cut be on the left from the navel three fingers.
And if his generation is on account of the spleen, then let the cut be on the right side: and it should not also be that the cut is on the side over which the sick person wants to lie, lest there flow to that weak place.
Then pierce through with an instrument the whole cut. Then insert into that cut and lift up your hand with the spatula between the cuts, and scrape, as if you were removing it: and let be the quantity that is scraped off the quantity of a nail, or nearly so.
Then pierce through with the spatula until it reaches the empty space, and that is the place of water. Then extract it and insert into the opening the instrument which has this shape. And it is similar to a cannula: it is made of silver, or bronze, or tin, smooth and polished: with a small hole at the lower part of itself, and on its sides are three holes, two from one side, and one from the other: and the tip of it is cut off, according to the shape of a reed pen, as you see: and there is an armlet at the upper end of it.
For when the instrument reaches water, then it itself sinks at once upon the instrument, therefore it drains out of the water by a quantity middle.
Because if you drain out from it more than is necessary, perhaps the patient will die in an hour by the resolution of his vital spirits: or a suffocation will come upon him that approaches death.
But drain out from it according to the quantity of its virtue, and according to what the patient's arrangements signify to you through his pulse, and through his condition.
Then draw out the instrument, and retain the water.
Which is because it is retained at once on account of the skin which retains the hole that is over the abscess: of which I spoke, as to perforate it in this manner.
Then introduce the instrument on another day, if you see that the patient is able to tolerate it; and also draw out a small quantity from the water.
Do this for several days without fear, and guard yourself against error, until only a little remains in the water.
But if you fear for the patient, and leave much out of the water: then treat him with burial in warm sand, and great sweat in a bath and sun, and let him wait for thirst: and treat him with dried medicines, until he is healed; and cauterize over his stomach, liver, and spleen, after the extraction of the water: according to what I said previously.
Concerning the care of infants born with unperforated places in their urinary organs.
Chapter 55.
When someone is brought out of the womb of his mother and the infant's head has not perforated, or it has a narrow opening, or in an unnatural place.
Therefore assistance must be provided with the perforation of it at once with a very fine spatula as it is born.

When, however, he wishes to urinate, let it be removed, and let him urinate. Then put it back in.
And if you do not place the metal, it is possible that the urine which flows over the place will prevent it from closing.
But he whose hole is tight is cured with metal, as we said, over many days, until it becomes enlarged.
But those in whom the hole is in a different place are hurt. Which is because the hole is at the end of the head of the penis; so he cannot urinate towards the front until he raises his penis with his hand to the top. Nor is he able to generate because the semen cannot reach the matrix according to its straightness; and it is very great suffering.
And the manner of operating on him is, that he be placed supine over his back. And beware for yourself at your operation lest there be bleeding. For indeed that often happens. Therefore apply to him the thing with which you staunch blood, and cure the wound until it heals.
Concerning Albuter (possibly a reference to a medical condition or instrument) which happens in the foreskin and head of the penis, and corruption, blackening, and sticking.
Chapter 56.
These kinds of boils often occur on the leg, and there is a foul swelling of the flesh.
And from it another is malignant, and another is not malignant.

Which if the boil is malignant and of foul colour, then one must apply a caustic after cutting it open and removing its reason.
But if the boil is on the foreskin of an uncircumcised captive and is a part within, and another part outside, then one must remove the boil that is inside first until it consolidates, then take care of the other one outside: for when you treat them at the same time, the foreskin does not heal but rather becomes inflamed.

Adhesions of the foreskin to the head of the penis actually occur in a man whose foreskin is healthy, and circumcision is not required over him. Chapter 57.
Of the cleansing of infants and the cure of errors that occur to them.
Circumcision is not a solution of continuity, as are other wounds: however since it is from our operation, by our will, and its use is properly in boys, it is necessary that we mark in it the better operation and the easier way leading to salvation.
I say therefore that the ancients did not mention circumcision in any of their books: because they do not use it in their laws, and it is only what we have acquired by experience.
Which is because I have found the majority of surgeons and bloodletters to use a razor and with forceps in circumcision, and they also use stitches and ligatures with thread, and an incision with a nail.
And I myself have already tried all these methods and did not find that the cleansing with forceps and stitching with thread was better.
Since in cleansing with a scraper, the skin often escapes. Since the skin of the foreskin has two coats: therefore perhaps the upper skin is struck, and the lower coat remains; it is therefore forced to an incision in order to hit the other, and future pain. And since cleansing which is done by means of a lancet, that is, with the fingers, as surgeons use them, does not secure an incision of the extremity of the male member: because it perhaps enters into its opening.
But in cleansing with a nail, perhaps the iron will escape and corrupt your operation; and indeed the skin of a boy is short-lived by nature, and many times they are born such that they do not need cleansing: and I have already seen this.
But the cleansing with forceps, and bandaging with a thread, has shown me a good example: because the forceps are of proportional cutting: therefore, what is sharp above, is as sharp as what is below. Therefore when you press your hand, the two sharp edges are proportioned and cut simultaneously according to the operation: and a compression is made similar in shape, containing the skin of the foreskin from all parts, with which there does not entirely fall an error.
And the mode of the proper operation is, that you should cause him to suppose, especially a boy if he is one of those who understand somewhat, that you are not doing anything unless you tie a thread on his penis alone and send him until another day.
Then make him rejoice and be glad in every way as much as you can, because of the recovery of his understanding. Then make him stand between your hands prepared and standing, and not be sitting; and conceal the forceps in your sleeve or under your foot, so that the boy may see them entirely, nor may he see anything from the instruments.
Then insert your hand into his penis and puff air into the skin, and pull it up until the head of the foreskin comes out. Then cleanse it thoroughly from what accumulates within it as dirt.
Then bind the marked place with a doubled thread. Afterward bind below it, for a little while, another thread secondarily. Then grasp with your index finger and thumb the place of the lower ligature holding tight, and cut between the two threads. Then lift up the wound quickly, and pull out the head of the thread. Then let the blood run slowly (for it is better) and put powder on top; and over the powder a cloth in which an egg boiled in rosate water has been crushed with rose oil. A recent good wound: and let him be above it until another day; and take care of him with the rest of the cure, until he heals.
However, the shape of the forceps for cleansing is this.
Let the two ends be blunt and let them meet: so that a branch of a nail cannot issue forth from them; and let the length of the two sharp edges be as the length of two equal hands.
But an error occurs in cleansing because perhaps the inner skin escapes entirely or a part of it at the incision.
Therefore you should stretch it forward from your thumb with your fingernail before the place becomes inflamed; and cut it according to equality.
And if you are not able to hold it with your fingernail, then pull it out with forceps and cut it.
But if it has passed three days and the part that is under the thread remains inflamed with an apostema: then let it be, until the warm apostema settles; and strip off the scab easily, and cut it according to how it prepares itself for you: and beware of the head of the thread. For indeed it will not harm him.
Then therefore take care of it with him that consolidates the wound from powders, which we have narrated in the treatise on powders; and if it cuts out of the skin above measure, and escapes to the upper part, it will not harm him either.
Therefore treat it with that which we said, until it heals.
Chapter 58.
On the extraction of a retained splinter in the scrotum.
Retention of urine in the scrotum occurs from a swelling made by stone, or frozen blood, or pus, or flesh that has formed, and similar things from those which we said on modes of cure in division.
And if the swelling does not subside and you see retention of it to be because of a stone already in the neck of the scrotum: then it is necessary that he lie ill on his knees bent. Then his feet are to be placed over his back and he is to stretch out his belly from the upper part of his back, and the invalid is to take a breath as much as possible for him. Therefore with the scrotum drawn out in front, the stone is forced to come out behind, whereupon the swelling is released.
And if it does not subside with what we said and the matter is severe on the invalid: then it is necessary that you perform its extraction with an instrument which is called a syringe, of which this is the form. It should be made of silver, and should be subtle, soft, concave like the tube of a bird's feather in subtlety of radius, long in similarity to the palm of one hand and middle: and the tube should be thin at its head.
And the method of exit of the swelling through it is, that you take a doubled thread and bind a good knot at its end with wool or cotton: and insert the end of the thread into the lower part of the syringe: and cut with scissors if anything superfluous comes out from the wool, but insert into the tube with vehemence as an alembic.
Then anoint the syringe with oil, or with butter, and with egg yolk: and have the invalid sit on a seat, and apply ointment to the scrotum, and to his rod with moist oils and olive oil, and warm water.
Then insert a syringe into the penis with ease, until it reaches the root of the penis.
Then double it back from the part above the navel.
Then push the syringe inside it until it reaches the bladder and let the sick person feel that it has reached the empty place; and this is not done in this order unless the passage through which it goes is twisted.
Then extend the thread with wool gradually. For the hernia will follow the wool.
Then pull her out and make the hernia run through the way of urine: and put the syringe back and do not stop doing that until the bladder is evacuated and the sick person finds relief.
Concerning the quality of cathartizing the bladder with a siphon.
Chapter 59.
When an ulcer occurs in the bladder or blood freezes in it or pus is compressed within it and urine wishes to be distilled into it with waters and medicines: then make use of the instrument which is called a siphon, and this is its shape.
For it is deaf, that is closed. And in it are three holes, two from one side and one from the other side, as you see.
And the concave place where is that which drives it, should be according to the quantity that suffices for it without addition, until when something is drawn with it from moistures is attracted: and when it is driven, with it is expelled to a distance: according to what the siphon does, in which naphtha, that is Greek fire, is thrown into the lists of Mars.
So when you wish to throw moistures into the bladder, insert the extremity of the siphon into the moisture and draw with it that which drives it upwards; for the moisture is drawn into the concavity of the siphon.
Then insert his extremity into a reed, as we have narrated concerning a syringe. Then urge moisture with him as that which urges it reaches the blister immediately, so that a sick person feels it.
The shape of a delicate suppository by means of which the blister is cleansed.
It shall be made from silver or gold: whose head be higher like the tip of a small alkam (reeds used for medicinal purposes) and under it a concavity in which a cord falls.
Then take the blister of a goat, and put into it the moisture with which you wish to cleanse the blister. Then bind it between two concavities with a strong thread doubled as a ligature, and warm the moisture slightly over the fire.
Then insert the extremity of the suppository into a reed, afterwards tighten your hand over the blister with a binding string suitable to it, until the sick person feels that the moisture has already reached the blister.

Chapter Sixty.
Of the extraction of a Stone.
Now we have spoken in the division of the kinds of the Stone, and its cure with medicines: and I said the difference between the Stone generated in the kidneys, and the blister, and the signs of the whole thing: and I said the Stone on which the operation is done by hand and cutting: and it is the Stone generated properly in the blister: and that which is fixed in a reed: and I am telling you about that one with clear and concise explanation.
I say therefore that a stone generated in the bladder occurs most frequently in boys.
And a sign of it is that the urine comes out from the bladder very fine like water: and there appears to be white sand in it: and the sufferer scratches his private part with it: and labors with it: and often hangs. Then he is raised up, and the anus comes out with him in many of them.
And there is easy healing for boys, as long as they reach fourteen years. And it is difficult in the elderly. But in young people it is of a medium sort, in that which is between the two and its cure is easier if the stone is greater, but on the contrary if small.
When therefore we come to the cure, it is necessary first that we cleanse the patient with a clyster, which takes out all the dung that is in his intestines: for it sometimes prevents the finding of the stone in the examination.
Then let the patient be taken with his feet and shaken and moved towards the lower parts, so that the stone may go down to the depth of the bladder, or sometimes come out from a high place.
Then make him sit between your hands prepared, and his hands be under the base of his own body, so that the bladder may slope completely to the lower parts.
Then examine him and touch him externally.
If therefore you feel the stone in place, then hasten immediately with a knife over it.
But if it does not fall under your touch entirely, then you must wipe the index finger of your left hand with oil from your hand, if the patient is a boy; or the middle finger, if he is a young man who has completed his growth: and insert it into his anus, and seek out the stone until it stands steady under your finger: and turn him slowly around to the neck of the bladder.
Then press upon it with your finger and push it out against the external place of the wound, where you want the section to be. And command one of the attendants to press the swelling (vesica) with his hand, and command another attendant to stretch the testicles upward with his right hand: and with the other hand, remove the skin that is under the testicles in the part opposite to the place of the wound.
Then insert a spatula cut into shape, whose form is this. And find it in that which is between the anus and testicles, not in the middle but to the left side of the navel: and let the section be over the stone, and your finger be in the anus: and let the section be transverse, so that the outer part may be wide and the inner part narrow, according as it may be possible for the stone to come out without being larger.
Perhaps indeed the finger which is in the anus compresses the stone at the section and it comes out without difficulty.
And know that from the stone which has angles and edges, its exit is difficult because of that.
And it is easy to come out, similar to a gland, and round: and its exit is easy.
Therefore to the one who has angles and edges, add something into the fissure.
But if it does not come out that way, then you must think of something else to do over it: or string something over it with a decent gesture, whose end is like the Moon which strings itself over the stone and does not escape from it: or insert a subtle instrument with a curved end underneath it.
But if you cannot do that, then widen the hole a little bit. But if something from blood overcomes you, cut it off with a razor.
If the stone is more than one, then push the larger one into the opening of the swelling first. Then cut over it. Then push a small instrument after it: and similarly do this if there are more in pairs.
But if it is very large, then ignorance lies in cutting upon itself with a great cut. Because an injury happens to the patient either that he may die or a frequent and persistent dripping of urine occurs to him: therefore, because the place is not completely consolidated.
However administer a purging of it, until it goes out: or take hold of its pieces with forceps, until you extract them bit by bit.
Therefore when you have completed your operation, then fill the wound with gall, and aloes, and dragon's blood: and bind it up, and place over it a cloth infused with oil and wine, or rose oil, and cold water: so that the hot swelling may be alleviated.
Then place the patient on his back over a pillow or his own neck, and do not loosen the bandage until the third day. When therefore you have loosened it, anoint the place with much water and oil.
Then care for him with Diaphnetikon ointment and Basilicon ointment, until he heals.
But if an added and festering swelling, and ulceration, and similar things occur in the wound, such as if blood congeals in a vein, and urine is prevented from flowing from it: and you recognize this by the discharge of blood with urine. Therefore insert your finger into the wound and extract that blood. For if it remains, it provokes the vein to corruption, and its putrefaction.
Then cleanse the wound with vinegar and water, and salt: and apply to it of every kind what is suitable for its cure, until it heals.
And it behooves you during the entire period of cure to bind together the two thighs and join them, because of the medicines which are placed over the place.
If a stone is small, and it is situated in the urethra, and it is stuck there, and its exit is prevented by urine: then cure it with what I narrate before you come to the incision. For often are you excused from an incision through this treatment, for indeed I have already tried it.
And take a perforator of iron soldering rod, whose shape is triangular at the end, sharp, fixed in wood.
Then take a thread and bind with it the twig under the stone so that it does not return to the bladder.
Then insert the piercing iron into the twig with ease, until it reaches the stone: and turn the perforator with your hand in the stone little by little: and you will make a perforation in it, until you make it penetrate from the other side.
For urine is immediately released.
Then press your hand over what remains of the stone from the outside part of the urethra. For it breaks and is expelled with the urine, and the sick one is healed.
But if this cure is not prepared for you because something prevents it from being done: then bind a thread under the stone and another thread above the stone. Then cut above the stone on the urethra between the two bindings. Then extract it. Later untie the bandage and clean out the frozen blood that forms in the wound.
And it is not necessary to bind a thread under the stone except so that it does not return to the bladder: and another bandage above except so that when you untie the thread after the stone's exit, the skin returns to its place and covers the wound: and because of this you must do this when binding the thread above, that you lift up the skin upwards, that it may return into your body and cover the wound, as we said.
Chapter 61.
On the Extraction of the Stone from Women.
The stone is formed to a small size in women.
But if it happens to any of them that she has a stone, then the cure is difficult and is prohibited because of many rules.
One of these is: that perhaps the woman is a virgin.
And the second one is, because you do not find a woman who will uncover herself to a physician when she is chaste, or from women with husbands.
The third one is, because you do not find a woman who is well-versed in this art, especially the operation with the hand.
And the fourth one is, the distance from the place of the stone; wherefore it requires deep incision, and there is fear.
For if necessity provokes to that, then it is necessary that you take a woman physician, well-versed; and there are few found.
If however you privatize them [the women patients], then seek a chaste physician, subtle, and present a well-learned woman obstetrician knowledgeable in the affairs of women, or a woman who has some part in this art. Then make her present, and above all command her to do everything that you prescribe to her on investigation concerning the stone.
First of all that which is, to see if the woman is a virgin; then it is necessary that she insert her finger into her anus and seek out the stone.
If therefore she finds it [the stone], let her cover it under her finger, and then command the obstetrician to insert her finger into the vulva of the infirm woman and inquire about the stone, after placing her hand left over the vesica and compressing it with good pressure.
If therefore she finds the stone, then it is necessary that she gradually move it from the opening of the vagina to the lower parts with all her strength, until she comes to the root of the hip.
Then he cuts on him above the opposition of the middle of the jaw at the root of the hip, from whatever part it is prepared for him, and he feels a stone in that part; and his finger does not move away from the stone, constricting under it: and let the cut be small at first. Then he introduces a reed over that incision.
Then he introduces a reed over that incision. If therefore he feels the stone, then let him add to the incision as much as is proportional to what stone issues from it.
And know that there are many kinds of stones. From these is the small and the large, soft and rough, long and round with branches.
Know therefore its kinds, so that it may be signified to you through this over what you wish.
If therefore blood holds sway over you, then add to the place crushed myrrh and hold it for one hour until the blood is cut off.
Then return to your operation until the stone goes out; and do as you have prepared with instruments which I said for the extraction of a stone in the bladder, so that they may aid you through them in your operation.
But if bleeding holds sway over you, and you know that the expulsion of blood is from the artery which was cut: then put dust over the place, and bind him with a decent compressing bandage: and do not change it; and let the stone go, and do not extract it. Perhaps indeed the weak woman will die. Then care for the wound.
When therefore the sharpness of the blood is stilled after days and the place becomes putrid, then return to your operation until the stone comes out.
Chapter LXII.
A section above the Hydrorach (Hydroureter)
The hydrorach is not a stone, unless it is an aggregation of moisture in the scrotal sac white part, which is under the skin containing the seed; and it is called the scrotal sac.
And at times it is found in its own sheath, which nature prepared for itself on the scrotum, until it becomes putrid; which is another kind of sac and skin of the testicle between its two layers of fat. And that does not happen often.
And the generation indeed of this cord is from the weakness that happens to the testicles: wherefore this matter flows towards them. And at times it occurs due to a blow against the testicles.
And this moisture is having many colors. For indeed its colour is inclined to yellowishness, or it is reddish bloody, or it is fecal and black, or it is watery white, and that is most often.
And the signs by which one knows where the aggregation of water is, are these. For if it is in the white layer that we mentioned, then the abscess is round in length for a little while like the figure of an egg: and the testicle does not appear, since the moisture contains it from all parts.
And if there is moisture in its own sheath, then the abscess is round with an egg-like part, and for this reason man supposes that it is another kind of egg.
And if the moisture is between the skin of the testicles and the white layer, then it will fall under sense.
When therefore you wish to have knowledge of the colour of the moisture, then try out the colour of the moisture in the abscess with a square instrument which has preceded in shape. According to what comes out therefore in the track of the inserted thing, judge about that which is between it.
When therefore we come to the treatment with iron, then it is necessary that you order the patient to be bled if it is possible for him, and you see his body full.
Then he will be placed on his back over a high thing somewhat, and place many cloths under him.
Then sit yourself over the left side of him, and command your attendant to sit on his right side, who should extend his rod across one or two sides of the scrotum, and into the region of the navel.
Then take a broad spatula and cut the skin of the testicles in half along its length as far as possible towards the navel: and let the incision be made according to straightness on an equal line which divides the skin of the testicles until it reaches the white ligament: and clean him out and observe, or beware lest you cut into himself: and let your cleaning be that by which you cleanse him from the part that hangs down like an egg more: and carefully search with cleansing according to as much as is possible for you.
Then pierce the white ligament full of water with a wide piercing, and draw out all the water.
Then separate the two lips of the section with a hook, and stretch out the white ligament upwards, and do not touch the skin of the testicles that surrounds it: and cut the white ligament in such a way as is possible for you to cut it, either entirely or piecemeal, especially its thin side. For if you do not carefully search into it, there will be no security in the cut of it, lest the water return.
But if the egg proceeds externally from its wound during your operation hour: then when you have completed the section of the ligament, return it. Then gather together the two lips of the skin of the testicles with a suture.
Then take care of it as you do other wounds, until it heals.
But if you find the egg corrupted by blackness, then it is necessary to tie up the vessels, which are the suspenders, fearing a flow of blood.
Then cut off the testicle with its suspender, and draw out the egg; afterwards take care of it as we said.
If indeed water is accumulated in two parts, then know that there are two branches: therefore cut the other part equally as you did to the one.
And if it is equal to you, so that it should be one operation, then do this: then wipe away the blood. And insert a thread soaked in oil or rose oil into the sections, and put another thread which you dipped in wine and oil outside it, and spread it over the two testicles and the entrails, and put folded cloths above them, and they are small sacs: and bind them above with a suture that has six threads according to this form.
Then two cloths are taken and are sewn together, and they are filled with the crimsoned linen according to this likeness: and they make the extremities by which it is pressed from the belly or moist wool: and they gather them along with the suture in the loin of the arms: and one extremity takes hold from the anus up to the back, and the second takes hold at the part of the breast: and two other extremities above the lower thighs, and two other extremities above the inguinals, and the whole suture is gathered together in the loin of the arms.
And sometimes artificially this branch is treated with a cautery in the place of the section by iron: and it is as if you take a sharp-edged knife-like cautery, and cut with it the skin of the testicles: and it is ignited, according to what we have narrated: until when a white pus containing water is shown: then take the cautery, according to this form.
It is assimilated to Greek letters.
Then pierce with it the pus and it is ignited, until all moisture comes out.
Then extend the pus with a hook and peel it off with a sharp-edged ignited cautery, and cut into itself as much as is possible for you, until all its roots are eradicated.
And beware that it not touch fire with itself.
And know that this operation is more safe and farther from the flowing of blood, and better than the operation with incision.
But if a contrariety happens to you in the hour of incision, or in the hour of cauterizing an aposteme warm, or flux of blood, or something else: then it behoves that you cure what contrariety is happening to you from it with a treatment that suits it, according as has been aforementioned.
But if the ailing person is timid and does not await this operation because of his frightfulness: then administer a perforation according to what I tell you, and it is.
When you have been assured with yourself by what we said about the signs, that the ramex is watery: then it behoves that you make the ailing person sit on an elevated chair.
Then pierce the aposteme in its lower part with a broad spatulum and place the perforation according to the length of the body, and let the opening be wide until all the water runs out.
Then place over the cleansed area a bandage and bind it, and let it be until the remaining water runs out: then take care of it, until the wound is healed.
But if the contrariety is of the flowing out of water at the hour when you pierce it, and it is not for this reason alone, because a white membrane makes contrariety in the wound orifice and prevents the exit of water: then it behoves that you insert a feather into the wound, or an instrument with which water from abscesses is drawn out, whose shape has been mentioned before: or add a little bit to the operation of the wound.
And know that the water returns and gathers after six months, or thereabouts. When therefore it is gathered, then it should be perforated in the same manner, and the ailing person will pass through his days.
Chapter Sixty-Three.
Of the care for wounds of the flesh.
You should know that in the incision above this wound, there is from fear leading to harm in many dispositions. Therefore it seems to me that its dismissal would save from it.
And I am recalling the treatment of it, and the appearance of the aposteme.
Therefore I say, that many apostemes occur in the bodies, from which carbuncles are formed: and this is done for many causes, namely.
Or from an excess of sharp or acute fluid that flows to the testicles. Or from a blow.
And the color of the aposteme is according to the color of the body, and no pain is found in it, and it is a grave aposteme.
And perhaps it is a stony aposteme, whose color is darkish, and there is no sense in it.
And sometimes it occurs from the clotting of arteries and their inflation: as I have already recalled its mention; or from the inflation of the veins.
Which therefore is from them from the inflation of the arteries, is recognized by the arrangement of the aposteme, when you compress it with your fingers. And it should not be touched at all.
But that which occurs from the inflation of the veins, does not dissipate anything under a pressing with fingers.
