And now it is possible that an incision be made over this kind and over the remaining flesh, and that one cuts the skin of the testicles. Then stretch the scrotum upwards and draw out the testicle with a white cord, and free the suspensory ligament from the veins, and bind the veins; and make an incision in the suspensory ligament after you have freed it from all parts of the scrotum.
So if the scrotum is consolidated with those born flesh tissues, then you should grasp the testicle and make an incision in it.
And if the adhesion is between something from a spleen or in what is between the vesicles: then it is necessary that you free the entire adhesion and cut it with a round incision.
But if it is the birth of flesh at the place of adhesion which is behind: then it is necessary that you cut all of it and take out the placenta, as we said.
Therefore when your operation is completed, then fill the wound with wool steeped in rose oil and wine: thereafter care for it with the remaining treatment, until it heals.
Chapter 64.
On the cure of Wounds which are with Vinegar.
Vine is a swelling wrapped around with some kind of wrapping, similar to an abscess, with the softening of the testicles: and it makes movement and exercise difficult over a weak person, and walking.
And in the cure of this illness is from fear, because of the thing that came before its remembering.
But it is necessary to say that the operation for it is done according to what the first ones did.
I therefore say, that you should have the sick person sit on a high elevated seat. Then push the supporter of the testicles downwards. Then take the skin of the testicles with your fingers, along with the vessels that are near the root: and have another servant hold it, and stretch it with a strong stretching.
Then cut with a sharp bistoury having a broad blade, a transverse incision directly on the vessels, until the vessels are revealed.
Then scrape away from all parts, as we said to you in the removal of arteries which are in the scalp.
Then insert a needle into them with a thread which is double the size of the filament, and tie it first at the places where the Vine occurs: and then tie it again at their last ones. Then cut them in the middle straight according to the length of the body, and draw out what is accumulated from corrupted putrid humors.
Then care for the wound with the treatment of other wounds, from which a discharge of pus is to be generated, so that the vessels nourishing one of the testicles may fall. For it is not bad through it.
But if the vein is cut with all the vessels, then it is necessary that the testicles themselves are drawn out because of this, along with their vessels: so that the sperm may not be deprived of nourishment on account of the incision, and wither away or perish, and there would be no benefit with them.
Chapter 65.
About the care for ruptured intestines.
The outcome of this rupture is from an incision which occurs in the navel extended over the belly in the part of the groin from the inside of the abdomen.
Why do the intestines descend from that rupture to one of the testicles. And this rupture is either from a hernia, or its extension.
And these two kinds occur from many causes. Or from a blow. Or a jump. Or shouting. Or the lifting of something heavy, and similar things.
And a sign is shown when it is from an extension of the hernia. It is that which happens gradually over a long period of time and does not happen suddenly: and it is an ecchymosis equal in size against the depth: therefore, because the hernia compresses the intestine.
And a sign of it when it is from the cutting of the hernia, because it makes a severe pain suddenly occur from its beginning: and there is an ecchymosis different, appearing near the skin: and that is because of the exit of the intestine, and its course to the exterior of the hernia.
And sometimes it emerges with the intestine a tumor-like growth: and this is called the intestinal reed, and the zirbia: and sometimes it occurs with inflammation.
And sometimes it runs into the intestine feces, and is retained there: and with it is the harm of the sick man: because it causes to occur evil pain, and especially when it is pressed.
And in the cure of this disease species with iron there is fear: wherefore it behooves that a case be guarded against in it.
And the method of the operation is, that you command the sick man to reduce his intestine by hand to the inner parts of his own concavity, if he obeys the return. Then it is raised up over his neck between your hands: and lift up his legs.
Then extend the skin which follows the skin upwards, and cut the skin of the testicles entirely along its length. Then fix in the two lips of the incision needles, according as you are able to open the incision with them: and let the incision be of such size, that it may be possible for an egg to come out of it.
Then peel the siphac, and what is under the skin of the testicles, until when it is uncovered the white, hard siphac from every part: then insert your index finger into that which follows the testicle, in that which is between the white siphac, which is under the shell of the egg, and the second siphac, and separate entirely with it the adhesion that is in the posterior part of the egg.
Then move it with the right hand to the inner skin of the testicles: and when this extends the white siphac upwards with the left hand: and lift up the egg with the siphac to the part of the incision: and command the servant, that he extend the egg upwards: and you loosen the adhesion, which is behind the completed operation.
And inquire with your fingers, that there be nothing at all there of the intestine wrapped up in the hard white siphac. If however something is found inside him from it: then push it downwards into his belly.
Then take the needle in which is a thick thread, made from ten threads: and insert it near the back part of the sifach, that is, the pouch, under the skin of the testicles which follows the incision: then cut off the ends which project from the thread, until there are four threads. Then tie certain ones on top of others in a shape like that which is in the form of a cross: and ligate with them the sifach, of which we spoke, because it is under the skin of the testicles with strong ligaments, until no vessel that nourishes them can send anything to them: so that an hot apostema may not occur. And put another ligament outside the first ligature, far from it by less than two fingers. And after these two ligatures release from the sifach, which is under the skin of the testicles, a quantity equal to the size of a finger: and cut off the rest entirely according to its roundness, and take out the egg.
Then cut in the lower part of the skin of the testicles a incision, from which blood and pus flow: as we narrated above.
Then apply wool soaked in oil, and put it into the wound: and apply the ligature which we have mentioned. And sometimes a cauterization is made on the white sifach that we mentioned, after its incision, fearing a flow of blood. For it often happens. And let the ligature go, until it falls on its own.
If however its fall is delayed, then it will be fomented with warm water: and put on top of it what we mentioned, until it falls.
Then care for the wound with the remaining cure of wounds, until it is cured.
Chapter Sixty-six.
About a bruised vein.
In this case, I have not seen anyone dare to attempt a cure for it with iron.
And the first thing they said is that it is treated just as we have described in the case of a vein that has burst from being cut. Which is to tie the vessels below after cutting over them with ease; then cut in the middle and cure it finally with what opens it, until the vessels fall; and cure the wound, as we said, until it heals.
Chapter Sixty-seven.
About a rupture that occurs in the groin.
When a rupture occurs in the groin, as we said, and the swelling appears, and does not descend to the testicles anything from the intestines: and if it does descend, it is little and returns in all hours: but if prolonged with that time, a fissure forms in the scrotum, until the intestines descend into the scrotum.
And it occurs from the swelling of the scrotum that is in the groin, as we said. Which is because the scrotum swells; then it becomes soft and suppurates, and the place swells.
And its cure is with a cautery, just as we have previously described.
And sometimes it is cured with iron, according to this method: and thus, the patient lies on his back between your hands. Then cut the place of the rupture's swelling and supuration with a cut across its width, according to the size of three fingers.
Then tie up the scrotum and what is under the skin until when it is uncovered white tissue that follows it.
Then take an iron rod and place it over the swelling in the scrotum, and press it to a depth into the abdomen.
Then when the two swellings are above the end of the iron rod in the scrotum, and attach one to the other with a stitch; then pull out the end of the iron rod.
And you must not puncture a fistula altogether, nor draw out an abscess, nor anything else, as I have instructed you to know in the cure of gut wounds.
Then its cure is by the treatment for wounds. Therefore when you cut the filaments, clean that, and restore the wound until it becomes stable.
For a suture contracts, and does not increase the prominence of the rupture.
And cauterizing is better in this place, because it is closer to health.
Chapter 68.
About softening the skin of the testicles.
The skin of the testicles often becomes softened in certain men, because of which the flesh between them is also softened and their appearance is fouled.
Therefore it is necessary for the person who is asked to treat this that he make the sick man lie on his back and cut off all the softened skin that has formed over the natural skin.
Then bring together the two lips with a suture, and if you want to, first tie three or four stitches of the softened skin that has become superfluous, and then secure a firm suture.
Then cut between the stitches.
Then treat it with the treatment for other wounds until it heals and the sutures fall out.
Chapter 69.
About Castration.
Know that castration is forbidden by our law and I am not permitted to remind of it in my book, unless for two reasons.
One reason is so that the physician may know about it when he is asked about it and be able to know its cure for whoever suffers from it.
And the other reason is that we frequently need to castrate certain animals for our farm animals, such as rams, he-goats, and male cats, and similar ones.
I say therefore that castration is done in two ways. Or by abrasion. Or by incision and cutting.
However, the way of performing that which is done by abrasion is this: we make the animal sit in hot water until its testicles become soft and relaxed, and then with your hand you scrape them away until they are dissolved and do not feel under touch.
But in the castration that is done by incision and cutting, it is necessary to hold the animal and press the skin of its testicles with your left hand.
Then bind the suspensory ligament and cut above the whole mass with a single cut until when they proceed two masses: then incise them after you have skinned both of them, and do not leave on top of them anything except the thin siphac which is over the vessels.
And this method of castration is better than that which is done by abrasion because abrasion perhaps leaves something from the testicles so that the animal desires to mate.
Then take care of the wound until it heals.
Chapter 70.
Of the cure of a Hermaphrodite in two ways.
A Hermaphrodite is made from males in two ways. One of them is because it appears in that which follows the space, or on the skin of the testicles, in that which is between the two testicles, a figure like a woman's wound, where there are hairs: and sometimes urine flows from what is on the skin of the testicles.
But in women there is one kind and it is a woman's wound on the pectoral region just like the small testicles of a man are entirely prominent on the outside: one of which is like the penis of a man and two like two testicles.
And the cure of these three kinds, two from males and one from a female, is that it is necessary to cut the added flesh until their impression flees. Then the cure of these wounds is treated with the same cure as other wounds.
However, in a specific wound of the testicles from which urine issues and is in the skin of the testicles, there is no operation, nor its cure at all.
Chapter Seventy-One.
On the incision of the labia and the fleshy prominence in women's wounds.
A growth perhaps is added to the natural part until it becomes foul and ugly in appearance. And sometimes it grows so large in some women that it expands as much as in men, and reaches as far as the genitals.
It is necessary therefore that you grasp the superfluity of the labia with your hand or with a pair of tweezers and cut it, and do not finish cutting in the depth of the root so as not to cause a flow of blood.
Then its cure is treated with the same cure as for wounds, until it heals.
But the flesh that grows is the flesh that grows in the opening of the womb, until it fills it up. And sometimes it issues forth to the exterior according to the resemblance of a tail: and on account of this some people first name the disease a caudated affliction. It is necessary therefore that we cut it out as we cut out the labia and treat it until it heals.
On the cure of a rupture, which is an unbroken wound in a woman.
Chapter Seventy-Two.
A rupture is that which is a wound in a woman not broken through or pierced, or be it a small hole. And it is either natural or accidental.
The accidental one is from an illness that preceded it, and it itself comes about either from flesh that has grown and been added, or from a thin membrane, or from a thick one. And it occurs either in the depth of the womb, or on its sides, or in its upper part, or lower part. And it prevents coitus and impregnation, and childbirth, and possibly menstruation.
And knowledge of this comes through the observation of the midwife, if a hernia is apparent nearby.
But if it is not apparent, then seek it with your fingers, or with a stick.
But if the hernia is from a thin and subtle membrane, and is near to both lips: then hasten and tear it.
And it is, as you place on two lips, poulilles.
Then place your fingers of both hands together, and the woman be on her back with her legs spread. Then stretch out the two lips with force, until the thin membrane is torn and the hernia is opened.
Then take a cloth, and soak it in oil, and place it on the spot: and have the woman heat up every day, so that the place does not grow stiff instead.
But if the membrane is thick and dense: then it is necessary to cut it with a spatula similar to a myrtle leaf.
But if the hernia is from a tumor of flesh, then suspend it with hooks and cut it: and be with you medicines that stop the flow of blood without bleeding, as aloe, and dragon's blood, and husk gathered with the membranes of an egg.
Then administer a cannula made of lead, that the wound does not quickly become stiff, keep it for several days: and administer linen applied dry, then take care of the rest of the treatment, until it heals.
And sometimes other flesh-born things are born in the womb: therefore it is necessary that they be cut according to this method: since there is no cancerous ulcer that is not contrary to iron entirely.
Chapter 73.
¶ On the care of hemorrhoids, Athehelil, and red boils on the wounds of women.
Hemorrhoids are the dilation of the openings of the veins, until much blood flows out from them constantly.
When therefore hemorrhoids become old, they are called thetheli. And I have already spoken of their kinds and signs in the division of species; and I will now say what from them receives cure and what does not.
I therefore say that hemorrhoids which are in the vulvar opening and do not appear to sense: then there is no cure for it with iron.
However those from them that are in the vulvar opening above which sense falls, are those which receive cure.
It is necessary therefore that you introduce the woman into a cold house. Then stretch out the thetheli with forceps or with a rough cloth and cut them off from their roots.
Then powder over them at the flow of blood from one powdered without bitterness as acacia and dragon's blood and thyme and similar things.
Then push her feet against the wall for an hour. Afterwards put her into cold water.
But if the flow of blood persists: then make her sit in a decoction of sumac and pomegranate rind and gall and similar things.
Then take barley flour and mix it with honey and vinegar and make a plaster with it on her back.
If therefore the blood is dried up with that it is good and if not make the sores adhere to her back without scarification.
Then take wool and dip it into the juice of taracti or the juice of agrimony or the juice of Alhala trees that is agaric Pontic wine and make it adhere to the place.
When therefore the swelling subsides, then treat the place with ointments until it heals.
Albath is a red plant similar to the heads of Alhassa and has a rough appearance.
It is necessary therefore that you cut what appears from it, according to what I said about the thetheli equally; and treat it, until it heals.
Chapter 73.
On the perforation of the uterus which occurs in the womb.
There occur in the womb many kinds of ulcers, such as cancer, and stony ulcers, and sores, and inflamed, and itching, that is herpes, carbuncles, fistulas, hemorrhoids, ragadiae, and theril, and hot ulcers.
And I myself have already said all these afflictions and kinds of them, and their signs, and their cures in the division: it is necessary for me to say in this treatise about hot ulcers which occur in the womb, when they are from ulcers that collect pus, and you wish to know how its opening is done with iron.
It is therefore necessary for you to consider. Then if the pain of the ulcer in its beginning is sharp with its percussion and inflammation and fever: and you see it to be red: if it presses heavily on itself, then do not hasten with its perforation: and cure it with that which helps for its maturation, until when the sharpness of the accidents ceases.
Then it is necessary for you to make the woman sit on a stool which has two armrests, and recline, or be laid over her back, and gather her feet towards the lower part of the abdomen: and let her hips be spread apart, and she place her arms under her knees, and bind each with a suitable ligament to it.
Then the midwife should sit on the right side and administer the instrument by which the opening of the womb is made.
And it is necessary when you wish to administer this instrument that you insert it with a finger into the depth of the womb of the woman, so that what enters from the instrument is not more than the depth of the womb and does not hurt her.
If however the instrument shall be deeper than profound, then it behoves thee to lay poultices on the surface of the profound matrix: so that the entry of the entire instrument into the profound matrix may be prevented. And it behoves thee to place the whorl, which runs in the instrument, from the upper side over: and to hold fast the instrument, and to turn again the whorl until the profound matrix is opened.
Since therefore exitura appears and is touched with the hand and is soft and smooth: then it behoves thee to cut off itself near its head with a flat spatula.
When therefore the purulence is evacuated, then it behoves thee to place a linen cloth steeped in rose oil: or green oil, in which there may be some heat: and place the cloth, and draw out the section into the profound matrix.
And place on the outside over the matrix a clean wool compress soaked in water in which mulberries have been boiled.
Then it shall be cared for after two days with an ointment that is suitable to it, until it heals.
And when the matrix and wound are to be washed, so that they are cleansed with water and honey: then with water in which the root of lily or long aristolochia has been boiled: then it shall return to the ointment.
But if the wound is hidden within the matrix, then it behoves thee to refrain from treating it with iron, nay rather treat it with that which we said in division.
Chapter 70.
A Treatise on the Midwives, how they heal living embryos, according to unnatural figure having emerged.
It is necessary for the Midwife, first to know the natural figure of birth.
From the signs therefore it is, that when you see a woman compressing herself downwards, and desiring to breathe the air, and she finds relief above her on what is of the birth, and hastens the exit of the embryo: then know that this birth is according to the natural figure, and its exit is over her head, with the cord and it along with it: or suspended, or hanging from the navel.
When therefore you see these signs, then it is necessary that his belly be compressed, so that the embryo may quickly descend.
For when it has descended on its own head, the second skin descends with it, and is cleansed of those excess things by good cleansing.
But that which is outside this birth is unnatural, inadmissible.
Because sometimes the fetus comes out over its own feet, and comes out of its body before its head, and its feet and hands together, or one foot: or comes out its head and hands: or comes out the entrails: and perhaps turns back over its neck, and such things from detestable forms.
It is necessary therefore that the midwife be prudent and subtle in all these things, and beware for herself from error and deception: and I am showing every form among them, and how the mind is in it, and let it be signified by it, and let her stand over it.
When the fetus comes out over its own head in a natural birth, and it becomes severe over the woman, and the labor becomes difficult for her, and you see that her strength is already weakened: then make her sit on a chair, or have women hold her: and vapour her vulva with fenugreek cooked, and with moist oils.
Then the midwife should insert between her fingers a small spatula and find with it the membrane, and wipe it with her finger, until what is in it from moisture runs out: and press on the woman's belly, until the fetus descends.
But if it does not descend, then it is necessary that the woman be purged with a mucilage of fenugreek, with sesame oil.
Then order her after the purging that she be compressed, and have her sneeze with a condiment, and hold her mouth and nose at the same time. For the foetus will issue forth immediately.
But if two hands of the foetus are first to be issued, it is necessary that you reduce them with some facility little by little. But if it does not obey on entering, then place the woman over a seat, and lift her feet upwards; then strike the seat against the ground: and the woman must be held so as not to fall by the shock.
Therefore if the hands do not issue and the foetus dies: then cut them and take out the remainder of the foetus: and bind his hand with cloth, and take him out with care: for he will issue.
The exit of the embryo over its feet. When the foetus issues over its own feet, then it is necessary to push them upwards; afterwards turn the foetus little by little; then straighten him.
Then take one of his feet and stretch it out little by little.
When therefore they issue up to the hips, then command her that she be compressed, and make her spit with a condiment: for it will come out, if God wishes.
And if it does not issue with what we have narrated, then reduce the foetus little by little, until you place him according to his natural shape. For he will come out then with easy delivery.
But if it is prevented with all that we have narrated: then take of the paste of aloes, and oil of fennel, and gum dissolved; and strike the whole in a mortar with good pounding; afterwards smear him with the cloth of woman's womb, and lower part of her belly. Then make her sit in warm water, until there is a looseness.
When therefore you see that already the lower parts of her are warmed up, then make her take suppositories of myrrh; and insert them. When therefore she holds the suppository for one hour, then make her sit on a seat. Then make her sneeze: and bind tight her mouth and nose: and press the lower part of her stomach with easy compression. For the fire will issue forth at once.
The exit of the fetus over the knees and hands. When it issues forth in this form, then it will make its way by slowly inserting its hands. Then the woman is placed on her back over a pillow, and her feet are hanging down below: and she herself lies on a bed, and folds her hands. Then press upon her stomach slowly. Then draw out what is under her feet from the bedding, until it be prepared below. And if the fetus does not issue forth, then take her feet and move them with a violent motion. Then press that which is above the navel slowly, until the fetus ascends to the upper part. Then she should insert her hands and equalize the fetus slowly: and instruct the woman to be compressed until the fetus issues forth.
The exit of the fetus hanging out sideways from the hands. It will make its way down by the lowering of her hand. But if she is unable to reduce it, then lift up the woman and make her walk.
And if she is unable to walk, then make her lie on a bed and strike her thighs with violent shaking.
Then administer the liniment which you prepared from the poultices.
And if she takes it a birth, then make her sit on a chair. Then strive to reduce the hand and equalize the fetus according to its natural shape: and make the woman sneeze, and instruct her to compress herself until it comes out.
The exit of the fetus over its mother's neck, with hands extended, and its face towards the back of its mother. The midwife should grasp the hand of the fetus, then turn it gradually, and equalize its position.
When therefore it is equalized, then place over the mouth of him what we have mentioned from oil and ointments.
Then command him to be moved gradually. When therefore you have done that, then make her sit on a chair, and command her to lean to the right side. Then command her to be compressed; and make her sneeze when you have given her [the draught].
The exit of the fetus prepared on the side. When it has descended to the maternal opening according to this method with a cord, then cut the cord with your finger, until it is evacuated of moisture.
If therefore it is suspended over the face and neck of the fetus, then cut her from the navel: so that she may not be retained, and perish.
Then push the fetus inward, until you have placed it as it should be; and command the woman to compress herself several times: for he himself will come out with a slight exit.
The exit of twins, or many fetuses. Know that twins are often born, and sometimes three, and four. However it is an outsider, and rarely happens.
However five is a thing outside of nature, and they do not live at all: and they are contained, although they multiply, by one cord: and they are separated by the siphac, distinguishing between them the ligament in the navel of each of them.
And sometimes they come out like a single fetus comes out naturally and easily: and they come out with an unlaudable exit, as we have narrated.
And if some of them are difficult at childbirth and arrive at that which we said from unnatural figures, then care for them as we have narrated: and let the midwife be subtle, and do what she does with ease and slowness, and ingenue it according to every figure with him who prepares something for him from ingenious leading to salvation.
A recollection of what is formed in the womb from embryos, and they miscarry.
At times a single one is formed in the womb, and two, and three, and four, and five, and six, and seven, and more than ten: and it has been verified to me that a woman miscarried seven, and fifteen others, all formed.
That which has happened by the measurement of the glorious wise one.
Therefore, all this ought to be in the knowledge of the obstetrician. Perhaps it will arrive at her similarly afflicted: so let him oppose it with that which is necessary.
Chapter 76.
About the extraction of a dead fetus.
When you are treating a dead fetus with what I said in the division of medicine, and it does not come out, and you are forced to use an operation with your hand, then it is necessary for you to consider.
So if the woman is of sound virtue and is not with some accident from which loss would be feared for her: then it is necessary for her to be laid out on a bed, on her back, and have her head inclined downward, and her legs raised; and a servant should hold her from two sides, or she should be bound to the bed, so that her body may not move during the extraction of the fetus.
Then moisten the mouth of the womb with oily liquids mixed with aloes and fenugreek and flaxseed.
Then the obstetrician should anoint her hand with these oils and mucilages, then insert it into the womb with ease. Then she should ask the woman where to place the hooks from the fetus and what places are fitting for that.
So consider then if the fetus is prepared to descend upon its head: then insert the hooks into its eyes, or on its neck, or on its mouth, or on its palate, or under its chin, or at the joints of its limbs, or in nearby places near the ribs, and under the hollows.
If however the descent is upon his feet, then on the breastbone, and middle ribs of him.
And it is necessary for him to hold the hook with his right hand; and to place the curvature of it on that which is between the fingers of his left hand: and he shall insert with his hand with ease, and fasten the hook into one of those places which we said, until he reaches the empty space.
Then he shall fasten another hook into opposition to it, or a third one, if needed: so that there is an equal contraction, and it does not decline to the side.
Then it shall be extended with an equal extension, and not merely according to a straight line, but rather it shall move to all parts, so that its exit is eased: just as happens when a tooth is being extracted.
And it is necessary in that which is between them, so that the extension may be softened. But if a part is retained from it, then the obstetrician should apply some fingers with oil, and insert them on the side, so that he may turn out with them what is being retained.
So if a part of the fetus comes out, then the hooks should be changed to another place that is higher: do this until the entire fetus comes out.
So if a hand comes out ahead of the rest, and it is not possible to return it because of its constriction: then it is necessary that a cloth be wrapped over it. Then draw it out until the whole thing comes out; cut it at the junction of the wrist or elbow: and do this with the other hand and two feet.
But if the head of the fetus is large, or there is constriction on its exit, or there is water gathered in it: then it is necessary that a spiny spatula be inserted between his fingers and he shall cut with it the head, and draw out the water; or he shall break it up with an instrument called Almisdac: whose form comes with shapes of other instruments in the chapter that follows this.
Similarly he should act if the fetus is of a large head nature, then it is necessary for him to cut quickly: or pull it out with a misdach, as we said. Then you will grasp the bones with forceps.
But if the head will come out and be caught at the pelvis: then it is split open, until the moisture which is in the chest pours out.
And if it does not come to a close and will not obey the exit: then cut the head into bits, as much as is possible for you.
And if the lower belly has an abscess, or is in it dropsy: then it is necessary for him to pierce it, until the moisture runs out of it.
But if indeed the fetus is above its feet, then its traction is easy and its direction towards the mother's birth canal is light.
But if it is caught at the belly or chest: then it is necessary for him to pull it out with a cloth which you wrapped over your hand: and split open the belly or chest, until what is in them flows out.
But if the remaining limbs are removed and the head returns: then the midwife will insert her left hand. And if the mother's birth canal is open, then she will insert her hand into the depth of the birth canal, and seek out with it the head, and pull it with fingers until the mouth of the birth canal: then she will insert in it a hook or two hooks, and pull it with them.
And if the mother's birth canal is constricted, because of the warm abscess that has occurred to it: then it is not necessary for her to be worked upon with it, rather it is necessary now to administer an effusion of moist things, greasiness: and apply emplastrum: and sit in waters which soften and moisten.
And if the fetus is on its side, then it is possible that it will be born normally: then let what we said about a living fetus be administered.
If it is not possible for that, then it should be cut little by little, and it should be extracted.
And you must make sure to leave nothing at all from the string inside entirely.
When therefore you have completed your operation, then administer what we said about hot blisters which occur in wombs.
And if there occurs a flow of blood, then let it descend into hot waters and administer the remaining cure.
And I myself have already seen a woman whose belly was dead with a fetus, then she became pregnant over it with another in its place, afterwards the other fetus died also. It happened therefore to her after a long time an abscess occurred in her navel and it was swollen until it was opened and she began to discharge pus.
I was therefore summoned for the cure of her, and I treated her for a long time: and the wound was not consolidated.
So I placed on it some plasters of strong adhesion, and it came out from its place. Then a few days passed for her, and another mouth came out. I was therefore amazed from that, since there is no mouth inside the belly.
So I thought that they were of the bones of a dead fetus.
I therefore asked the wound and I took out many bones, the woman being in better dispositions herself: and now she has lived thus for a long time, and sends from its place a little pus.
And I did not leave this foreign thing firm here, except that it has knowledge and assistance to that which the skilled physician administers with his hand in the cure.
Chapter 77.
About the shapes of instruments necessary for the extraction of a fetus.
The shape of the vertigo by which the uterus is opened.
Below is the form of a Strictorium, by which books are measured, to which two handles are equally attached at both ends of two wooden beams. However, this vertigo must be finer than a suture thread. And it can be made either from ebony or from the wood of the box tree.
And let the width of all the wood be as two fingers: and the edge of each is as one finger. And its length is a palm and half: and in the middle of the two woods are two additions of the same kind of wood, which are already secured to both, whose length is a half-palm and somewhat more: and their width is as two fingers or somewhat more.
And those two additions are inserted into the mother's womb, so that it may be opened with them when you turn back the two vertigos.
Another form similar to it, finer and lighter.
Let it be made from the wood of ebony or box, according to the shape of forceps. However, at its end there are two additions, as you see above: and the length of each addition is a palm: and the width of each is two fingers.
Therefore, when you wish to open the uterus with it, then have the woman sit on a bed, her feet hanging down, and the opening between her legs be uncovered. Then insert those two additions joined together into the uterus: and you are holding the end of the instrument below her hips. Then open your hand with the instrument, just as you do with forceps, equally in size to what you wish to be the opening of the uterus mouth, until the midwife does what she wants.
The shape of another vertigo which the ancients called The Impeller by means of which the fetus is driven out.
The shape of the Almisdac, by means of which the great head is crushed, and it resembles forceps, with teeth at its end, as you see.
And sometimes it happens that something is bent, like pincers in this shape, as you see, with teeth like the teeth of saws, by which it is cut and crushed.
The shape of a pair of pincers having one spike.
The shape of a pair of pincers having two spikes.
The shape of scissors having two ends for cutting off the head.
The shape of two broad scissors as well.
All these instruments are exercised and the more their forms are multiplied, and they are prepared by the craftsman, the quicker he is at his operation, and more esteemed among men because of their quantity.
Therefore do not despise any of them, lest it be prepared in front of you. For their necessity is inexcusable.
Chapter 78.
About the extraction of a second-degree (thing).
When a second-degree thing is retained at birth, then it should be given to the weak one, so that she retains her breath. Then make her sneeze with the condiment, and press your hand over her mouth, and her nose. And if it then comes out by this means, it is good.
If not: then take a vessel and pierce in its lid an opening: and put into it herbs that open the mouth of the womb, as calaminth, rue, and anniseed, and chamomile, and sesame, and cassia wood, and centaury: take all these herbs or some of them and immerse them in water and put them over the fire.
Then place a piece of reed wood over the opening of the lid of the vessel, and the other end in the mouth of the womb, and hold it until the vapor reaches the heat of the womb.
Then make her sneeze, as we said. For subsequently the Secundina will come out with ease.
But if it remains after this treatment and does not come out: then instruct the midwife to dip her hand into oil of siseam or almond paste. Then she should insert it into the womb and seek out the Secundina with it. When therefore she has found it, then let her take it and stretch it out gradually.
But if it is attached deeply within the womb, then she should insert her hand, as we have said, until she finds the Secundina and draws it out gradually along its length so that the womb does not fall due to violent traction: rather it should be turned with ease to the right and left side; then it should be added in quantity of attraction. For itself will then obey, and be preserved, and escape from adherence.
But if the womb's mouth is constricted, as we have already told you: treat it with sneezing and by medicine of the cauldron with herbs.
But if it does not come out with all that we have told you: then beware lest you weary yourself over her in the repetition of extraction.
But it should be bound to the woman's thigh where what comes out from it goes into the mouth of the womb. Then cleanse her with the ointment of Tetrapharmakon. For it itself will corrupt her after days and dissolve and come out.
But when it putsrefies, a bad smell rises up to the stomach and head, and hurts that sick woman. It is necessary therefore that suitable fumigations be administered for it.
Some of the ancients have already experienced a fumigation of nasturtium and dried fig.
The form of the instrument with which a woman is fumigated in the hope of retaining the Secondina and her menses.
Let it be made from wood similar to almond or rush: and let the broad end be placed over fire: and the fumigation be put above embers: let her hold it, until that fumigation is consumed: and let her repeat another.
Chapter 79.
About the cure of an unperforated abscess.
Many children are born and their abscesses are unperforated which have already been beset by a subtle inflammation. Therefore, the midwife should pierce that inflammation with her finger: and if not, she should pierce it itself with a sharp spatulum: and beware the arm, lest it touch it.
Then place on top of it wool steeped in wine and oil. Then care for it with ointments, until it is healed.
But if you fear that it will suppurate, then place a lead tube into the abscess on many days, and it is to be removed when the child wants to pass urine.
And now suppurating of an abscess also occurs from the closure of a wound or from an apostema. Therefore it is necessary that the consolidation be cut out, then it should be cured with what we said about the abscess and lead.
Chapter 80.
About the cure of fistulas which occur in the lower part.
Fistulas which occur in the lower part are nodulation and thickening which occur near the anus outside: or in a space of one part: and it is a fistula one, or many.
When therefore the nodulation becomes old, it opens and flows out of it a white aqueous humidity, or a subtle purulence.
And sometimes they are penetrating from these fistulas. Therefore the penetrating ones are recognized by what comes out from their emission and flatulence, when a sick person uses emission: and perhaps worms come out of them.
And they are fistulas when they penetrate into space up to the bladder and up to the root of the penis. And they are penetrating up to the joint of the hip and up to the nodule of the tailbone.
But from the fact that a fistula penetrating up to the anus is known even if it does not penetrate, is this: you insert your index finger into the anus and you insert a thin probe into the fistula made of air or iron; when there is no twisting.
But if there is a twisting in it, then insert into it a thin probe made of lead or a bristle from horsehairs until you feel the probe or bristle with your finger.
But if you do not feel it at all, nor does anything come out through the orifice from an emission, nor inflammation, nor worm as I said: then know that it is non-penetrating; therefore hurry to treatment.
And when the fistula is penetrating up to the bladder or to the urine duct then its significance is the exit of urine from it and prohibition of this so that the place may be consolidated with medicines.
But if it is penetrating up to the joint of the hip or to the bone of the hip then its sign is that a probe reaches that far if there is no twisting up to the bone and finding of pain in the surrounding part and what comes out pus from it always and that medication does not succeed in it nor does it consolidate with ointment.
And in all these fistulas penetrating there is no cure at all and the treatment of them is toil and vanity for foolish doctors, who are formed over him.
But in that one which is not penetrating and not old, there is hope for healing with a needle: according to what I tell you, and I have already proved it. And it is, that the infirm person lies between your hands on his back: and raise up his legs until above his hips, and his hips are inclined towards his belly. Then he introduces a lead or air probe into the fistula, if there is a twisting of it, until you know where the probe has reached.
But if the patient feels it around the anus, then you should introduce your index finger into the anus.
So if then you feel that the probe has penetrated through itself and is detected apart from what you feel between your finger and it, or the flesh: therefore you will certainly know that it has pierced, and you will not labor with it, since its cure was as we said.
However they said that the flesh is cured in some people and from the medicine by which one hopes for a benefit, it is, to apply a burning cautery finely according to the width of the fistula, just as was said before, and to introduce the ignited thing into the fistula until it reaches around the anus. Then repeat that twice or three times until you know that all the added and filtered flesh which are similar to the reeds of a bird's feather have already been charred.
Then cure that with linen bandages wrapped in butter until the charred flesh comes out: then cure that with ointments generating flesh and consolidating it.
If it is therefore cured, it is good.
And if not, then it will never be cured with any other treatment.
And if the probe comes out and has not reached as far as your finger which is in the anus, and there is a thick membrane of flesh or sifac between it and the probe: and you see the fistula on the surface of the skin that follows, then cut the skin from the beginning of the fistula, and you go cutting with the probe that is in the fistula until the incision reaches where the end of the probe comes out, and the probe will be freed and fall.
Then cleanse those filtered flesh parts which resemble the reeds of a bird's feather so that nothing remains from them.
If however blood overpowers you and dissolves between you and the operation so that it cuts out those meats: then the better thing is done, and more speedy assistance, to cauterize it with fire, or with sharp medicine.
Since a cauterization with fire brings together good conditions, the burning of those added flesh parts is added, and the drying up of blood and the drying out of humidity.
Then care for that with linens wrapped around it in butter, or crushed gallstones, until the place emits pus, and those burnt flesh parts will issue out into pus.
Then care for the wound with consolidating ointments, generating hard flesh: and there are medicines in which are coagulation and drying.
For when they are restored, then already it is healed: and you will not fear a relapse.
But if the fistula has already reached down to the depth of the anus, and is lengthened from excess of the body: then insert your finger into the anus, and question with it.
So if you feel a thickness, and between it and you is a membrane of siphac or flesh, and it is near to penetration: then there is no operation on it, unless according to the way of trust and hope.
Which is, because it is administered in one of three ways: either to cauterize it itself, as we said: or to cut it out until you attain its concavity, so that it may be possible for you to insert a linen and cure from close by. And do not proceed much with the cutting, lest you cut the muscle surrounding the anus, and there occur an unpleasant exit of excretion without will. Then care for that in the manner which we mentioned.
Perhaps it will be healed: or you will lengthen that membrane, and cleanse it after having cut it near the anus, as we said, with a tent, or with another sharp-edged instrument. Then cleanse those filtered meats which are in the incision, until you can with inspection.
Then attempt the restoration of the entire wound with the incision and the upper opening where it is consolidated: and a fistula remains open on the inner part of the anus, and it will be easier over an infirm person.
And sometimes the fistula is closed in this manner: and it is when you insert a probe into the fistula: and it is on the side of the anus near the surface of the body with skin and edge of the anus: then take the perforated end of the probe, which is like an awl needle, that is, a suture material, according to this shape. And insert into it a thread wrapped from five threads, and around it. Then insert the probe with the thread into the fistula until its concavity is reached.
If therefore it is penetrating into the edge of the anus on the inside nearby, then draw out the thread from that opening: thus inserting your finger into the anus and drawing out the end of the thread and gathering both ends together: and tighten both, and leave it for one or two days: and as much as the thread inclines in the flesh, tighten it well until the flesh between the two ends of the thread becomes incised and drops: then cure the wound until it consolidates and heals.
But if the opening of the fistula is not penetrating: then make it penetrate however you prepare for yourself, unless it be very deep. For indeed it is not for you to do that because of the tendon, lest you cut it.
Then do with it as we said, until it heals.
The shape of the splint for a spiny fistula in which it is cut, whose curvature is very sharp and the other part is not sharp so that what is not necessary does not cut into it, as you see.
Chapter 81.
Concerning the stitching of hemorrhoids from which blood flows out through an incision into them: and the cure of their cracks.
There are hemorrhoids in two ways. For either they are inside the anus, resembling carbuncles or blisters, or else like themselves are like clusters of grapes. And there are small and large ones: and blood flows from them always, and it is one, and they are many. And they are outside the anus, and at its extremities.
However those which are outside the anus are for the most part few in moisture, from which clear water or a little blood flows with steady course: and they are according to the color of the body.
And its cure which is inside the anus is, that you should command the patient to sit down and be pressed: and it should be squeezed until the anus comes out and they appear to you. Therefore also hang them with a hook: or hold them with your finger. Then cut into them at their roots.
But if the hooks do not hold in them because of their moisture and softening: then take them with a rough cloth, or with a strip from double woolen cloth, and pull them out with your fingers. Then cut into them, and sprinkle over them after the cutting some hot powders to make a cauterizing. Or cauterize them according to what preceded in the chapter on cauterization. Then cure them with the rest of the treatment until they are healed.
But if the anus does not obey evacuation, then purge the patient with a purgative in which is a little bitterness, so that he may be cleansed with it what is in his rectum and quickly obey evacuation when he presses out.
But if the growths are external to the anus, then their treatment is easy and simple: and it is as if you take them with your finger, or take them with a hook, and cut into them. Then treat them with that which we said, until they are healed.
And he who despairs of a cut with iron, then it is necessary to sew them up in this manner. Take up the thread which has been wound and insert it into the needle. Then draw out the needle to the upper part and make it pierce with the needle into its root from the other side: and wind both ends of the thread around the lower part of the needle and be placed laterally: and draw tight the needle strongly. Then tie the thread and draw out the needle.
Do this with all of them and let one [suture] go loose: do not make it so that the surplus of blood runs from it.
Then place a cloth dipped in rose oil or ceruse on the anus and place it over the anus: and command the patient to rest. Then let him go until he falls.
Therefore when the sutures fall out, treat them with ointments and the rest of the treatment, until they are healed.
¶ On the care of Fissures. For many times they happen from drying up of feces and constriction of nature.
So when they become old and medicine does not help in them: then it is necessary to scrape with the points of a sponge or with your own nail until they are moist and remove from them the top crust which prevents them from consolidating. Then treat them, until they consolidate as is necessary.
But if they do not consolidate: then repeat them with a sharper scraping than the first, until they are bloody and then scrape again treat them, until they are healed.
¶ On the cure of ulcers which turn inward, and not inward: and of dry sutures and of ants.
Chapter 82.
Know that Almirmar is nothing but a rounded knot according to the color of the body, similar to a key's handle: and it forms throughout the entire body, especially in the lower parts of the feet and fingers: and pain occurs at walking.
Therefore it is necessary to cut out that which is in the surrounding area of the key's handle, and hold it with forceps or a hook, and be rooted out from its root.
Then cure that; and if you wish, cauterize it according to what preceded in its chapter.
From the armpit however dry there is a small, rough, hardened, rounded elevation above the surface of the body. And many times it occurs on the hands of boys.
And its cure is to stretch out the armpit, and cut it: or bind it with a thread of silk: or with a thorn until it falls. And if you wish, cauterize it with fire or with sharp medicine: and now the armpits which were inverted are eradicated, and not those that I shall call by an instrument.
However a Formica is also a small, hardened, thick elevation above the surface of the body, running deep into it: and when cold weather finds it, one senses something similar to an ant's bite in it, and it occurs throughout the entire body: and most of all it occurs in the hands.
And its cure is to take a reed from the pen of a goose or a strong vulture: and place that reed over the Formica, until it holds the circle of the reed on its parts. Then turn your hand with the reed, until you cut out what is in the surrounding area of the Formica: and place it deep into the flesh, and root out the Formica from its root.
And if you wish, do that with a reed of air or iron, in this form.
The cannula should be stretched upwards as high as possible while drawing out the swelling, that it may be eased over the finger and its own reversal.
And if you wish to cauterize a scorpion according to what has been previously said in the chapter on cauterizing a burn.
Chapter 83.
On forms of instruments which are administered for cleansing of the intestines and solutions of the stomach and the bowels.
Sometimes a clister is made of silver or of tinned brass or melted bronze, or hammered iron.
And sometimes it is made from these instruments small and large, according to those in which they are administered.
Therefore that which is administered for the care of small children should be small; and that which is given to those whose intestines are weak or painful should also be similar.
The large clister.
Its tube, that is the upper part in which the uterus is tied, should be wide according to this manner; and there should be a distinction so that the uterus may be tied as you see it, and its lower extremity which is inserted into the anus, should be mute, that is not open, soft, very, drawing to subtlety: and on one side of it are two holes, and on the other side a single hole.
And the width of the hole should be according to the size of the stitches, a little larger or smaller, and it should be an uterus in which medicines are placed from animal bladder: or from ox parchment, which is shaped like a small dried sponge, whose root is one palm.
And it should be perforated with many holes as it revolves, and the space that is between every hole should be the width of a finger. Then insert into those holes a strong thread from raw thread, which is of ten threads, or about that many, and tie it with the uterus like a sponge. When therefore the uterus is placed on it, a string should be tied around the head with a strong constriction between two distinct divisions by means of a clister. Then tie a ligature with him.
And this is the shape of the circle and the openings depicted here.
But the quality of tying a ligature with him is: that you fill the uterus or bladder with medicine which is warmed according to the tolerance of the sick person: and beware lest it be very hot or very cold.
Then the sick person is laid back on his back and lifts up his legs to the upper parts: and place under him an heapan, that is, a skin on which men lie, or a cloth.
Then anoint the extremity of the clister with oil, or with lamb's fat, or with fenugreek paste, or similar things. Then insert it into the anus with ease. Then the servant should take hold of the uterus with both hands at once, and squeeze it out vigorously until all that is in the clister is evacuated into the intestine.
Then draw out the clister, and let the sick person lift up his legs to the wall.
But if the medicine causes him to evacuate, then he should hold on for as long as possible. And if he sleeps with it all through the night, it is a greater relief.
But if you tie him with the clister for diseases of the digestive tract and he hastens to evacuate it: then repeat the clistering until he is cured.
But if there are hemorrhoids or painful swellings in the anus: then let the clister be very fine and soft, like those used to tie up children.
And this should be the shape of a thin clister over which tight skin lies.
I don't see any text to translate. The notation "Caput LXXXIIII" appears to be a medieval heading, possibly from an index or table of contents. If you provide the actual text for Caput LXXXIIII, I'll be happy to assist with its translation into English.
I have said in the division concerning the cure of wounds what suited this book, and I will say here their cure with hand, and with medicines according to completeness.
I say therefore that wounds are divided according to the thing by which they are made, and according to the place on which the wound falls.
Therefore, the things by which wounds are made often are such as a stone thrown: or cutting with a sword, or a knife: or striking with a spear, or wood, or an arrow: and similar to those many things.
But the wounds that are made according to places of the body are such as a wound that falls on the head, or neck, or chest, or belly, or side: and similar to those members.
And I am relating the cures of some wounds, so that I may place this reasoning and rule over the remaining wounds.
And I will begin with simple head wounds, because the treatment of compound wounds will come at the end of the third chapter.
I say therefore. I say therefore. When a simple wound occurs in the head and there is no break of bone: then consider whether it was caused by a stone or something similar and already broke only the skin: and if the wound is large and you fear an evil outcome from a hot apostema: then hurry and apply immediately the cephalic phlebotomy: and do not delay it: and draw out from him blood according to the quantity of its virtue: if nothing prohibits bleeding, especially if the blood coming out of the wound is little. And let the phlebotomy be on the opposite side of the wounded part.
And let the phlebotomy be on the opposite side of the wounded part. And let the patient beware for himself to avoid filling, and wine, and coarse foods.
If something prevents a cutting, then it will be lessened from food, and it will not approach meats nor wines.
And place over the wound if it happens to have a hot apostume, a poultice infused in rose oil alone, or with wine in which there is sharpness.
And if you are free from a hot aposteme, then place over the wound, if it is recent with its own blood and has not changed air, powder which I will tell later: and if already it has changed air, then place over it certain ointments, until it heals.
And if the wound is great or is from a cut of a sword, and similar things: and its lips do not come together by themselves, then gather it with a suture, according to what I will say about the wounds of the belly regarding their sutures.
And if the wound has already been exposed by a bone and hangs, and there is no holding of it except with a small bandage: then cut it. Then cure the wound with medicines and restore hard flesh to it in place of skin, and command the sick person to nourish himself with foods in which there is thickness, such as heads and cooked barley until he be like a pulse: and things similar.
But if you find a putrefaction in the skin and it does not adhere to the bone: then cut out the whole putrefaction and throw it away: afterwards cure that.
And if there is an artery or a vein from which blood flows in these wounds and it cannot be cut off by remedies, then search above the artery. So if you find it not to be cut with a razor, then cut it with cauterization or bind it. And if necessity prompts and does not provide what we said, then cauterize it until the blood is cut off.
If however these wounds are small, simple, then the treatment of them is easy, light: thus powder them with this powder, for it solidifies them before air alters them.
The method of powdered flesh. Recipe one part of olibanum, and two parts of dragon's blood, and three parts of unextinguished ashes: it should be ground well and sifted through a sieve. The wound should be filled with this and constricted tightly until it adheres well on top and coagulates with the blood.
Then bind it tightly from above with bandages, and let it alone, nor loosen it until it remains in that way so that it does not become inflamed. For the medicine is not removed from it until it heals.
And sometimes it makes the same thing from ashes alone when you do not have olibanum and dragon's blood. And this also performs the same operation in many great wounds when it is placed on them and bound up, and they are fresh wounds with their own blood which air has not altered.
But if air has altered them in some way, especially when it is in the time of summer, then this powder is not in position for that: so treat it therefore with what we said, and place on it some digested ointment: or place on it cakes made from barley flour, made with water and honey, until they discharge pus. Then cure the rest of them with the remaining treatment, until they are healed.
But if a fracture happens with the wound in bones and it is small, then extract that thing with Galena. And I will now say the treatment of fractures of the head, as for what will be later.
On wounds of the neck.
But if a wound occurs on the neck then there is no difference in treatment between it and a simple head wound.
If however the nerve of the neck has already been incised, or the artery: then if it is a nerve, there is no treatment for it except that its care be abandoned with it just as it causes intense numbness, such as tinidrosis, and zegi, and similar things. Because the nerves injure them and contract them.
And do not place anything cold whatever on the wound: because the substance of the nerves is cold and its continuation is with the brain, which is a more elevated part of the limbs: and that with which it is treated should be gentle medicines such as slaked lime mixed sometimes with clear water and oil or rose oil, and tuthia when washed even with clear water: and the rest of the mineral stones in this manner, and humid unguents.
If however the wound is large, then administer a suture or bind its edges with lint. Then care for it until it heals.
If the wound has depth or occurs in a cavity on its lower part where pus has already accumulated, then puncture it in a more depressed place.
Which if it has already been incised in the wound and blood flow from it has followed: then separate or cut it, and bind it itself: or cautery it, if necessity prompts to that.
If however the vertebra of the throat has already been incised, or all of it, and other things have been saved: then bring together the edges of the skin with a suture over the cane of the throat, and do not touch the throat, indeed leave it alone, and return it to its natural shape. Then bind it tightly with a suitable bandage, and let it go for several days. Then care for it with ointments in which is numbness and dryness, until it heals.
If however the wound has already made an impression in the bone, then probe it: then if there are fragments of bone within it, extract all of them or as many as you can, and that which is not prepared, leave it until the wound becomes foul, separates, and its discharge eases.
If the wound is fresh, then sprinkle on it the powder which I have mentioned. But if a bone seems to be in the wound and an abscess forms: then treat it with that whose description comes in its own chapter.
On a cut on the soles.
If it is a blow from a lance or knife and you see its depth, then consider [the treatment]. So if from the wound there issues a swelling and when the sick person breathes with difficulty from it, then know that the wound is mortal.
But if it is not deep and is fresh with its own blood, then do not put powder on it from the first hour nor bind it so that the blood does not remain in its depth and return to the heart and kill the sick person: but put a drawing ointment on it and dissolve it twice a day.
But if the ointment is not present to you, then put old cotton in the wound's opening so that it sucks up what comes out of it from moistures: and have the sick person sleep over the wound, so that what gathers in it heals.
But if already three days or more have passed over the wound and there has not befallen the sick person a spasms nor bad trembling of the heart nor constriction in breathing, and you see its remaining conditions good: then know that the wound is sound. So therefore cure it with liniments and the rest of the treatment until it heals.
But if its healing is delayed and it always opens up, then know that an abscess has already been formed. So therefore cure it from its own chapter.
And if the wound is from a cut with a knife or sword and is simple on the surface of the chest or back: then cure it with that which preceded for suture, if it is great: or with powder, if it is small.
If, however, he has already made an imprint on the bone and it is splintered off from it; then examine the wound, and hasten to extract those splinters if they are separated. If, however, they are not separated, then leave them alone, until the wound puttrefies; for extraction of them is eased.
But as for other wounds that occur in other parts of the body, the judgment in their cure is the same as that which we said.
Concerning wounds of the belly, and the outlet of the intestine, and the outlets thereof.
A rupture which occurs in the belly is great, medium, and small.
From a great rupture, therefore, sometimes an intestine or the number of intestines are protruding.
Why there is intrusion of them, and their stitching more violent and more difficult.
And a small rupture also becomes difficult from another mode. Therefore it follows that their reduction is more difficult, if you do not hasten to their intrusion into its place at once, because it swells, opens up, and becomes enlarged: why their entrance becomes difficult.
On account of this therefore the middle rupture is a better kind of rupture: because with it the reduction of the intestine does not become difficult, as becomes difficult in these two kinds.
Know that the stitching of the belly is according to four modes.
Two common stitches are suitable for the stitching of the belly, and also for the stitching of other wounds of the body; and two other stitches properly communicate with the other wounds. However they are more proper for the stitching of the belly: and I will tell you each one with an explanation and a declaration.
I say therefore, that when the wound is small and something protrudes from it out of the intestine, and it becomes difficult to reduce it; then that is due to one of two modes. Or because of the fragility of the tear, as we have said: Or since there is inflation within the intestine due to the coldness of air.
When therefore it is so, then you should heat the thing itself, just as you would soak a sponge or a damp cloth in lukewarm water. Or you should boil the sap of the squill, and the root of the cypress, and the spike of the asafoetida, and apply them to the intestine with it until the inflation is resolved.
And sometimes he makes that wine in which there is astringency, and it is better than water alone for the resolution of inflammation.
And when you see the inflation already resolved, then moisten the intestine with water in which aloes or mallow have been cooked. For by that it is eased into him and a small thing is allowed to enter.
But if there is failure of return after this cure, then search for a little disruption with the instrument by which hemorrhoids are cut.
And the first cure is better when prepared than a cutting, and there is no need to be forced to cut afterwards because of necessity and deficiency of the instrument.
Let one part of it be twisted, sharp, and the other part not sharp, and the extremity subtle is not with subtlety so fine as to be like a Scampi, but rather slightly obtuse, and that which resembles a hook, just as you see.
And when the wound is therefore enlarged and enters the intestine, then it should be reduced back according to its natural form to its proper place for itself, if possible over it, since that is better.
And when the disruption is wide in the lower part of the belly, then it is necessary to have the sick person lie on his back and place their legs higher than their head. And if it is in the upper part of the belly, then place his head and chest higher than his lower parts.
And similarly if there is a disruption in one of the two parts of the stomach, then place your intention and your thought always on this, that the part where the wound is should be the higher part of the other.
And so it should be, that you take care of it in great wounds, and in middle-sized wounds.
But in small wounds, place it according to what is prepared for you.
Then have a pious attendant present between your hands, who holds the disruption with his hand and closes his lips. Then pull out from it a thread to one who follows to sew, bit by bit: and this is the common method of sewing in one.
And you should take needles, or a number of needles according to the size of the width of the wound. Then release from the end of the disruption a length of a finger's breadth, that is, of a doctor: and fix one needle on one side so that you insert thread into it in two ends of skin, with two ends of sifac as if what is under skin from the inside, until you make it pass through the other part, and you have already brought together two ends of skin, and two ends of sifac: and four orders are made.
Then tie with double thread around the circumference of the needle several times from both parts at once, until the two lips of the wound are properly brought together.
Then release the thickness of a finger and fix another needle into them: afterwards draw it back with thread, just as you did with the first needle. Therefore do not cease to do that with him from whom you need needles until you complete the closure of the wound altogether.
And let the needles be of medium size between thickness and fineness: because very fine needles quickly pierce the flesh: and coarse ones are difficult to enter into the skin: on account of this it is necessary that they be of medium size in fineness and thickness.
And it is not necessary that the needle be inserted into the two extreme parts of the skin near together much: so that the flesh does not cut quickly, and the wound is opened before its own consolidation, and the edges are not lengthened even with suturing, lest the wound be prevented from consolidating.
Then cut off the tip of the needle, lest it hurt the patient in his sleep: and place cloths around them on all sides that hold the ends of the needles: and leave them until you know that the wound is already consolidated.
And indeed this type of suture with needles is more suitable for small wounds. Because one or two needles suffice for their stitches, or roughly that many.
However the second stitch is common, that gathers with the stitch four edges: two of skin, and two edges of silk in place one with another, with a needle where there is thread wrapped around it equal in fineness and thickness.
Then when you have pierced with the needle these four edges, draw back the needle from the part where you started it again, so that the thread which is left above will fall, that it may be a stitch, like the stitching of cloth by which cloths are sewn together: and place between all the stitches the quantity of thickness of a small finger.
Men know this stitch.
And with this stitch I closed the wound which happened to a man in his belly, who had been wounded with a knife, and there was a greater disruption of the wound than the breadth of one hand: and he had come out from the intestines about two hands from the middle intestine, and there was a disruption in the middle of the abdomen.
Therefore I returned it after his intestine had stood outside the wound for 24 hours.
Therefore the wound was consolidated in a space of about 15 days: and I cured it, and he was healed: and he lived after that for many years, living in his usual ways.
And the physicians had judged that it would not be healed.
And by a miracle it was, that I did not heal it with ointment. Because I was in a place where the ointment was not found, nor anything else from medicines. So I placed therefore on the wound a worn-out linen cloth twice a day, and I made frequent washing of it with honeyed water, until it was healed.
But two indeed kinds of suturing proper I will say according to the narrative of speech.
And it is, that you begin with acute suture: and you insert the needle from the outer to the inner part.
When therefore the needle pierces the skin and into the muscle running according to length and breadth of the entire body: let go the end thereof which is in the side wherein you inserted the needle, and cause it to penetrate into the other end from the inner part to the outer in the other side.
When therefore you have made it to penetrate, then make it to penetrate thus in the very same end mirach from the outer part to the inner: and let go the end thereof which is in this side, and cause the needle to penetrate into the other end from the inner part to the outer: and cause it to penetrate with your penetration as you make it to penetrate into the end mirach which is in its part, until it penetrates into itself entirely.
Then begin also from this very same side, and sew it to itself with the end which is from the sifac in the other side, and draw out the needle from the skin wherein it pierced.
Then return it to that skin and sew the end of the sifac which is in the other side with this end mirach; and draw out it from the skin which is of its part, and make this thing vice versa, and another: and make this thing vice after vice, until you have sewn all its exterior according to that example.
The sum of this suture is to bind the sutures together like those used for skinning hides. So that you stitch them in one place with one end of the skin and then let it go again on the other side until you have completed.
The second kind of proper suture which Galemus spoke of.
This is his word said that you should do it in a way similar to what certain physicians do. They gather all parts together so they match each other by nature: and apply the end of one suture to the end of another, and the end of one thread to the end of another. And that is done according to what I am telling you.
You should fix the needle so that it is in the end of one thread close to you from the outer part, and make it penetrate to the inner part alone in it. And let go the two ends of the thread. Then turn the needle around and make it penetrate from the outer part to the inner part in the two ends of the thread, that is on both sides.
Then turn the two [threads] around again and also make them penetrate from the inner to the outer part, in the other two ends of the suture which are on the opposite side.
And this method of suture is a better and easier common suture, and it is the suture which was previously mentioned.
This then is also the word of Galen as well.
And some have said. The suture of the belly is corrected with two needles. That is, to insert one thread into both and start with the insertion of the needle from what is at your hand, and make it penetrate as far as to the other side, according to the suture of those who sew skins equally.
And know that when there is a disruption in the middle of the belly: then its suture is more difficult than other places of the belly.
The judgment of a wound's treatment is the judgment of other wounds.
For this is because when you treat a fresh wound with its own blood, before the air alters it, and reduce the intestine, and restore it to itself, and bandage it properly: then lay it on powdered flesh.
But if the air has already altered the wound, then lay on it some ointments which are present to you, until they make a discharge and threads fall and become healthy and sound. Then cure it as is the care of other wounds, until it heals.
But if medicines are not present to you, then lay on it immediately an old linen cloth where a discharge begins to come from, and change it twice a day, as I have taught you, until it heals.
For you do not need another medicine for most dispositions, when the wounds are simple.
But if you fear that the wound will communicate to the principal parts with an inflammation, then it is necessary that you soak a soft cloth in tempered warm oil or rose oil, and put it on the areas between the thighs and under the armpits.
But if it feels pain, or punctures, or putrefaction within its own intestines (for this often happens) then cleanse it with a warm, black, bitter wine enema: especially if the putrefaction has already reached the intestine and become an open perforation to its depth.
And know that the wound from the thick intestine is easier to heal and the one from the thin intestine is harder to heal.
The intestine however that is called the Jejunum, does not accept healing from a wound which happens in it altogether: and this is because of its multitude, which is formed from veins, and the magnitude of them, and because of the thinness of its body, and because of its proximity to the nature of nerves.
If however that which proceeds from the wound is pus, and you follow it up recently: reduce it according to your reduction, by which you reduce entrails equally.
But if it has passed the time and has already become green or black: then you must tie it up with thread above the place that was discolored from it, so that there may not be a flow of blood. For in pus are veins and arteries.
Then cut what is beyond that ligament, and place two ends of the thread hanging from the lower part of the wound outside of it: so that there may be a relief to you from its attraction, and extraction of itself in case of pus formation or discharge from the wound.
A reminder of the wound which occurs in the intestine.
When there occurs a disruption in the intestine and it is small: then it is possible that it may be restored in some people: therefore, because I have seen someone who was wounded in his belly with a spear thrust, and the wound was on the right side of the stomach, and the wound was old; and a fistula had formed, and there came out from it an evacuation, and inflammation.
I therefore applied a treatment, until I was not confident in his healing: nor did I cease to remove it, until he was healed, and the place consolidated.
After I saw the place now consolidated, I feared for the sick person, that an accidental evil would happen to him in his belly: and it did not happen to him from that accidental evil. And he remained in better physical dispositions, eating and drinking, copulating, and entered the bath, and was exercised in his usual habits.
And some of the experimenters have said, that when there occurs a wound in the intestine and it is small: then it is necessary to tie it according to this manner. And take the large-headed ants. Then gather two of the edges of the wound and place one ant with its mouth open over the two edges of the wound. When therefore it bites and closes its jaws, cut off its head, for it adheres and does not come loose.
