Then place another ant near the first one, and do not cease to repeat this with an ant after each other according to the size of the wound as a whole.
Then pull it back and sew up the wound. For its heads indeed remain attached to the intestines until they are eaten and the wound heals, and no harm will befall the patient entirely.
And now it is possible to sew the intestine with a thin thread, which I have tried and tested by attaching it to an animal's intestine after introducing it into the needle.
And take the end of this thread from the intestine and it will be very thick. Then bind a thin linen thread wound around its end. Then insert that thread into the needle and sew with it the intestine. Then pull it back to its place.
And this method of stitching with ants and intestine is nothing but through the way of faith and hope.
Know that a wound which is great and wide, especially if it is on one of the slender intestines, then there is no skill except for the gentle sewing, nor its cure at all.
Concerning the care of abscesses and fistulas.
Know that every wound or abscess which becomes old and turns to an ulcer, does not consolidate, and constantly discharges pus, and is not healed: then it is universally called a fistula, wherever it may be in the body, and we call it a Zucheum.
And indeed fistula is a true node that is filtered, hard, white; with which there is no pain, whose concavity resembles the feather of a bird: and because of this some call it risatis, i.e., a feather.
And it is in certain hours wet, which emits pus. And perhaps the moisture is broken up in certain hours. And sometimes this moisture is abundant and scant; and thick and thin.
And I have already warned in division, when I spoke of wounds, that every ulcer which does not heal, nor flesh grows in it, is not that except for one of nine causes.
One of which is, or because of the small amount of blood in the body: or because of its badness: or because there is hard flesh between it and its edges, preventing the growth of flesh: or because it is very dirty and foul-smelling: or because the ulcer itself is putrid, and the matter sent to it has bad quality: or because medicine is not suitable for its cure: or because a corruption falling in the region is a kind of plague, or by nature in the region, as happens in Caesaraugusta: in which there is difficulty in digesting sicknesses and healing boils is delayed by nature: or because there is one bone or a number of bones in it.
And I have already shown all those causes and signs of them, and their cures with medicines in division; and it is necessary here that I make you know the cure of fistula and zuken, which occurs by way of operation with the hand.
Therefore know that a fistula can occur in all parts of the body. For some fistulas reach the large veins and arteries, or nerves, or bladder; or the intestines, or the urinary tract, or to some of the ribs; or to some of the vertebrae of the back, or to some joints: such as hands and feet. And from these there are those that go towards the proximity of the main limb, and similar ones.
What therefore is next to these fistulas in terms of difficulty of healing, is one that does not accept treatment and its management is labor and ignorance.
But if it is from a fistula whose depth does not reach the places I mentioned: and you hope and trust in its cure, and wish to come to an understanding of it, then take a probe made from air or iron, if the fistula runs straight up and down, and seek out the bottom with it.
But if there is a twisting in the fistula, then probe it with a thin lead probe. Because lead, with its soft body, is alleviated and curved around the twisting.
And if the fistula has many openings, it will be impossible for you to find your way over it with a probe. Therefore cleanse one of its openings from among its openings.
For the moisture with which you clean it flows towards other openings and runs out through them.
Then carefully investigate by which means it is possible for you to know that there is an opening or a nerve, and whether the fistula's concavity is far away or near; or if it is one fistula with many openings, and stand over the whole of it with your greatest strength: with your questioning of the patient, and discovery of pain at compression with your hand on the place, and similar signs and causes of accidents that happen in an abscess.
So when you have stood over all those places truly, then go to the cure according to your confidence. And it is, as you consider then, if there is a fistula appearing near or in a safe place far from the joint, or from a nerve, or artery, or vein, or one of the places I told you: then cut the fistula according to what has been previously said in my narration, and remove what is in it by filtration, and corrupted flesh that is born in its opening from the flesh or skin, and similar things: and heal it until it is cured.
If however a fistula is of great depth and along the straightness: then it is necessary that you cut it as deep as possible. Then cleanse it from all corrupted flesh around it.
Then administer a linen strip wrapped in sharp medicines; and insert it into the hollow of the fistula as far as you can reach with iron; do this several times until the sharp medicine eats away all that remains in the hollow of the fistula from the corruption.
Then restore it with ointments which generate healthy flesh, until it is healed.
If it does not heal by that, then cauterize it as we have described.
If therefore the cause of the fistula is a bone, and you are certain about it: then investigate it, and cut it out as I told you to; if nothing from the vein, or nerve, or main member hinders you, as we said.
If therefore a bone is revealed to you and there is something of corruption and blackness in it: then rasp it away until the corruption of it is entirely removed. Then care for it with that which fleshes it out, until it heals.
But if it does not heal and discharges pus as it was doing: then you will know that you did not reach the complete eradication of the corruption.
Therefore scrape again over it and make a careful examination of its cavity and cleansing of it with all your might.
Then restore it. But if it heals, good; and if not, then you will know that the corruption is in a distant depth of the body which you did not reach. So there is no ingenuity for you with it, except to leave it to nature.
But if the corrupted bone is small and you are able, draw it out with fine pincers that suit it.
If however there are many bones, then make an exact union of them all, and do not leave anything out with your strength.
If however something makes you oppose it from the moistures, and there is no fear or error in their incision: then cut those moistures and flesh, if they are there, and free the bones.
If however you do not have preparation for removing the bones from your wound, then place something over it that will make it rot itself: and leave it alone, until what is around those bones from flesh and moistures has rotted.
And guard the wound so that it does not heal. And tighten the incision which you made in your operation. Indeed place over it, if you fear, a cloth immersed in crushed sulfur mixed with oil: or a cloth immersed in Egyptian ointment, or some green unguents.
For when those moistures rot, namely they will separate from them bones, the removal of them and their union is eased.
If however there is one large bone, such as the leg bone or hip bone, and similar to those, and what has become corrupted from it is a major part: then shave it with a final scraping, until that blackness and corruption are removed. Then restore the wound.
If however what has become corrupted in it is a major part, and corruption has already reached the marrow of the bone, then it is necessary to saw it and cut it all the way down to where the corruption has come: then care for it until it heals.
And I tell you Zukan, what happened to a man in his leg: that you may set the example and aid over your own operation.
There was a young man about thirty years old, to whom happened pain in his leg, from cause which moved against him within the body, that is, from inside, until it came to the thigh and formed a great apostema: and there was no external cause.
Therefore it was extended with him at that time with the error of physicians, until an abscess was opened up, and many materials occurred with him. And after that his care was abandoned until the leg had become a fistula and many openings were made in it: which all emitted pus and bodily humors.
Therefore a multitude of physicians treated him for about two years and there was no one skilled in the art of surgery among them until I was called and I saw his leg and the materials that were flowing from those openings in great quantity: even his entire body had grown foul and its color was yellow.
Therefore I inserted my probe into one of the openings, and it reached to the bone.
Then I examined all the openings, and I found that they were all communicating with one another from every part of the leg.
I therefore proceeded and cut over one of those openings until I exposed a part of the bone: and I found it to be corrupted: which was already eaten away, and denatured, and had become putrid, and perforated, until it had penetrated all the way to the marrow.
I therefore cut out what was found in me, and prepared from the corrupted bone.
And I left what would not be in the bone except that corruption which I cut out and removed. And I had already eradicated it.
Then I placed the wound's restoration with consolidating medicines over a long period. And it has not consolidated.
Then I tried again and cut into the bone a second time above the first discovery, and I found continuous corruption with the bone: and I removed what appeared to me from that very corruption.
Then I attempted to restore it. And it was not restored.
I therefore cut into it again and did not stop cutting away the bone little by little, and attempting to restore it: and it was not restored, until I cut out from the bone about a hand's width, and extracted that with its marrow.
Then I restored him with medicines, and he was quickly consolidated and healed. And this repetition of the operation on him and his section was not necessary, except because of the weakness of the sick man's condition, and the scarcity of his tolerance, and my fear for him from death: since harm befell him at every hour from excessive evacuation of bad humors.
He was therefore completely healed by the healing: and there was formed in place of the bone hard flesh, and a good disposition was made in his body, and his strength returned: and he became converted to his former dispositions: and no harm or injury at all occurred to him on walking.
But if the prominent bone in place of the body is already broken: then it is necessary that you set it, according to this manner: and thus: take a cord, and bind it at the end of the projecting bone: and order someone to stretch it upwards: and put another cord from thicker wool on top of the first cord. Then bind it on top of the flesh which is under the bone: and stretch out its extremity so that you pull the flesh downwards: and you must uncover the flesh over the place which you wish to saw, so that it does not injure the saw.
Place a wooden board or a plate under the lower bone properly: because when you do this, it is not prevented that the corrupted flesh will be cut.
And it is necessary that the sawing be a little over the place of corruption, so that there may not be concavity of the corrupted bone: and may not appear in its sensation, and may cause another to come in place of the sawing.
But if the bone is corrupted and not prominent, indeed its part continues with another part: and corruption is in the middle of it or in one of its parts: then uncover all the flesh from around it completely. Then place a wooden board underneath.
Then saw it from the first part where there is corruption, until the sawing continues from the other part: and let the sawing be according to length slightly beyond the corruption, as we have said.
If there is corruption in a joint, cut out the corrupted joint itself, and saw off the bone where it continues on the other side.
If indeed corruption is in the joining of two joints, then there is no remedy for it except abrasion.
But if corruption is in the pelvis of the hand, or in the pelvis of the foot: then the case in that part is very difficult. However, you must inquire about the corruption and see how it appears to you. And you should scrape it clean and cleanse the area according to what is possible for you, and with whatever device you correct it when it does not cause contradiction in a vein or nerve.
And you should know that scalpels and saws for cutting these bones are many, according to the location of the bones, and their preparation, and their thickness, and subtlety, and size, and smallness, and hardness, and rarity. Therefore it is necessary that you prepare all sorts of surgical instruments fitting for the surgery.
And know that these operations are meant to tell you about the kind of instrument you need, when you have a long study with it. And knowledge of the ways of this art is a witness to the ways of diseases; then indeed you will already find through yourself what suits it from instruments for every weakness.
And I am establishing for you at the end of this chapter a number of instruments that you will put examples on which you will cut, and a proof by which you will try others.
The shape of another saw.
The form of a razor for joining things together. Let the head of this razor be in the shape of the head of a star-shaped needle, and its points to be in the shape of the points of a slitting needle.
And it is not suitable unless its heads are rubbed with them when the joining parts corrupt or a large and great bone.
The form of the greater razor.
The form of the wide razor.
The form of a curved razor.
The form of another fine razor.
The form of the other curved-end razor.
The form of a small, fine and pointed razor.
It resembles a reamer and its end is like a saw: it is shown in many places for the reason of bones.
The form of the razor which suits the piercing of bones.
Its end is triangular with sharp points.
It is made from steel. And similarly all the things that we have said should be made from razors and knives and saws.
About the cutting off of extremities and the removal of bones.
The extremities putrefy either from an external or internal cause.
When then you have been medicated for that corruption and the cure does not take effect: and you see corruption occurring in a member which is not relieved by anything: then it is necessary to cut off that member as far as the corruption goes, so that the sick person may escape death or greater harm from the defect of the member.
And the sign that it has happened is that the member becomes black until it rots: which when fire burns it or after blackening putsrefies: until the decay is cut out as far as follows that member and spreads throughout the body.
Therefore with its wound and similarly if the cause of corruption is from the sting of some venomous things: as of the sea scorpion, and viper, and rattlesnake, and similar ones.
Therefore if the corruption or sting is in the extremity of the finger, then cut off the finger and do not allow the corruption to spread so that it may ascend to the rest of the hand.
And similarly if it happens in the arm, then cut it off from the elbow and do not allow the corruption to spread until it reaches into the forearm.
And if it invades that one, then cut off the arm at the elbow at its joint.
But if however corruption passes through and you see him around the elbow: then in that one is the death of the sick person. And to administer anything beyond that from cure is quantity of power.
And similarly do this for the foot, when corruption invades the finger, then cut off the same at one of the joints: and if it reaches into the heel of the foot, then cut off the entire foot. But if it ascends to the knee, then cut off the leg at the joint of the knee.
But if corruption has already reached above the knee, then there is no art in it except for cutting off and leaving the sick person to death.
And the method of cutting off a member or sawing it is, to tie the sinew below at the place you wish to cut: and tie another sinew above the place. And let the attendant stretch one sinew towards the lower part: and let the attendant stretch another sinew upwards to the top. And you separate the flesh that is between the two sinews, with a broad spatula, until the whole flesh is exposed. Then it is to be cut or sawed.
And it is necessary that you place a linen cloth over all the parts and the saw should not touch the healthy place: and an added injury occurs to the sick person and a hot abscess.
If, however, a bleeding of blood should occur internally while you are operating, then burn the place quickly; or put over it some powdered substances cutting off the blood. Then return to your treatment until it is completed.
Then bind the wounded limb with a suitable bandage and take care of it until it heals.
And I tell you what happened to a man on his foot, the same thing that I am telling you.
Which is that a blackness occurred on his foot, with a burning like a fire's combustion; and the corruption first occurred in his finger, until it began to spread throughout his foot.
Therefore the man drew out from himself, after he saw the corruption fall on his member with its inflammation, which he found because of pain and burning of it: and it fell at the joint, and he was healed.
And after a long time had passed over him, that same corruption occurred to him in the finger of his hand on his index.
So he came to me, and I tried to recover that excess with him on the hand after putting medicines over his body: and the excess was not stayed, and it began to go into another finger; until corruption occurred in his hand.
So he called on me to cut off his hand: and I did not want it, hoping from me on the recovery of that excess: and I also feared death for him at the cutting of his hand. Because the man's virtue was over taking a risk.
Therefore after he had despaired of me, he returned to his land: and it came to me about him, because he hastened, and cut off his hand entirely: and he was healed.
And I did not tell this story, unless it was to be an aid in regard to that which falls into the kind of this sickness: and so that there would be indication by means of which it should be indicated, and according to which it should be done.
On the treatment of sinuses and the quality of the cleansing with medicines.
When an apostema occurs in certain fleshy parts and the space of the apostema is prolonged until it forms a collection of pus: then it bursts or becomes perforated, and what is inside comes out with the pus, and the place remains empty like a pouch: and the skin that covers it is like a cloth that has been worn thin: and it does not reach its ultimate point in corruption, nor presses into the bone, nor into the nerve, nor into the ligament: it deserves to be called a sinus: but if its space is prolonged until it imprints corruption on something of these parts, then it is called a fistula or abscess.
And its treatment is to cut the entire skin over it as it peels off: and especially when it has been worn thin and become like a cloth: and you trust that it will not adhere with the place because of its corruption.
But if you hope that the skin will adhere and the corruption does not reach its ultimate outcome: and if there is a thickening of skin on it from flesh: then treat it with a poultice: and it is as if you are considering a sinus.
Then if it is large and the pus that comes out of it has a foul odor: then cleanse it with Egyptian medicine, which is made from equal parts of vinegar, oil, and zinar (or gum arabic): they are mixed together in a vessel: and heated over fire until the medicine becomes red: and thickens like honey.
Then take as much of it as you need and dissolve it with water and honey: and cleanse the sinus with it: and bind shut its opening, and leave the medicine inside for the space of two hours.
Then draw it out with a gentle squeezing: do this daily until the sinus is cleaned and the foul odor is removed.
And sometimes it is cleansed with a cinder water, when this medicine is not present to you: and thus, take the ashes of vine wood or ashes from ilex trees; and pour water over them. Then anoint it with oil and cleanse her with it until you trust that the sinus has been washed clean.
But if the sick person is not able to tolerate Egyptian medicine nor cinder water, then cleanse him with water and honey in which something from ground gallnut has been mixed. Or cleanse him with honey and wine mixed together, since the nature of honey is to purify and wash, and wine makes the sinus adhere especially when there is excess dryness and astringency in it.
When you have done this several times and trust that the corruption of the sinus has been removed: then cleanse him with what makes flesh grow there.
And it is, to take from the diaphrenic ointment and dissolve it with rose oil and astringent wine: or cleanse him with some medicines and other ointments, whose properties I wrote down in the treatise on ointments.
But if the opening of the sinus is narrow, into which the cannula of the cleanser will not enter: then widen it with iron for a little while: or place a linen folded in Egyptian ointment, or in basilicon ointment, within it until it is widened.
And similarly if the opening is also large, then join its lips with a suture and let it be empty of as much quantity as the cleanser will enter without adding anything.
And similarly if the opening from which pus flows is raised upwards: then cut it at a lower place that is within it, so that the pus will flow out of it downwards: because when pus is constricted in the depth of the sinus, it prevents flesh from growing there.
If it is not possible for you to cut the sinus around below it according to your will: then try, as you prepare the member with a preparation by which it runs from it pus easily according to how it is prepared for you. Try, that nothing of the pus is retained in it altogether: and take consolidating medicines which are put on a sign, fitting for what you wish. And stretch them out over linen cloth. And let the cloth be of such a size as to make communication of the whole sinus. Then cut with scissors into the direct opening of the sinus' hole, enlarging the opening of the sinus a little. Then stretch out a cloth dipped in gentle ointment over another cloth, according to the size of the hole; and place it on top with a position far from the opening of the sinus, so that it eases the exit of pus from it: and you will not remove the large cloth unless after some days once.
But that one which is over the opening of the sinus, you remove every hour, so that what gathers in it from pus may go out; and so you will know whether what runs out from it is much or little, or digested or undigested.
And when you have done this again, examine the place of the sinus itself, if its owner feels pain in it, or if there is no pain in it; and if there is an aposteme in it, or if there is not an aposteme in it.
When therefore your medication is thus prolonged, and you see the matter which comes out over the opening of the sinus, then you will know that the sinus already adheres, or approaches adhesion: then therefore dissolve it in the morning, or on the third day: and when you have dissolved the wound, then change another cloth, and renew the ointment.
But if after a long space there comes out from the sinus a thin and subtle pus, then do not lose hope of its adhesion, for its healing is approaching, because among men are those who retard the formation of flesh in their own wounds, and also those who act against it.
If however unclean discharge issues from a sinew after many days, then you should know that the sinew is not adhering.
If therefore the sinew is delayed in adhering and its treatment is prolonged, then add to the drying remedies that true physicians do, so that they be moist in substance and dry in virtue: as balm of Vaseline when excess of the added ingredient from gall is removed.
And from the final most serious treatment, is that you take balm of Vaseline, and dissolve it with rose oil, and pour it over equal wine in substance.
Then apply it to him, and use it for him.
And now do the same with this action, when you do not have balm of Vaseline present, honey, when it is cooked and thickened, use it. Or take myrrh, and aloe, and galbanum; rub all together, and sprinkle that over honey when it is still on the fire. Then tie it over it with a cloth, and bind it over the sinew; then tie with honey which you cooked until thickened: and sprinkle over it spices, and place on top a cloth, and bind him.
And sometimes it is administered to that Aristolochia longa, and roots of lily of heaven, and the lily itself, and wheat of the herb, and Centaury. Use them simple or compound, rub them together, and sift it; and sprinkle over the honey that I have spoken of, and use it with it.
But if the treatment of the sinew is prolonged and does not heal with this cure: then know that there is corruption deep within it; or already it makes an impression in the bones, or in the other hard parts, or in the ligaments: then treat it with the cure of Abscesses, as was previously said.
About the care of paralysis and worn nails, and the cutting of an added digit, and division.
Paralysis is a boil which arises under the nail of the thumb of the hand, or foot, and perhaps it also arises on other fingers.
Therefore when it prolongs and is neglected in its care, and boils up with a warm boil, or becomes corrupted and lets out pus, until it corrodes the root of the nail, and perhaps corrupts it all the way through to the bone, so that there is a foul odor: and the end of the finger becomes swollen, and its color becomes dull.
Therefore when you are treating it, as we said in the division, and your treatment is not effective: then you must cut out with iron all the superfluity that remains from the nail. Then cauterize the wound after that. For a cauterization is very helpful.
But if the bone is sound and the nail is also healthy, and the additions of the nail are now compressing the flesh to the inside, and begin to pierce it, and hurt it: then you must put a thin rod under the angle of the nail which pierces the flesh, and push it upwards: and cut out the flesh with ease, and put over what remains of the flesh medicines that burn and corrode, until all of it is removed. Then treat it with ointments, until it heals.
But if the corruption has already sunk into the bone, then you must cut it out and remove it: for it will never heal while the corrupted bone remains.
And if you see the corrosion and corruption spreading into the finger, then cut it off at a joint, according to what has been said before about its amputation. Then treat it, until it heals.
If, however, a nail has been driven into or struck an artery: and there occurs in it a severe pain, then the patient must first be bled. Then cut off the nail with a sharp steel saw from top to bottom.
And be careful that the cutting does not reach the flesh which is under the nail, for through it a severe pain will occur on the patient and there will be a cause for the birth of new flesh in place.
Then cure the place with what soothes pains.
However, the finger which grows on some people's hands, is perhaps entirely fleshy and perhaps in some of them there are bones and perhaps a nail, and sometimes it grows from the root joint or its birth is in some of the finger's nodes. Therefore, that which grows in some of the finger's nodes does not move and that which grows at the joint of the finger perhaps moves.
So, therefore, that one which is fleshy is easily cut off. Which is to cut it at its root with a wide-bladed steel.
But the cure of that one whose birth is in the joint of the knuckle is difficult. Therefore refrain from cutting it.
But truly he who grows on the finger at the last joints, then it is necessary to cut into his flesh first with a rounded cut up to the bone. Then saw through the bone with one of those saws fitting for it. Then cure him until he heals.
But the joining together of fingers which often occurs together, happens sometimes from the birth of man, that is, it grows with him, or from the healing of a wound, or by burning of fire, and similar things.
Therefore it is necessary to cut off that joining until the fingers return to their natural shape. Then place between them a ligature, or linen cloths soaked in rose oil, so that they do not heal quickly, and separate between them; or place between them a thin sheet of lead, until the place consolidates as it should.
And similarly if there is a consolidation of some fingers with the palm, then cut that consolidation, according to how it is prepared and fits the shape of the member.
About cutting a wound.
Ulcers are veins that are wrapped around, thick, and full of melancholic superfluities which occur in many parts of the body: and most often they occur in legs, especially in the legs of travelers and workers, and those who carry heavy loads.
Therefore you must administer a purging of the body from black bile several times with strong cleansing.
Then apply bloodletting to the patient's Basilica vein.
The treatment of them with iron is according to two modes. One of them is, that they are cut, and the black blood is drawn out. And the other mode is, that the vein be lifted up, and the whole thing drawn out.
The cutting of it is according to this method. Wrap the leg first with hot water very much, until the thick and feculent blood dissolves. Then bind the weak leg of the patient from the upper part of his hip to the lower part of his knee with a bandage. Then cut the vein in one place, or two, or three wide incisions.
Then raise it, that is, draw out the blood from the lower part of the leg up to the top, and first let the physician take out the blood from the upper part. Then cut the nail down to the bottom until the quantity of blood that you see is sufficient and as much as the weakness of the patient can tolerate.
Then bind her up. And order him to abstain from foods producing black bile. And let blood and repeat evacuation and venesection when the veins are filled, and it harms the sick one.
Its evacuation however is in this manner. Shave the legs of the sick person if there are many hairs on them. Then insert him into the bath or pour hot water on his leg until it reddens and the veins are swollen: or exercise him with strong exercise if there is no bath present, until the member is warmed.
Then cut the skin in a straight line along the direction of vein sectioning or at its last part near the knee, or at its lower part near the heel.
Then open the cut with a hook and strip the vein from all sides until it appears to the sense: and at its appearance you see it reddening towards blackness. When therefore it goes out of the skin, you see it white as if it were a cord.
Then insert under it some linen until when it is raised and goes out of the skin: suspend it with a blind hook gently.
Then cut another section nearby, three fingers from that first cut. Then strip the skin away from the upper part of the vein until it appears. Then raise it with linens as you did, and suspend it with another hook as you first did.
Then cut another section, or more sections if necessary for that. Then raise and incise the very last section at the heel.
Then draw it out to the section which is above it.
And do that until you draw it out from the upper section or all higher sections, until when it goes out entirely; then cut it.
But if it does not obey you to be drawn out and raised: then insert a hook with a strong string doublefold, and bind it, and draw it out; or insert under it some linen: and turn your hand over in all ways until it comes out. And beware, lest it be cut. For if it is cut, it becomes difficult for you to lift it up greatly, and a hurtful trouble enters into the sick person from it.
So when you have drawn it out entirely, then place over the place of wounds a cloth dipped in wine and rose oil, or in oil, and take care of him until he be healed.
But if the vein is contracted, having a twist to which its turning is towards the parts, and it is not as we said according to straightness: then it is necessary that you cut on top of it for all part of its twist, and places of its turning.
Then suspend it with hooks, until you lift it out entirely: and beware near your cut on top of it lest you cut the vein, or wound it: for it will be difficult for you to lift it, or draw it out. Therefore preserve your prudence.
The Form of Cutting by which the Vein is healed.
The Form of a Blind Hook.
It does not have the curvature as with the other hooks, nor is it sharp at the end, so that it may not wound the vein: and it has a thick, gentle bending, because if it were slender, it would cut the vein by its slenderness: nay, let its thickness be superfluous, as we said.
On the drawing out of the vein of the thigh.
This vein is generated in the thighs on hot lands, such as Egypt and the land of the Arabs, and on dry hot lands, a few in softness, that is, in which grass nor tree does not grow.
And perhaps it is generated in other parts of the body besides the thighs.
And its generation indeed is from the putrefaction that occurs under the skin, just as it happens in the bodies of snakes and ascarid worms, and the worms generated between the skin and flesh.
And the sign of the beginning of this vein's outcome is, that an inflammation occurs mildly in the leg. Then the area becomes watery. Then the vein begins to protrude from that place of swelling, as if it were a root of a plant or an animal.
So when its extremity appears from it, then it is necessary to wrap around it a piece of lead whose weight is one and a half drachms, up to two drachms, and tie the thread on it: and let the lead hang from the leg.
Whenever therefore something protrudes from it to the exterior, wrap that up in lead and tie it. But if it is prolonged much, then cut off a part of it, and wrap the remainder. And do not cut it from its roots until it has protruded completely. For if you cut it, it contracts and enters into the flesh: and causes an abscess to occur, and a foul decay in the place, and a bad ulcer.
Therefore it is necessary that it be treated gently, and extracted gradually, until it protrudes completely from its last part, and nothing remains of it in the body: and from this vein a certain thing issues forth in some men which is five palms and ten in length. And now it has come to me, because it protruded from a man of twenty palms.
But if it is cut into you during your time of treatment: then insert a needle into its hole and perforate it with a long hole, along with the body, until all that is in it is emptied out from matter: and administer corruption to the place with medicines for several days. Then treat the place itself with the cure of abscesses.
And this vein indeed sometimes has many branches, especially when it appears at the joint of the foot, or on the foot itself: and many openings occur to it, and a branch issues out from every opening.
Therefore, cure it as we said in the division, and with what has been said beforehand.
On the cutting of worms that have been generated under the skin, and it is called a horse affliction.
This affliction is called among us a horse affliction, on account of the fact that it often occurs in horses, and it is a small worm which is born between the skin and the flesh.
And it wanders throughout the body, ascending and descending, and is perceived by sense from member to member, until it bursts wherever it bursts through a place in the skin, and comes out.
And its generation is from the corruption of some humors. Just as worms and serpents, that is, earthworms and tapeworms occur in the belly.
And it is feared from its evil. Because when it itself goes into the body, and rises to the head, and reaches a place: then perhaps it opens up in it, and comes out, and destroys an eye: and that happens very often.
Therefore when you wish to cure it and extract itself, then it is not done unless during its movement, and appearance of itself to sense.
Therefore you should bind what is above it and what is below it with a strong binding tie. Then cut above it, and extract it.
But if it has sunk deep into the flesh, and you do not find it: then place a cautery with fire on top of the place, until it dies.
And more feared is its corruption, whereby it corrupts an eye, as we said.
But if you see it already having arrived at the head near the eye, then bind under it the eyebrow with a good binding. Then cut above it and extract it.
And it is necessary that the sick person should purify his body with medicines dissolving bad and foul humors.
And let him beware of foods that generate putrefaction.
Concerning the cutting over an ailment called Nakir.
This ailment is called Nakir in our land, and it is a pain that occurs in some members, then it passes from member to member.
And I myself have now seen it, just as I am telling you. I was called to a sick woman in certain villages. And she showed me her arm, and I saw a small swelling in the vein of the arm's tendon.
And after I had stayed an hour, I saw one swelling move with the flux, as a worm moves, ascending to her shoulder more quickly than it can be, just like quicksilver when it runs from place to place.
Therefore the pain receded from that place and was fixed in the shoulder.
Then I sat for an hour, and it ran to the rest of the body until it reached another arm.
Then she told me that it was revolving throughout her whole body, just as I had seen.
I therefore marveled at the swiftness of its change from member to member. And I myself had not seen this ailment with my own eyes in such a manner before that time. But I had seen many who found the pain to be changed from member to member, and I did not see it myself with my own eyes as I saw it in the woman.
And I do not think that it is anything else, because the woman was from those people who have dry bodies and their veins are found to be detected: therefore this wind-like swelling appears to me to be changed.
And it is necessary that they not be visible according to this reason in those who are delicate and have moist bodies, of hidden veins.
Therefore when you wish to treat it and the patient feels that pain, then if it is visible to your eye as we said, then bind something over it quickly from above and below: and cut off above it until the swelling goes out: and cauterize the place.
But if you do not see it with your eyes: then treat it with purging of the body and with that which expels swellings, and plaster them with pills of stinking things, and pills of asafoetida, and similar things.
On the removal of arrows.
Arrows are differentiated according to their kinds, and according to the places in which they fall from the body.
Their diversity is that there are those with large points and small ones: and concave and curved. And among them are those which have three angles. And from them there are tongues. And from them there are stars.
Those however that fall according to members in which they fall, is according to two modes. Or else the members are of concave principal members, such as the brain and heart and liver and lung and kidney and intestine and bladder and those similar. Therefore when it falls into one of these members and signs of death appear to you which I will tell you next, then it is necessary for you to flee from the extraction of that arrow from them, because death follows him in whom it is, in many dispositions. And when these bad signs do not appear to you and the arrow is not hidden in the depth of the member, then extract it and treat the wound.
When a bullet falls into the brain and penetrates the skull, and exits through the siphac that is above the brain, the symptoms are: because of it there occur numbness, weakness, scotoma, vertigo, redness in the eyes, swelling, redness of the tongue, spasm, and mixture of intellect, and choleric vomiting. And perhaps blood will issue from the nostrils or ears. And perhaps speech is cut off and voice is destroyed. And a white fluid like pus issues from the place of the wound. And it comes out similar to meat juice.
If therefore these signs appear to you, then abstain from treatment and draw out the arrow if it does not issue.
The signs of the arrow when it falls into the heart are: that it is near the left nipple: and it feels as though itself is fixed in a hard thing, not in an empty one. And perhaps the movement of the arrow is similar to the movement of the pulse: and blood flows from the wound that is black. And it follows that chillness of the extremities, and cold sweat, and syncope. Know therefore that death comes certainly nearby.
And the sign of the arrow when it wounds the lung is the issuance of frothy blood from the wound: and the vessels that follow the neck become inflamed, and their colour changes to that of an invalid. And it breathes out a large, deep spirit: and it seeks the inhalation of cold air.
Which if the arrow falls into the membrane that is in the breast: then it is near to the small ribs: and there is a great panting with severe pain, with sighing: and all the members of the shoulders are moved.
And if the arrow falls into the stomach, then perhaps something undigested comes out from the wound.
And if an arrow falls into the abdomen and is stuck there: something exits from the wound, either feces or a intestine that has already ruptured. So then there is no hope for its cure, nor for the removal of the arrow.
Which if the arrow falls into the bladder and urine exits from it, and something proceeds from it to the outside, and the pain is greater over the patient: then you will know that it is mortal.
But other members, such as the face, and neck, and throat, and scapula, and ribs, and furcula, and coxa, and leg, and similar ones are cured from their wounds according to many dispositions: when the arrow does not find an artery or a nerve, and the arrow is not poisonous.
And I tell you what I have seen regarding these kinds of arrows, so that you may know through it about your own cure.
Which is, because an arrow fell into the tear duct of his eye, at the root of his nose. So I extracted it from him on the other side, under the fat of his ear, and he was healed, and there was no damage to his eye.
And I extracted another arrow from a Jew, which had fallen into the fat of his eye under the lower eyelid: and the arrow was already hidden, and I did not manage to extract it except for a small part of it, with which it adhered to its wooden shaft: and the arrow was large among the arrows of Turkish bows, with a light iron point, in which there are no barbs.
So the Jew was healed, and there did not occur any evil thing in his eye.
And I extracted another arrow from a Christian's throat.
And the arrow was Arab, and it is that one with barbs. I therefore attached to it two thread-like filaments, and already they were submerged in his throat. I have therefore managed to extract it from her with difficulty, until I drew it out: and the Christian woman was healed.
And I extracted another arrow from a man that had fallen into his belly: and we thought, that he would die from it.
After therefore thirty days had passed over him, and nothing in his conditions had been changed, I followed after the arrow: and I devised a plan for it, and I extracted it: and he was healed, and no evil thing happened to him.
And I saw a man upon whom an arrow had fallen into his back, and the wound over it had been closed. After therefore seven years had passed, the arrow had come out at the root of his hip.
And I saw a woman upon whom an arrow had fallen into her belly, and the wound over it had been closed, and the arrow remained within her: and there was not changed anything in her conditions, and the woman did not find any harm from any of her natural functions.
And I saw another man upon whom an arrow had fallen into his face: and the wound was closed, and it remained, not finding much pain for him: and similar things to these many.
And I extracted an arrow from a man who was one of the king's princes, which had fallen into his middle nose, and it had already turned to the right side a little: and the arrow was completely hidden.
I was therefore called to his cure after the fall of the arrow for three days. I found then the wound of the arrow very tightly closed.
I therefore questioned it with a fine probe, and I did not feel it: and he indeed was finding a puncture and pain under his ear on the right side. I therefore hoped that the puncture was from the end of the arrow.
I therefore applied a dressing to the place with an ointment which had the virtue of attraction and digestion, trusting within myself that the spot would become inflamed, and I would see a sign of the arrow, so that I could cut over it: and no unfortunate event occurred in the place indicating that the arrow had followed it.
Therefore I applied a plaster on him for many days and an accident did not occur, and the wound was consolidated in the meantime. And he remained ill from the removal of it for a period of days until he felt the arrow one day inside his nose. And he told me that.
And he told me that. I therefore placed on that wound a sharp and corroding medicine for many days until it was opened. And when I had touched it with a probe and felt the tip of the arrow which was adhering to the wood.
Then I added through the opening of the wound the sharp medicine until it appeared visibly to us that tip of the arrow, and a period of time passed with him about four months.
Then after the wound had been enlarged and it was possible for me to insert forceps into it and move it, but it did not obey going out. Then I did not cease to refine and devise over it with the means of artisans until one day with well-made forceps, in such a way as their shape fits at the end of the handle, until I extracted it. Then I restored the wound.
And indeed the doctors judge that the cartilage of the nose is not restored. I therefore restored it and the wound was consolidated, and the sick person was healed with complete health, nor did anything bad happen to him at all.
And I tell you the quality of extracting some arrows, so that you can place a rule and explanation over what I did not say.
Since the parts of this art and their distinctions are not comprehended with a narration, nor is it comprehended by that book, and a wise craftsman compares little to much: and what is present over what is absent: and he finds a new operation, and a new instrument among the descending things of foreign matters of this art when they occur.
Therefore, I say that arrows do not emerge from the limbs in which they are fixed, unless according to two modes. Or by means of attraction from the place from which it entered. Or from the opposite part of another one.
And that which emerges from where it entered is either an arrow going into a fleshy place and is attracted and emerges.
Which if it does not obey exit on the hour when it falls: then it is necessary that you let go of it for a few days, until the flesh around it has putrefied and its attraction has been eased, and its emergence facilitated.
And similarly if it is fixed in a bone and does not obey you to emerge, then let it go also for a few days; and then try it with attraction and movement every day, until it emerges.
Which if it does not obey you to emerge after days, then it is necessary that you make a perforation around the arrow in the bone itself from every part with a fine awl, until the arrowhead is enlarged. Then draw it out and extract it.
If, however, the arrow is already hidden within the body's location and is concealed from sense perception: then seek it out with a probe. If, however, you feel it, then pull it out with some instruments which suit its extraction.
If, however, you are not able to pull out over it, because of the constriction of the wound and the length of the arrow in depth, and there is no bone, nerve or vein: then dig down over it, until the wound is enlarged and it becomes possible to extract the arrow.
And if there are ears in which it is stuck, then free the fixed flesh in them from all sides, by whatever means you can, and make use of instruments, if it cannot be pulled out over the evasion of flesh in the fracture of the ears and their twisting, until it comes out.
And you when administering the extraction of the arrow, in whatever place it is, then make use of twisting with your hand, with forceps to all parts, until you make it come out: and with subtle subtlety, so that the arrow itself does not break and its extraction becomes difficult for you.
And if you are unable to pull it out from one hour, then leave it for several days, until the things that are around it become putrid. Then proceed to pull it out again; for it will be more easily done if it has become softened. If, however, the extraction causes bleeding, then administer what we said in its cure in this chapter: and take care of your virtue from an incision of a vein or nerve or cord.
And administer the means of extracting the arrow in every way possible that it becomes easy and tranquil, or slow and firm, just as I told you.
And it is necessary that you do it as the patient is weakened, according to the figure, just as he was at the moment of the arrow's insertion. Since it is more useful. But if this is not possible for you, then administer as much as you can from what is available in forms.
But the arrow which comes out from the opposite part of another, either has already proceeded something to the outside: or makes a swelling at the end of the arrow by sense from the upper part of the skin nearby, and you see it prominent: therefore cut above it, and let the incision be according to the size that the forceps enclose in it.
Then draw it out: for indeed it becomes easy to come out.
But if it is retained in the bone, then twist your hand according to the roundness, until the arrow bites into the bone and enlarges itself there: then draw it out.
And if not, leave it for a few days: then repeat it, until it comes out.
But if the wood of the arrow is in it, then push it along with it.
But if the wood has fallen and you wish to administer a push: then insert into it an instrument curved like a shell, so that its concavity may enter into the tail of the arrow: then push it along with it.
But if the arrow is curved, then push it out with the instrument entering into it. For the arrow is alleviated by that.
But if the arrow is poisoned, then it is necessary, so that you concave the entire flesh, into which the poison has entered: if this is possible for you. Then treat it with what suits that.
But if the arrow is falling in the chest, or in the belly, or in the bladder, or in the side: and is close to what you touch with a probe, and it is possible for you to cut over it: then cut, and beware of an incision into a vein, or nerve, and draw it out.
Then if you see a wound and heal it until it is well.
And this is the form of forceps with which arrows are extracted.
Their extremities should be similar to a bird's beak which is made of iron: when they take hold of the arrow, they do not let it go.
And they are made in many shapes, large and small, and medium-sized, according to the size of the arrow's magnitude, its slenderness, and the width and constriction of the wound.
The shape of a concave plunger.
And the shape of a deaf plunger.
This is because of the deaf extremity like an arm to ease its entrance into a concave arrow and it is propelled with it.
On venesection or bloodletting.
The veins for venesection which have run their course in the body are thirty.
Of which there are sixteen in the head: two pulse-veins, which are behind the ears and called Two Herbs: and two arteries which are apparent in two temporal regions: and two veins which are in both tear ducts of two eyes, which are called Looking ones: and the vein that is prepared in the middle of the forehead: and the vein that is at the end of the nose. And the Two Guides, which are in the neck. And two veins which are on the upper lip of the mouth. And two veins which are on the lower lip. And those are the veins which are called Algecherich. And two veins which are under the tongue.
The veins that are let in the arms, are five in every arm.
One of them is Cephalica, and it is on the side of the elbow: and the common people call it the vein of the head. And the Bicuspid. And there is the Median vein, and it is made up of a branch of the Basilica and a branch of the Cephalica: and the common people call it the vein of the body. And the Basilica, and it is placed on the inner side: and it is also called the Titillicaris. And the common people call it the vein of the belly. And the vein of the arm, and it is placed above the elbow: and it is that one in which bloodletting is done, and it is that one which appears above the wrist with a very clear appearance.
And the Saluatella: and it is the vein that is between the Annular and the Minimus: and it has two branches.
And on the leg and on the foot there are three veins. One of them is that which is under the curve of the ankle from the side of the elbow. And the second one is the Saphena: and its place is at the heel from the inner side. And the vein Nesse: and its place is at the heel from the outer side.
And on the other leg there are three veins similar to those.
About bloodletting from two veins behind the ears.
The bloodletting from the two veins behind the ears is a remedy for old catarrhs, and hemicrania, and scabbing, and bad old ulcers of the head.
And the quality of bloodletting from it is according to what I say. And it is, that the head of the infirm person is scraped: and his occiput is rubbed with a rough cloth in the place of the two veins with good friction. Then the patient tightens his neck with a cord until two veins appear. And their places are behind the ears, in two depressed places on the head. Therefore seek them with your finger and where you feel their pulse under your finger, mark that place with pitch.
Then take a phlebotomy knife, and it is called an Incisive one. Then insert it under the vein on the skin, until the phlebotomy reaches the bone. Then lift your hand with the vein and skin upwards, and cut the vein with a cut of the skin cutting it. And let the length of the incision be the measure of two joined fingers, or about that much: and let blood flow out as much as you will.
Then bind it with lint, and leave it until it heals.
And already before the beginning of this book was their cutting and also their cauterization described.
The two arteries which are in the two temples' regions of phlebotomy bring benefit for an old headache, and a bad Sodam, and an obstinate Ophthalmia, and a current flow of excessive and sharp humors to the eyes.
And the quality of bleeding them is according to what I tell you. The patient tightens his neck much, until two veins appear to the sense as manifestly apparent: and it should be shown that their pulse is felt under your finger. Then mark with pitch.
Then lift the skin on top of the vein upwards with your index finger, and insert the cutting instrument beneath it, and lift the vein upwards. And cut her, as you did to the other two veins, and let blood flow out according to your own necessity.
Then loosen the constriction of the patient, and place your finger on the vein for one hour. Next, place a poultice and compresses on it, and bind it with strong constriction from above; and leave it until it heals.
And now there follows the remembering of those things, and their incision, and their extraction at the beginning of the book.
The remedy for venesection of the vein of the forehead is after cephalic venesection, for old pains in the face: and for half-hysteria, and redness: and foul ulcers.
And the quality of its venesection is, according to what I tell you. The patient constricts his neck with a bandage until the vein appears. Then take the instrument which is called a Dore.
Place the sharp point which is on the head of the Dore over the vein, and strike upon it with a spatula, or with some other thing in its manner; and let the blood run according to the quantity that you wish. Then loosen the perforation of the patient, and bind it until it heals.
And sometimes it is opened with a wide lancet. However, it should not be a sharp-edged venesection, like the other venesecutions, but rather one with a wide edge. And venesection is done through it according to the opening: because the bone is close and perhaps the venesection will be broken when it is fine.
The remedy for the two veins which are in the two tear ducts of both eyes is for an eye from scabies and redness: and inflammation in the eye, and pains in the face.
And the quality of their bloodletting is, that they straiten a sick man's neck with a cord. Then let them do a bloodletting on her: and you be standing over her head.
And let the bloodletting be done across from front to back somewhat, with a small lancet, not very wide, for there is no flesh in that place. For if the bloodletting is too fine, perhaps it will break through.
Then let it flow out of the blood as much as you need. And place on her a poultice, and bind her for one night, and then release her.
The veins of the nose being bloodletted is beneficial against acute fever and severe heat, and also troubles of the face such as headache and redness that occurs on the face, especially when it is old.
And the quality of this bloodletting is, that a sick man should straiten his neck with a cord. Then take hold of her nose with your left hand and grasp a thin lancet, long, and place it in the middle of the cartilage itself, between that which distinguishes the nose according to straightness. For there the vein does not appear to sight and because the blood begins to flow at once.
And it is necessary that you leave your hand with the lancet a little while and make flow out of the blood according to your need. Then bind her for one night. For she is restored quickly.
The benefit of bloodletting on both temples is for constriction of panting, and the beginning of leprosy, and troubles of melancholy, and scabies and bad ulcers, and eating corrotted things, that is, sores in the privates.
And the quality of venesection for them is that the sick person binds himself under both on his neck with a bandage: and the surgeon stands over the head of the sick person, and the sick person sits on a stool.
Then the venesection should be made to the length of the broad venesection for a short time. Then draw out from the blood a quantity tempered, or according to what necessity is present at that time. Then do similarly with the other vein.
Afterwards untie the bandage, and bind together the two veins with a moderate tightness: so that the sick person may not become overheated. And leave her until morning. For indeed the wound heals.
About venesection of veins, from Algeberich. And indeed the veins of Algeberich are beneficial for venesection: because they bring relief to the throat in the mouth, and corruption of the gums: and bad ulcers: and fissures of the lips, and bad ulcers, also which are in the nose, and about it.
And the quality of venesection for them is that you cause the sick person to sit before you: and bind his neck with a cord. Then turn back his lip and look at the two veins: of which one is on the right side of the lip, and the second on the left of him: and demonstration of both is by their blackness. And it is true that around them are small veins, black. Therefore cut both with a cut of piercing.
But if doubt is over you, and you do not know which of them it is: then look towards the cut of the larger of them, and the more apparent one.
And similarly do for the two veins which are in the upper lip: and very much customarily does it go with him: and cut the two veins which are in the lower lip.
On bleeding: The veins under the tongue after cephalic venesection. The utility of the bleeding of the two veins which are under the tongue: that is, the cephalic venesection is for obstructions that are in the throat, and affliction of the uvula, and ailments of the mouth.
And the quality of bleeding them is that the patient sits between your hands in front of the sun, and elevate his tongue, and look under his tongue from one side to see a vein, and on the other side to see another vein, and the color of each declivity to blackness.
So then bleed both of them, and be careful not to strike your hand too strongly when bleeding them. For under these are arteries, and perhaps it will happen that a flow of blood will come out from those arteries.
The three veins which are bled at the elbow are those of whose bleeding a custom has run in all men.
And their bleeding is done in two ways. Or by puncturing with a broad lancet of myrtle wood, or slitting to the thinness of the declivity, or by cutting with an instrument of small knife and is called Alnesil. And this is its shape.
And among them are the broad and thin types according to the width of the veins and their constriction. The form of a lancet of myrtle wood is broad, as you see. And it suits for opening veins that are concave, full, advancing, visible, thick, and those which contain thick, turbid blood.
And this lancet is of lesser width and thinner in the extremities.
And it suits for bleeding the veins that are thin, which contain clear blood.
However, one of these three useful venesections is that of the temple. Because it takes blood out of the sicknesses that are under the throat and neck, because they follow the chest and belly: and it is fitting for a venesectionist to beware of it, and be on guard from it, since there is an artery underneath. Therefore if he errs, and adds to the venesection cut, he will strike that artery, and a flow of blood will occur. Therefore it is necessary to let the person being venesected cut for himself in this place, or let him be cut by a barber.
But if the Basilica does not appear in a clear appearance, then it is necessary either to leave it and pass on to another place, or to seek out one of its branches; or to make a venesection at the bend of the arm. For it is clearly visible and should be cut by a barber-surgeon such as Alnesil, just as we said.
But if you wish to make a venesection of it itself, then before stretching out the arm, you should touch the place until you know the place of the pulse. Then mark the place with encaustum, afterwards stretch out the arm, and cut the vein with a concave sectioned cutting instrument with barber-surgeon Alnesil, just as we said. And be careful that the stroke not fall through the space from the place of the artery.
And when you see that with you tightening the bandage there is an inflation in the place which you marked with encaustum, then that inflation is the inflation of the artery; therefore avoid it.
But if you see blood rising in the venesection like a child's urine, and the blood is fine and red: then know that it is from the blood of the artery. Then therefore hasten, and place your finger upon it in a wide space, then take away your finger. Therefore if blood is cut off (for it is often cut off) then bind the arm and let him go; and may the patient beware of its movement; and be over his fear.
And do not move it for some days until it heals.
But if blood is not cut off and you are overcome: and medicine is not present to you at that moment, then cut the artery, if it appears to you. For its extremities are contracted, and blood is cut off.
Or take the skin of a ligature and sew it, take half one and bind it over the place of the vein strictly constricted, with a cord and lint until another day.
Then if blood is cut off, it is good. And if not: then treat him with the same remedy as that which precedes from the place of dusts cutting off the flow of blood: and cutting off his blood is not difficult in many dispositions, because of the small size of the wound and the possibility of binding from the arm.
But the benefit of bleeding the dark vein is that it draws blood from the upper part of the head and lower body; therefore, because it is composed of a branch of the basilic and a branch of the cephalic, as we said.
And it is suitable for the phlebotomist to be over his fear because of its bleeding, for under it is a nerve. Therefore if the phlebotomist adds to the wound of bleeding and finds a nerve, it happens in that place a numbness, and its healing becomes difficult, and perhaps it does not heal at all.
And this nerve is often felt by the sense. If it however is hidden in certain people and is subtle and does not show itself: then you must put your cupping glass so that you may suck out the bad humors, a cut with aloes and scrape the nerve with your strength.
But if the vein is between two nerves, then find the vein along its length.
Of the Head Vein. The bloodletting of the head vein is beneficial, because it draws blood from the head and relieves sufferings of the eyes.
And it is necessary in this vein itself, if you wish, to bloodlet from it with a gold-tipped lancet, or with a myrtle-tipped lancet: because it is the most safe of all veins, since there is no artery beneath it nor nerve.
However it suits you well to use a cupping glass at the bloodletting, so that you scratch with it only as far as the head of the finger and look for a softer place: and it does not harm him if it is not found with the first stroke, in order to be done over it again with the cupping glass several times.
However perhaps an abscess will form on some people if they are not bloodletted in the first stroke, but it does no harm to them that abscess.
Of the utility of Bloodletting and the precautions to be observed in it. However, the quality and incidents of Bloodletting from which you should think about how to remedy them.
First therefore of this is that you should know, that bloodletting is not administered unless for the preservation of health and its perseverance, and caution from the advent of illnesses.
And that bloodletting is done in one of the three veins in the elbow, namely the Head vein, and the Black vein, and the Basilar vein. And that bloodletting is done at the beginning of Spring on fasting, in spring and summer: and autumn and winter after digestion, when signs of repletion appear.
And let phlebotomy be done on the first day, or on the third, after three hours have passed since sunrise.
But the phlebotomy that is administered for illnesses does not have a fixed time; but when necessity and convenience call for it, either at night or by day, and at any hour and at any time.
And boys should not be let blood until they have come to fourteen years of age. Nor should a pregnant woman be let blood except from the fourth to the seventh month. And also not those who have passed sixty years of age should be let blood.
Therefore when someone wants to be let blood for any reason: it is necessary that he cleanse his bowels beforehand with a gentle clyster, if there is much dung retained in them; so that they may not draw the veins during phlebotomy towards their superfluous and putrid contents which are harmful to the main limbs.
And neither should a patient be let blood who is sated with nausea, nor drunk, nor fleeing from drunkenness, until that has been removed from them. Nor the weak with dropsy.
And let phlebotomy be avoided again after the passion of colic, and vomiting, and flow of the belly, and multiplication of coitus, and labor, and exercise, and vigils, and fasting, and all that dissolves virtue from bodily or animal substance.
Then consider the subtlety of the humors before it, if blood is thick with food and drink, and medicines: if it is possible to him.
Then let him enter a bath, if nothing prevents him: or exercise in some manner, so that the blood may be thinned.
And place his phlebotomy on the chest, around the third part of the day, as we said: and attempt to have him be emptied that day from all bad animal accidents, such as constriction, and anger, and fear: and from all bodily accidents, such as labor, and pain, and superfluities, and those similar things.
And let him sit in his presence those things with the use of which custom has run in men, by virtue of their aromatics, and odors, and festivities, and similar things to them, as much as possibility allows.
Then let the phlebotomist sit on a cushion higher than that of the patient who is to be bled: afterwards let him take out his arm and rub it with his hand twice or thrice. Then let him tie the ligature with a cord, and wrap it around twice.
And let the constriction be moderate, because a constriction that is uneven, or because of excess in the constriction, then prevents the flow of blood: or in relaxation, then also prevents the flow of blood.
Then after the constriction it is necessary that the one to be bled should rub his hands together until the veins are swollen and become apparent to the senses.
Then let the phlebotomist wipe the area to be bled with a little old oil properly.
Then let him place the tip of his left index finger on the vein under the spot where he wants to make the bleed, slightly so that the vein does not flee and the blow may be struck. Because veins are like a string that flees from the phlebotomist's touch: and they are full of blood-vessels: and when you place the phlebotomy instrument on them, they contract under it and deceive the phlebotomist, and the phlebotomy does not open the vein: and if it opens it, it is constricted: therefore it is necessary that the phlebotomist be firm and careful in all these things.
Then let him put down the phlebotomy instrument. If therefore the vein is opened from one side, then it is well. And if not, then do it the other way round under that place a little, or above it with haste: if the spot does not suppurate.
If an abscess forms, or if the patient is inflamed, then leave him for a day, or two days: and do not tighten the bandage. Perhaps indeed the abscess will attract heat. Nor should he enter a hot bath.
Then let him be bled again, if it helps.
If the opening is small or the blood flows thinly and you fear that not enough will come out: then do the bleeding again into the very same spot, according to what is right, and add a little more in the procedure.
And do it with haste, before the place becomes an abscess, for a place that is small during the operation often turns into an abscess.
Therefore if you see it already become an abscess, then do not bleed on it at all, for nothing excuses you. And put something from the fat of oil on it, because it will ease the flow of blood. And it is better in this place than oil itself, and than other oils.
And similarly administer the fat of oil in every bleeding of yours, by which you bleed veins against the flow of blood: and they are called tyriaca alfarach and sagzenea, when it is put from one of them over the place.
For the blood becomes thinner and is resolved, when it is thick.
If it happens that a great abscess forms at the place of bleeding, for it often happens, especially to those who are not bled except in that way, or whose vein is small and open, then hurry and put a sponge soaked in warm water and salt over the place, and bind it tightly for an hour: because it will dissolve.
And you must do that after the blood has come out of the vein itself, with its completion, or from another vein.
If something of blackness or greenness remains in the place after a day, it does not harm; and if you wish, put something of aloes, or myrrh's solvent over it: or something from mint juice, that is, calaminth, or similar to it.
And many times indeed abscesses and swellings occur among those who are let blood at the church.
Therefore place your hand on it, then if you find softness at the pressure upon it, then that swelling is bad. Be careful therefore, to place something over it from what we said. For perhaps blood will flow out of her from the artery.
But make a plaster for him with that in which is pungency, so that the place may harden. Then care for him with the rest of the treatment, until he be healed.
And it is necessary that you let out blood from every man according to the quantity of his strength. And what appears by the color winning over the blood.
For blood if it is black, then let it out until it becomes red: and similarly if you see it to be thick, then let it out until it thins: and similarly if it is sharp, until its sharpness is taken away.
And it is necessary for him who is full, strong, and needs blood letting once, that the venesection be increased on the other side, and thus he may be a left-handed bleeder: and contrariwise for the weak one.
And it is necessary that he let it out in turns, and that the opening be narrow: and better what is administered in venesection is, that it be a cross-cutting incision, not a puncture.
And this method of venesection is safe from blood flow and cutting of the nerve: and it is more praiseworthy and safer than venesection by breadth and length.
And to him who is accustomed to syncope at venesection, it is necessary that he eat before venesection a little bread dipped in grape juice water: or chicory, if he is heated: and let out from his blood in three turns, or four.
And if the complexion is chilled, then let him take before bloodletting a bread dipped in a honey syrup, or in a honey syrup flavored with spices, or in an aromatic syrup that smells.
Which if it happens during bloodletting and is the cause of the exit of much blood: then he must take in drink a broth of meat, and a subtle aromatic syrup, and use aromatics with aloe, and anoint his chest with them: and use the other things which we said in division in the chapter on syncope from evacuation.
But he who wants to succeed in cutting his arm and letting out his blood a second time: it is necessary for him whose bloodletting is because of great evacuation, and whose strength is weak, that he let out the blood gradually by the amount of his strength over continuous days.
Whoever however wants to cut his arm and let out his blood a second time, and his body is strong: then he should do it after seven hours or nine from his first bloodletting.
Whoever however wants to draw blood from his body to the opposite side to which it declines: it is necessary that he succeed on the second day, or on the third.
In whose body there is much blood already warmed and made acute, and makes a fever occur: it is necessary that the blood go out from him on one side, and a great quantity be drawn from him: and the opening be enlarged until syncope occurs: after you have been preserving all the conditions of bloodletting: and as you put your hand over his pulse at the course of blood, so that death does not occur in place of syncope. For indeed it often occurs when the bloodletter is unaware and carelessness falls.
And it is not necessary when you want to cut the arm and let out the blood a second time, with the vein already closed and the exit of blood made difficult, that you press on it with violence, and stretch it curved quickly. For that is a great evil, nay, abandon it until it be bled a second time.
Or remove with the sharpness of a lancet that which is coagulated from blood in the opening of the vein, and place over it something dissolved salt in water. And put over it something from Tyrian crocus or saffron, and put it under a gentle compression until the blood goes out.
If however there is now an abscess in the vein, then abandon it and do not bind it until the abscess has subsided.
Which if necessity calls for letting out blood a second time, and there is no excuse, then either let the blood from above that place or in another arm in another vein.
However the sinew of the arm is let out at the place of the Basilica and Fuscæ, when these are not found and are hidden, because it is compounded from them.
And the quality of its bleeding is, that the patient puts his hand into warm water until it reddens with heat and the vein becomes apparent.
Then let a bandage be wrapped over it with a thread in moderate constriction. Then let the bleeding be done across in a gradual manner, not widthwise nor along its length: and let the opening be wide, and let your bleeding be over the join of the hand for a little while.
Which if the outlet of blood is hindered, then repeat with your hand into warm water and let the blood flow into the water until you achieve what is necessary for you.
If however it be days of summer, then excuse yourself from repeating your hand in warm water. And indeed the flow of blood is put into warm water most frequently in winter time.
And the bleeding of this vein is safer for all veins, since there is no pulsating artery under it, nor a nerve.
The art of bloodletting with the right hand is the relieving of afflictions related to the liver.
And the quality of this bloodletting is that you tie a band or strip on your hand with a cord, after you have put it into hot water until the vein swells and becomes very sensitive. Then perform the bloodletting by crossing it over. And if you cut it completely, it will not harm him at all.
And take care, so that your hand does not become sore from the bloodletting. For under it are the naked nerves of the fingers stripped bare of flesh.
Then repeat the hand in the hot water and let the blood flow from it. For if you do not repeat the hand in hot water, the blood will congeal in the vein's opening and prevent it from flowing.
When therefore you have extracted the necessary quantity of blood, then place oil and salt on the vein so that it does not clot quickly.
And similarly it is necessary to do with every tightly bound branch.
The ointment of the bloodletting from the left hand is because it relieves afflictions of the spleen and similarly do with this bloodletting as you did in the second equally.
The ointment of the great vein bloodletting is for afflictions in the lower part of the body, such as womb diseases and retention of menses, and infirmities of the kidneys, and old ulcers of the hips and legs and similar ailments.
And the quality of this bloodletting is that the infirm person puts his foot into hot water and makes a friction on it until the veins swell, and ties above the ankle with a cord. And indeed the place of the veins is near the heel, appearing towards the thumb, and they branch out from it into the face of the foot many branches.
Venesection therefore should be done on a larger branch of it, or near the heel in its gathering; for it is better and safer.
If venesection be done on the face of the foot, then beware of the nerves which are beneath it over the face of the foot.
And place your venesection in a direction across it, as if you were cutting it through, and the venesection should be incised.
But if outlet of blood is prevented, then go on to soak his foot in warm water, and let the blood run into it until it be emptied.
But if the surgeon makes a mistake and misses the vein on the first try, then repeat the venesection somewhat higher up, for the place is safe, and you do not fear evil from it, when you beware of nerves, as we said.
And similarly with the vein on the other foot, which is equal to the saphenous vein.
