But the location of the sciatic veins, as we said, is near the heel on the outside of the lateral part.
And the benefit of venesection in that place is for pain of the ankle, when it is because of hot blood.
And the quality of venesection in that place is, so that the infirm person hastens into hot bath and quickens; and bind his leg on the side of the ankle up to the heel with a long thin strip, because it only appears because of that.
Therefore when it appears, then venesection should be done according to whatever method is possible for you, or across it; and it is better, either by cutting it with an incision, or by taking it out with a section. For the place of that vein is safer and itself is very much hidden in many men.

Concerning wind [gas] inflation, and its proper use.
Wind inflations are made from horns, and from air, and from vitreous [substance].
And wind inflation is indeed done in two ways. With scarification and bloodletting: and the other is wind inflation without scarification.
And this wind inflation which is without scarification is done in two ways. Or it is with fire. or it is without fire.
And wind inflations that are administered with scarification and bloodletting are fourteen places of the body. Of which one is the wind inflation of Alnocrati, and it is at the back of the head: and Alkehel, and it is in the middle of the neck: and wind inflations of Alhagdahim, and they are two plates of the neck from both sides together: and wind inflations of the mind, and it is under the jaw in the lower part of the face: and wind inflations of the spatulas, and they are at the elbow joints: and wind inflations of Alachosos above the end of the tail: and wind inflations of the two focii, and they are in the middle of the two arms: and wind inflations of the two legs: and wind inflations of the two heel-bones.
And indeed wind inflation does not extract blood from you from small scattered veins in the flesh, and because of that its power is not present in them, as is the case with bloodletting. Nor is it necessary to administer wind inflation, whether with fire or without fire, for any kind of sickness that arises from fullness, until the whole body has been emptied.
But if necessity calls for wind inflation due to illness, or because of habit, we administer it at every hour at the beginning of the month, at its end, and in its middle, and at whatever time may be.
Which is this: because from men there is who when he multiplies in him blood, until he needs its extraction through venesection, finds a heaviness in his head, and jaundice.
And from them is he who findspletion and redness in his face, on his head, and on his neck. And from them is he who finds itching in his face and forehead, and cloudiness and dullness in their eyes.
And from them is he who scrapes the place of venesection of himself. And from them is he whose laughter multiplies. And from them is he who finds the taste of blood in his mouth: or his tongue has become infected and spits up blood. And from them is he whose sleep multiplies. And from them is he who sees blood and redness, and the slain, and wounds, and things similar to these in his sleep.
When therefore we see something from these and properly if it is in the third part of the month: we then prescribe venesection to be done after two hours or three have passed since daybreak.
The juice of venesection from the neck is: because itself brings to heaviness in the head, and what is poured out to the eyes. But however it ought to be that it is after evacuation of the whole body.
And this indeed is a venesection in place of phlebotomy of the head.
And it is not allowed for him to use it who has a cold constitution, or who has a running nose. For it brings him great harm.
And because of that it is not fitting for old men, and for those in whose head are cold sicknesses.
But he who makes himself frequent with this generates forgetfulness over him.
And therefore it is necessary for you to order a Ventosari to place his hand with the ventosa a little at the lower parts, fearing from the generation of forgetfulness.
Ventosatio or Alkehel is a place for dark bleeding and basilical bleeding. And because of this it cures the hiccough and constriction of breath, and instrumented hiccup and coughing, and repletion.
And it is necessary to push the ventosia alkehel a little, because if it is placed lower down, it generates weakness in the heart and stomach.
The ventosia alhagdahim indeed cures pains occurring by accident in the head, and eye pain, and headache, and perforation, and tooth root pain; and it is a place for basilical bleeding.
And it is necessary to order the ventosari not to press his hand with a scarification so that he may not touch an artery and there occur a flow of blood.
The ventosia however under the chin cures ulceration in the mouth and corruption of the gums and similar ailments which are in the mouth; and it is a place for alieherich bleeding which occurs in both lips.
The ventosia however of the spatula cures alchafacham which is caused by constriction and heat.
The ventosia however of the internal parts of two cup-like formations cures the benefit of bleeding from the three veins, Basilicae, Fuscae, and Cephalicae: because it attracts blood from all those small veins which are in the flesh; and those small veins attract blood from other larger veins until the attraction reaches the three veins.
And it is necessary to order the ventosari not to proceed too much in scarification because the place is stripped of flesh and beneath it are nerves and arteries.
A type of blister (ventosa) is applied over a swelling (alchausos), it benefits hemorrhoids of the anus and lower ulcers.
And you should order the ventosas to be large and made of air: because the place needs strong suction, and perhaps the thin ventosas break: and let them be punctured with a large incision.
The ventosas of the legs decrease (the swelling) by clear diminution: because they draw blood from the whole body and bring relief to old pains in the kidneys, womb, and bladder: they provoke menstruation, and bring relief to boils, and fistulas: and stand as a place for phlebotomy of the two saphenous veins, and for the opening of ulcers.
But they weaken the body much, and cause syncope to occur in many people.
And the benefit of ulcers of the ventosas is close to the benefit of ventosas on both legs.
The quality of the position of the ventosas is that you place the ventosa in a first vacuum and suck with temperate suction, and do not prolong the position of the ventosas, but quickly put them on and quickly take them off, so that the humors come to the location in equal arrival: and do not cease to repeat it and continue, until you see the place already red and inflamed, and the appearance of the redness of blood appears: then scarify and iterate suction gradually. Then consider the position of the body.
For those among men who have delicate skin, a few pores, it is necessary to scarify them once, not more: so that the location does not become ulcerated.
And order the ventosa-maker to amplify the scarification and make it a bit deep: and moderate the suction with ease, speed, and gentle motion.
If there is coagulation in the blood, then it is necessary to scarify them twice. In the first incision indeed there is opened up a path for the subtlety of blood and its fluidity: and in the second indeed an examination of the extraction of thickened blood.
If the blood is very feculent, then let there be a scarification in the third incision, so that the ultimate result may follow.
And as for the ultimate step, when we wish to draw out blood little by little or a bit at a time, one single scarification is sufficient for us. Which if we wish the extraction of blood in many places, let us make multiple scarifications.
And if we measure, that the blood is thick: then it behooves us, to make a deep scarification: and the limit equal in scarification, is the depth of the skin.
What that thing is which it behooves to be administered from oils at the placing of wounds, and what a ventosandus should beware.
In that one indeed whose skin is thick, hard, dark, and whose pores are tight, it behooves that the places of the wound to be anointed with oils which are openers, soothers, resolvents: if in the time of summer, as is oil of alkikeri, or oil of violatum, or oil of sweet almonds, or oil of cucumber seed.
And if it is in the time of winter, then oil of narcissus, or oil of lily, or cammomile oil, or sardinian oil: and similar things to these.
If indeed the superfluity is thick, cold: then oil should be bitter almond oil, or oil of balsamita, or white oil, or oil of anethion: and similar things to these.
If indeed the ventosand is of wide pores, tender flesh: then it behooves that he be kept from oil, and it behooves that those places of wounds be washed with rose water, or with cold water, or with solatri water, or with cucumber water, or with portulaca water: and similar things to these.
He, however, whose blood is of great humidity, shall wash the ulcers with vinegar, or with water of myrtle, and sumac, and similar things to them.
And he whose discharges are coarse, shall wash the ulcers with old wine, or with water of saponaria, or with a decoction of dill, or of chamomile, and similar things to them.
And it is necessary that ulceration be avoided in a bath, or in the water of a bath; nay, rather, it is necessary to administer after exiting from the bath for an hour, or for two hours.
And it is not fitting that someone should sleep after ulceration.
What is to be observed for the regulation of Ulcerative sores, or Bleeders before ulcers are opened.
AND after that it is necessary to observe first: then if Ulcerative or Bleeder be choleric, and have dominion over his blood sharpness and inflammation: then it is necessary for him to take cooling things, as the fruit of a pomegranate: and also endive with vinegar and lettuce, and centaury and juniper: and similar things.
And put cooling foods before him, such as eggs, and the flesh of young birds cooked well, and uncooked foods with wild herbs: and similar things.
And for whom the complexion is cold, it is necessary for him to take in drink a syrup made from honey, or from almonds, or from calaminth made from seeds: and he shall take wine that has a medium odor, which is between old and new: and it is commanded that he shall use moderation of food. And put before him food such as eggs, and canes, and sparrows, that is, small birds: and he shall eat the eggs of pigeons.
And it is necessary that the drink on the day of ulceration and bleeding should be more than the food: and it is necessary to give in drink to certain people in hours of sharp fever aloes or medicine made from musk, that is muscata, or alesselithe before ulceration: and before bleeding, or after, so that the main limbs may be strengthened and the blood thinned. And it should not be taken with a warmed drink.
On Fissures without Scarification. Those however which are without scarification, are those that are placed over the liver, and spleen, and podagram, and over the belly, navel, and places of the kidneys, and pixidem anchae; because these members do not endure scarification on themselves.
And inquiry is not made through them, unless an application of blood from one member to another: as when we place a Ventoza over the nipples in a disease of flowing blood from the nostrils. And we use them to dissolve from the member an uncleaned and cold effluvia on the member: as when we place a Ventoza over the belly and navel. For it itself rarefies the member, and warms it, and removes the pain therefore, because it resolves that effluvia.
And also it is placed over the kidneys, when there occurs a blockage or stone in them. For by the virtue of its attractive power it perhaps opens the blockage, or eradicates the stone from its place.
And similarly it does when placed over the liver and spleen for cleansing of an effluvia in both of them.
And these Ventozas are administered empty with a cut only. And sometimes they are administered with fire.
And sometimes they are administered full with warm water in cases of pleurisy; and that is, so that the Ventoza may be filled and be large, either with hot water alone or with water in which herbs suitable to it have been steeped.
The shape of a Ventoza administered with fire.
The size of the opening of it should be two fingers opened wide, according to how we have formed it. And its quantity in depth is half a palm: and there is on one side of it, at the place which is near its middle, a small hole, according to the size that the needle enters into it. It is made from air together, or citron, with thick smooth equal edges, so as not to hurt the member where it is placed upon it. And there is in the middle of it a cross-piece transversely from air, or iron, where the candle with fire is put.
And now this ointment is made great, greater than what we have said, and somewhat less so, according to the illnesses and age of him who uses it.
For the wind-swept boys and those with slender bodies are others than the wind-swept men and those of sturdy bodies.
But the quality of putting this ointment with fire on the body is, to set alight a linen wick or a small candle from wax and place it over the middle vein which is in the middle of the ointment: so that the fire may be ignited about the lower part of the ointment, lest the sick person's body be burned.
Then it is placed on the body and a finger is put over the hole which I said, until when we hold the ointment: while we are in need, we remove the finger, and steam will come out over that hole, and the ointment will dissolve immediately.
Then set alight the linen by fire in its own manner, and repeat it if you need to that one.
But in the ventosa which is administered in the pain of pleurisy with water, there is no transverse vein nor hole: and it is not administered, unless the water fills it, and it is placed only on the body.
And this is its form.
And as much as this ventosa is larger to take in a great deal of water, it is better.
About the suspension of Bloodlettings.
Bloodlettings are not administered in many cases, unless there is an impossibility of placing ventosas on account of their smallness, such as the lip and gum, and things similar to them. Or on a bare member from skin, such as a finger and nose, and those similar to them.
And the quality of using them is: that the leeches from the bloodlettings which are in sweet, clean waters free from putrefactions.
Then they are released day and night into sweet water, until they are famished, and nothing remains in their bellies. Then the body is evacuated first with bloodletting, and with emetics. Then cleanse the afflicted member until it becomes red. Then they are placed on top of it.
Therefore when they fill up and come out, and there is a possibility of drawing the place with an emetic, then it is most beneficial for recovery. And if not, then wash the area with vinegar. Then with much water, it is rubbed and squeezed out.
And if the flow of blood continues after a wound, and that is dripping from it, then pour a linen cloth into cold water, and place it on top, until the dripping stops.
And if blood multiplies, then powder thereto crushed zege or gall nuts, and similar things from stypses, until the blood stops.
Or place over the place halves of peeled beans, and let them go until they stick to the place, for the blood is stopped.
And it is necessary if a repetition of the purging is necessary, when possible, to change them.
But if leeches are prevented from suspending themselves, then the place is cleaned with fresh blood. Or stick a pin into the places until something comes out of the blood. Then they are placed there, for themselves when they feel something coming from the blood, stick right away.
Therefore when you wish them to fall, powder something over them from crushed aloe, or salt, or ash, and they will fall right away.
This chapter is often necessary in the art of Medicine, and it is the restoration of fractures and dislocations that have occurred in bones.
Know, sons, that the foolish doctors and common people boast about this Chapter, and those who have never looked at an old book, nor read a single letter from it.
And because of this reason, the method of treatment is concealed among our people. And I have never found in it anything good at all, and I did not attain that which I attained except because of the length of reading old books and diligence over understanding them, until I extracted knowledge from them.
Then I adhered to experimentation and diligent study for the length of my life.
And now I have described for you from it all that my knowledge contains in this Chapter: and beyond what has passed the test of my experiment, after I shortened it for you, and freed it from the burden of prolixity, and shortened it at the end of abbreviation, and showed it at the end of declination: and I have formed many shapes in it from the forms of instruments which are administered in it, since it is from the addition of declination, as I did in the two preceding Chapters.
Chapter II.
It is necessary, when saying the preface to this Chapter, to declare the summaries of speech and chapters, by whose understanding the artist is compelled in the first place to stand over their truth: that is, so that you and those who are ready may know or learn this noble art, and others through you.
I say therefore that when a fracture or dislocation or twisting or a fall occurs to someone, then it is necessary to rush first to bleed them and to dissolve the pain, or both at the same time: if nothing prevents this, such as weakness of strength: or if the person who has experienced something from these is a child or an old decrepit man: or if it is a time of intense heat or very intense cold.
Then let him be content with his food, cold vegetables, the flesh of birds and hares, and prohibit wine, and thick meats, and to fill himself from foods and all food that fills the veins with blood. Until when you are secure from the warm boil and do not fear a flowing of matter to the member, then the sick person shall return to his original regimen with which he was accustomed.
So when the broken bone begins to be restored, then it is necessary for the sick person to be fed nourishing foods with much nourishment, thick, heavy, and sticky; such as food made from boiled wheat with water, and roots, and heads, and feet, and stomachs of mussels, and eggs, and fresh fish: and strong wine, and similar things. For by this regimen the coagulation of the fracture is done more quickly and better.
And know that broken bones, when they are in the veins of those who are in the first old age and decrepitude, it is not possible for them to be continuously and firmly consolidated according to their own nature, because of the drying out of their members: and they are consolidated and continued which are at the end of calmness, as the bones of young children.
However, nature connects to the broken bone from all parts something similar to glue, with thickness, by which it adheres and binds itself, until they are stuck together and bound together, until it comes to an end of strength and firmness as was in the beginning, until nothing prevents it from operating: and because of this reason it is necessary for you to put nourishment into the sick person food-like substances, with heaviness and stickiness and thickness, such as we said.
And know that the species of fractures are diverse, according to the diversity of members: for a fracture of the leg is different from a fracture of the head of a bone: and a fracture of the chest bone is different from a fracture of the back bone: and similarly all the remaining members exist differently among themselves: and I come with the recollection of every species of fracture exposed in its own chapter, distinguished from another. And the species of fractures of a bone are differentiated also within itself.
For there is a fracture that is curved, and besides which happen to be splinters in it. And sometimes it happens that a fracture occurs along the length of the bone: and there are splinters from it, that is, shavings, and additions proceeding, just as are on a reed when a pen is sharpened for writing. And not proceeding; and it is a fracture with an open wound and disruption at the head; and it is a small fissure.
And every species has its own skill or tool for restoring it, according to the recollection of it which will come separately in its places.
And from what is known of a fracture of the bone, there is its curvature and its prominence, and its appearance to sense, and its sound when you press it with your hand.
So when the curvature is not appearing in place, nor does it make a sound to your touch when you touch the bone with agitation, nor does the afflicted person find great pain: then there is no fracture there, rather it may be that there is a curvature or small fissure. Therefore it is not necessary to move it entirely with your hand and compression, but rather place above it medicines of which the recollection comes after the moment that suits the place; then let a fine bandage be wrapped around it.
And know that the place when it is curved and split into two, besides which happen to be splinters in it, yet all part is removed from its fellow: then you must begin at your hour for its correction, equalizing it before a warm abscess comes upon it.
If however a hot abscess occurs in him, then leave him for several days until the abscess becomes quiet. Then apply it evenly to him by whatever manner is suitable and gentle.
And know that his restoration and its adjustment are easier than that of bones in which suppurations occur.
Then bind him tight as a reminder comes to you.
If indeed there are suppurations in the bone, then it is necessary to extend this broken limb from two parts, if a hand or foot: or with your own hand, if the member is small: or with two cords, or with a cord and a staff.
And let your position be such as you place the member over an equal place, according to its natural shape, until when the parts of the broken limb are stretched: then try to return all the additions with every ingenuity and in whatever way you can in their places: and beware, according to your strength, lest there occur on a weak person pain or hurt through your operation. And try, according to your strength, to apply one of two bones together with its fellow, according to better forms.
And it is necessary at that time for you to test and feel him with your own hand. If therefore you see something different there, correct it and make it even by the extent of your skill.
And beware a strong extension and forceful compression, as many foolish people do. For indeed they often cause a hot abscess or contraction in the member to occur through their operation, as we have seen many times from their operations.
Then connect after the adjustment, and decent operation, and tightening of that member, and rest, and tranquility: and warn the sick person to be careful with it. And have him avoid moving it during his waking hours and sleep at the time of its revolution and agitation, and at its emission and all its movements with his last strength. And so that he may be cautious, lest the position of the member be such as would follow with pain. Which is that when he feels pain or injury in the position of the member, then it behooves him to change it into another position from which he does not feel pain.
And beware with him, so that its position be straight and even: lest there occur twisting of the member when it is restored.
And the quality of the fracture of the bone is, as I say.
Know that fractured bones are differentiated by their smallness and largeness, and their shape.
Which therefore of them are small, such as the arm and finger and fist and similar to these, then it behooves that there be a wrapping of soft, thin cloth. Which however of them are large, such as the thigh and leg, it behooves that there be a wrapping around them of something wide and hard.
Because a wide bandage connects the large member and tightens it from all parts equally so that no bruise enters into it.
Then begin after your evacuation from equality to place a suitable poultice over the spot of the fracture itself, in soft flour. Then begin to wrap the bandage over the spot of the fracture itself three times, or four, according as the member deserves it: and bind your hand a little with the bandage.
Then go with him to the upper part from the place of the fracture and bind less than was your previous binding which bound the broken place. Then stretch out with the bandage from the place of the fracture a little and soften the binding gradually until you take hold from the healthy place something great.
Then take another cloth and wrap it again over the broken place with bandages. Then go with ligature to the lower part from the fracture. And let your operation be in tightening of the ligature and softening of it, according to what we said in the first ligature above.
Then place between the ligatures a soft sponge or cloth so that it matches the winding of the fracture, if there is winding in it; and if not, put nothing in it.
Then tie another bandage over it on top. Then apply over these ligatures stars made of cotton as soon as possible, if there is no swelling and no warm abscess.
But if there is swelling or abscess in it: then place over it what keeps that abscess down, and takes away the swelling: and leave it for some days. Then tie on top of it the stars again.
And let the stars be from the middle of wide reeds that are smooth and well-shaped: or they may be from wood where sieves are made which come from pine or geraid, that is, the branches of a palm tree, or calingi; and let the width of all the stars be according to this form and same shape.
But it is necessary that the star which is put over the break, be somewhat thicker and wider than the other stars: and the length of the star be according to as much as allows a limb by size and smallness.
Then tie over the stars with another bandage, according to the same first tightening. Then bind over with threads decently, according to what we said about tightening; and it is, that your tightening above the place of the fracture be more, and as much as it goes further away from the fracture, the tightening be less.
And it is necessary that the threads be middle in thickness and subtlety, and be of soft flax.
For if the threads are thick, as I have seen in the operations of foolish people who put their threads made from twisted linen strings, it is a great mistake: because the tightening falls off with them outside the quality.
And fine threads are not suitable again; because you cannot follow with them what you wish by tightness.
And it should not be less than a finger between one anchor and another.
But if the sick person is injured by the edges of the anchors after tightening in sound places: then put beneath them a piece of soft wool, or fluffed wool, until nothing is hurt from it.
But when there is a fracture with a wound, and a disruption occurs on the skin, I go up to him with remembrance of it in chapter two by singularity.
And know that not every broken member is to be tightened with anchors from the first day. For this reason is that a member when it is large should not be put on top of anchors unless after five days, or seven, or more, according to your security from the event of hot postemat formation.
And this is the method of plasters by which the ancients restored them, which are put over a fracture and dislocation.
A Method of Common Plaster abbreviated for restoring a fracture, suitable to many complexions, especially women and children, because it is something over which neither heat nor cold prevails.
And it is that you take from the flour mill dust, and it is the heart of the flour which hangs on the walls of the mill with the movement of the millstone: and you make it as it is, except that you sift it with egg white: and put its composition not thick, nor thin. Then use it.
A Method of Another Plaster for restoring a fracture, and dislocation, and twisting. Recipe aloes, and ladanum, and acacia, and enula, and myrrh, each of them a pound and a half of each. And the juice of all together ten ounces of myrrh, and five ounces of aloes each, and alethi, twenty pounds are crushed, sifted, and mixed with alethi water: or with egg white if the complexion of the sick is heated. Then use this plaster. For it is itself a good composition, quickly restores broken bones and brings relief to many people.
Another method of plaster that also cures fractures and sprains. Recipe equal parts of wax, honey and white almonds. Add the weight of 10 myrrh, 5 aloes, and 6 acacia together 20 Armenian boli. Grind all these with much pounding and sieving, and make them into a paste with water or egg white: and administer them.
Another method of plaster for joints and bones separated from their places: it soothes the pain that occurs to them, and the crack in the bone and fracture. Sheep's wool is taken, submerged in vinegar and boiled oil, and placed over the place.
And in this plaster there is no virtue of restoration, but it is good for soothing hot boils and properly driving out pains.
Another method of plaster that restores a broken bone. Take the leaves of mute figs and the leaves of wild poppy: and pound all together, and make a plaster from both.
Another brief method of plaster administered for the restoration of a fractured bone: and when you wish the resolution of the remaining boil. Recipe equal parts of the root of white plantain, camomile, violets and herb flour: grind all these together, and make them into a paste with wine, if the limb is not hot.
If it is heated, then make that plaster with moist coriander water, or with water, and use it.
Another stronger method of plaster for the resolution, than this first one, when a hard boil occurs in the restoration of a member. Recipe equal parts of the root of white plantain, flaxseed, balaustion, mellilot, hellebore, violets and camomile: grind all these together, and make them into a paste with willow water, or sweet water, or wine. The whole of that [treatment] is according to the warmth of the member and the quiescence of its heat.
Hippocrates indeed did not say in his book that anything should be put on a fractured member for its restoration, except the plaster made from wax and oil: nothing else; and he told that it should be midway between bulkiness and thinness.
However Galen it seemed was of the opinion that things should be put on a fractured member for its restoration in which is dryness of heat, such as myrrh and aloes and frankincense, and similar to these from those we have mentioned.
The method of the plaster that is used for injury and pain. Take barley and chickpeas and the hairs of human teeth or the feathers of a bird or an antelope, and equal parts of salt: they are to be ground and sifted and mixed together: and from it make a plaster.
However the quantity by which it is necessary that the ligature remain and then dissolve, you must consider thus: if no pain or itching befalling the sick person, nor does the fractured bone move from its place: then do not dissolve it in many days. But if it befall him in the place a severe itching or disturbing pain or swelling: then hasten and dissolve it in an hour itself, and remove the plaster from him: and take a soft cloth or a soft seaweed sponge moistened: and immerse it in warm water and wash the place with it until the itching ceases and the pain is soothed.
But if it befall him in the place a severe itching or disturbing pain or swelling: then hasten and dissolve it in an hour itself, and remove the plaster from him: and take a soft cloth or a soft seaweed sponge moistened: and immerse it in warm water and wash the place with it until the itching ceases and the pain is soothed. Then let the member rest for one hour. Then place on it a cloth that has been cut short, immersed in vinegar and oil or rose oil: and bind above it for one night, until you are secure from suppurative heat, and the swelling of the member is calmed down, and its pain is removed.
Then go back with him to a fine stricture, and apply a small poultice: and do not tighten the first constriction on him, and along with it, until he is healed.
And if you see that the pustule, and redness, and pain, and swelling, and all the accidents have been completely removed, and he is still in need of the poultice and constriction, then go back to it just as you did at first equally.
And if anything of what we said does not happen to the member, then do not loosen it, unless after three days, or four, or five, or seven. And when it is let go twenty days after that, the whole of it, according to what appears to you from the loosening of the member, as we said, until when the fracture adheres and coagulation of flesh approximates over it: add also then in the constriction more than your first constriction altogether: and add also at that time a thickening of food for the infirm, according to what has been remembered before.
But if you see the place of the fracture already dried up and thinned out more than it should be: then know that nourishment is prevented from reaching it. So bind him with warm water all over the whole place where you loosed it, and let that be for three days altogether, and ease the constriction a little.
By this operation therefore nourishment flows to the member, and it is healed quickly.
But that which foolish restorers do from the fracture of a member by twisting it into another place when it is not restored first as it should be and is restored according to the curvature, is an error from their operation and great fear: and if it were straight they would have said so in their books and operated with it.
And I nowhere saw any mark of theirs being done like that at all, and it is better not to do it.
Concerning the making of an incision in the head.
There are many species of fractures and their forms are numerous; and their causes are diverse.
For a fracture occurs from a blow with a sword: and the blow removes the bone entirely as far as it reaches to the siphac, which is under the bone, just as an axe does in wood; and because of this it is called axial fracture.
Or the sword cuts into something of the bone and cuts its surface but does not penetrate to the last part of it; and this kind of fracture is called kalaan, that is, a splitting.
And it is a wound either of two fractures, large or small.
And there is a fracture which occurs through crushing or bruising; and its cause is a blow with a stone, or a fall upon a stone, or things similar to these.
And this fracture is also either penetrating, which has almost reached the periosteum, which is under the bone; or it is on the surface of the bone; and it is a wound of these two fractures, either large or small.
And there is a fracture which is hidden in the bone in subtlety of hair; and it is a small split, and because of this it is called this kind of fracture capillary.
And from these there is a fracture which occurs from a fall or a blow with a stone and things similar to these, which make a plate of bone enter inside, and it forms a concavity in the place; just as happens with bronze vessels when they are struck and part of them enters inside.
And this indeed happens very much in the heads of humid bones, such as the skulls of children.
And all these kinds of fractures are forced and separated; and I have finished with the reminder of that whole thing in its own place.
And we know all these kinds of fractures by inspection over them, and inquiry with strings, and removal of the corrupted flesh that is over them.
The capillary species is known by this, that it is disclosed over the bone and scraped away, and smeared over it with wax: for indeed a fracture appears black.
However, in the cure of a fracture, consider first the accidents of the patient; then if you see from these accidents that they signify something manifest over fear, such as vomiting of bile, and diarrhea, and loss of intellect, and separation of voice, and syncope, and acute fever, and protrusion of eyes, and redness of them, and similar things from accidents in many conditions, without a doubt it is not cured: and if you see that the accidents do not trouble you, and hope for healing: then begin its cure.
Which is this, if he comes to you wounded on being wounded: and it is in the dead of winter, then you must strive to remove the bone before the fourteenth day according to every condition.
And if it is in the days of summer, then you must hurry to remove the bone before the seventh day, lest what is under the bone from a cloth becomes corrupted and those accidents occur which we have narrated.
Which if the bone fracture has already come to a cloth touching the brain, and is with smallness and adhesion: then you must cut out the broken and worn part, according to what I tell you.
And it is as if you were to rasp the head of the wounded person: and uncover over the bone in whatever manner is possible for you: and according to the shape of the wound, and what is light over the patient.
Which if there occurs to you on detecting the bone a flow of blood, or a warm suppurating spot: then apply to it whatever is necessary. And you should fill the place with cloths steeped in wine and rose oil: and cause the wound to be quiet until the swelling is stilled, and you are secure from the flow of blood. Then begin with the cure of a bone and its removal.
And it is done according to one or two methods.
The first method indeed is, that you cut the bone with a sharp-edged instrument called an "incisorium", and this is its shape.
Then give another incisorium, set somewhat wider than the first incisors, and this is its shape.
Then give also another incisorium, yet even wider than the second.
And the second method is, that you have a number of incisors of different kinds with some wider than others and some shorter than others, and having their extremities very sharp, and being made of iron or bronze: and use them easily in striking upon the bone so as not to shake the head and damage it.
But if the bone is hard and strong then it is necessary to bore into its circumference before you give incisors with Terebres that are called shallow Terebres: and they are not so named unless because they do not pass beyond the term of the bony part to what lies behind it; therefore, since the Terebre has a rounded extremity above which is under the sharp point, like its edge, and a small circle prevents it from being submerged and passing through the thickness of the bone.
And it is fitting for you to take a number of these Terebres, each one suiting the thickness of the bone, until there is present with you one Terebre for every part of the skull: and the sharpness of its pointed extremity should be in length and shortness according to the thickness of that part of the skull.
And this is the shape of three kinds of Terebres or Trephines.
The quality of piercing around a broken bone is such: that you place the drill on the bone and turn it with your fingers until you know the bone has been pierced. Then change the drill to another place and insert what is between all the holes in size of a finger or about that.
Then cut with a saw what is between all the holes from the bone; and make it finer than you can on the end of it because of its thinness, as we said, until you root out the bone either by hand or with some other tool which you have prepared for this: such as forceps and fine pincers.
And it is necessary that you beware of all caution lest the drill or saw touch anything from a piece of wood.
When therefore you have gone around the bone perforating and it has been separated from the wood, if it was continuous with it and you have extracted it, then you must rasp and make equal the extremity of what remains in the bone with another similar sawing instrument. However, it should be thin and finer than other saws.
And if something remains from small bones and you are striving then take it with facility along with what is at your hand from instruments. Then care for the wound afterwards with liniments and ointments which we mentioned.
And another mode of the operation is easy and far removed from fear which Galen said and praised with great praise and this is his saying. It is necessary for you at first to begin uncovering the part of the bone from the place in which the fracture is more violent and manifest: until when you have uncovered that part and placed under it the extremity of this lens-shaped saw whose shape is this.
And the part of the lens-shaped saw does not cut anything smooth; and the sharp point of it running along its two sides to a length as you see: so that the part of the lens-shaped saw is leaning against the wood with its pointed face striking against the bone.
Then strike with a hammer over the incisor bone from one side until you easily cut through the entire bone as it turns over: and be sure to use a cloth so that what you fear may not happen does not occur.
And if the physician were not more foolish than men and more ignorant of it, nor sleeping, then if something of the bone remained attached from a cloth in some places, he would cut it away with the edge of the incisor shell itself, and free it with ease. For indeed it is freed from it without harm, and without fear.
And if the bone fracture does not penetrate up to the cloth, and what is being cut away from the bone is its surface, and there remain in it roughnesses and fine scratches: then it is necessary that you rasp that roughness, and root out those scratches with fine rasps of which you have prepared a number of different quantities, so that it may be possible for you to apply them in every place more suitable and correct for them, according to what the operation leads you, and the fractured bone itself, and its shape. And it is necessary that you administer to yourself with a kind of rasp in your operation, by which at first you rasp the bone, more widely than those small rasps. Then use one after another even finer than that.
And it is necessary that you administer to yourself with a kind of rasp in your operation, by which at first you rasp the bone, more widely than those small rasps. Then use one after another even finer than that. And do not cease to do this in succession until you reach the point of administering something finer and subtler than all of them.
And in each of the small remaining cracks and fine fractures, it is necessary that you administer care, according to what leads to its correction.
And it is a thing that is not hidden from everyone who has any study or zeal for this art, and stood over what we wrote and showed in great fractures.
But if the cloth that is detected is retained when you remove the bone: then it is necessary for you to take a piece of linen according to the size of the wound, and immerse it in rose oil, and place it over the opening of the wound. Then take another cloth, doubled or tripled, and immerse it in wine and rose oil. And place it on the first cloth. Do this as easily as you can so that it does not weigh heavily upon the abscess.
Then apply over it a broad bandage, and do not tighten it, unless by as much as is sufficient to hold the wound; and leave it with this treatment for one day or two days, until you are sure of the warmth of the abscess. Then dissolve it and apply after that medicines with which there is excessive dryness: such as lily roots, and the flour of a plant, and powdered galbanum, and aristolochia, and similar things to these. And this is how you should make from these medicines a powder, which shall be sprinkled over the wound like the dry ones.
And universally apply to it all medicine of a property that is such as to clean and not irritate. And take care according to your virtue that the wound in your cure be clean; nor let there be in it the dirt of ointment or its filth.
And take care according to your virtue that the wound in your cure be clean; nor let there be in it the dirt of ointment or its filth. And do not let the pus accumulate in it at all; for when the pus accumulates on the cloth of the brain, it corrupts and festers it, and a great evil befalls the sick person after that. And it also happens in the head abscess, with the mouth being cleaned from it, especially when its cure is neglected, blackness appears on its surface.
Therefore consider then, if evils befall the sick person that we have said would happen, know surely that it is noxious beyond doubt.
And if blackness does not occur except from the medicine that is put on it, and it is in the virtue of that medicine to make that blackness: then you must take one part of honey, three parts of rose oil, and mix with them a cloth. Then place it over the abscess: afterwards treat it with remedies and cure-all devices that are suitable, until it heals.
On the restoration of the nose when it is broken.
Know that the nose is not broken except for two of its upper parts together, or one of them, because it consists of two bones: since the lower part of it is cartilaginous, it does not break and an injury to it occurs only by bruising, twisting, and deformation.
If one of the two parts of it is broken, then you must put a little finger into the nostril and equalize that fracture with your index finger and thumb outside, until the nose returns to its natural shape and this is done artfully and easily. And beware lest there occur through your operation that excessive pain in it.
And if the fracture is in the upper part of the nose and you cannot reach it with your finger: then it must be equalized with the end of a small wooden stick.
And if indeed the fracture is in both parts, then do the same thing and proceed with its restoration on the first day of the fracture, if possible. And if not, then after seven or ten days at rest from the inflammation.
Then insert into the extremity of the nose a linen strip from flaxen cloth, if the fracture is in one part: or insert two strips if the fracture is in both parts: and let the thickness of the linen be such that it fills the nostril.
And some ancient restorers have said that linens should be steeped in butter and changed every day. That however does not seem to me, rather it is necessary for the linen to be steeped in egg whites, mixed with mill dust. Then release the linen until the bone has firmed up and the cartilage has hardened.
And sometimes it intervenes on the nose with a feather of goose down, after which is wrapped over it a soft cloth: and this happens, that it holds the break of the nose more firmly, and so the sick person is not prevented from breathing.
And this thing is not necessary. If you wish, do it; and if you wish, make a licina.
If the nose happens to break again while you are treating a hot abscess, anoint the nose with cerot or with a small amount of cotongue infused in rose oil, or a moderate amount from dragon's blood.
But if a hot abscess does not happen, then it is necessary to make an ointment of the same with sesame flour and powdered frankincense made with the white of an egg. Then place a soft poultice over it, and do not tie the nose at all.
But if the bones of the nose are broken in a small fracture, or crushed, then it is necessary to split over them and remove them with instruments which are suited to them. Then sew the incision and care for it with that which fleshes and consolidates itself from ointments or plasters suitable to it.
But if a wound occurs between the nose, then it is necessary to care for it with licines and administer a lead tube until it heals.
Of the restoration of the lower jawbone, when it is broken.
When the lower jawbone is broken and its fracture is not with a wound: then look if it is a break of it outside only, and does not break into two and bend to the inside: then knowledge of it is easy.
Therefore, if the fracture is in the right part of it, you must insert the index finger of your left hand into the infirm bone: similarly if the fracture is in the left jawbone, then insert the index finger of your right hand: and push with it the prominence of the fracture backward with facility to the outside with your other hand from the inner part of the bone carefully, and with it make it even.
If the fracture of the jawbone is already separated into two parts, then you should apply extension from both sides, according to straightness, until it becomes possible to equalize it.
But if there is already movement or disruption of the teeth, then bind them with what you hope will remain together with a thread of gold, silver, or linen. Then place over the broken jaw a cauterized [piece of] wood. Then place over it a doubled cloth and on top of the cloth a large splint made neatly or a clog skin piece equal to the length of the jawbone.
Then bind it from above according to the ligature that is prepared for it and suits its application until it does not destroy [the bone]; and command the patient to be in calmness and quietness, and place his food as soft drinks.
But if you think that already something is being altered in shape by some means, then hasten and loosen it on the third day. Then straighten out what was altered from it and apply to it a poultice of mill dust with egg white or with sumac flour after you have removed the wood splint, and place soft wool on top of the poultice.
Therefore while that attached poultice remains over it and the disposition of the bone does not change, let it go so that you do not untie it until it is healed or the fracture is bound. For indeed this fracture is often bound for three weeks many times.
And if meanwhile a warm suppurative swelling occurs, then administer to him that which we have said many times for his sedation until the swelling is removed.
And if the fracture is with a wound, consider [the treatment]. And if already fragments or shreds are separated from the bone, then be careful in this and remove those shreds with that which is suitable for their removal by instruments.
If the wound opening is tightened, then enlarge it with a phlebotome according to the quantity of your need.
Then when you have removed the stitches, and nothing remains of them, or then if the wound opening is large: and if not, put over it plasters that suit it, and they will fill it in until it heals.
On the restoration of a fractured clavicle.
Indeed the clavicle is most often broken from the front part of the shoulder and its fracture occurs in one of three ways.
For it either breaks and separates into two, apart from which the stitches occur in it: and it is easier to restore it.
Or so that happens in bone stitches: and it is more difficult to restore it.
Or so that it is a fracture with an open wound.
And the operation in it when the fracture is without a wound is, that you make two attendants present, so that one of them holds the aid that follows the fractured clavicle, and another extends the neck towards the side.
Then straighten the fracture with your fingers until it becomes its proper shape, according to what is needed; and let there not be in it prominence or concavity.
But if you need more extension, then it is necessary that you place under the patient's shoulders a ball of cloth or wool: and let its magnitude be according to the quantity of your necessity: and extend, and raise the clavicle, and bind the ball with your hand until it straightens the fracture, as is proper.
But if you cannot pull the end of the clavicle outwards because it has reached depth: then it is necessary that the patient be placed on his back over his own shoulders: and put under his arm a pillow of medium size in magnitude, and have the attendant press his shoulder downwards until the bone of the clavicle that is in depth is raised upwards: and then straighten the fracture and align it with your fingers.
If however you feel a stone from the hernia and it moves: then it is necessary that you cut above it, and draw out the stone with ease.
If however it is retained in the bone: then cut with skill in the incision of it with one of the saws which you prepared for this purpose, after you have placed under the hernia instrument that keeps the flesh from being lacerated; and the instrument is made of wood or iron. And this is its shape.
It resembles a snail shell, in which there is no concavity.
And its width should be according to that by which you are in need of it, from the magnitude of the bone and its smallness. Its length, however, is also as much as possible for you to do with the operation.
And it should have two ends, as you see them; and one end should be broad, and the other narrow.
If however the disruption of the wound which you cut when you extracted the bone stone is broad, and you secure it from a hot gangrene: then stitch together the two edges of the wound with suture.
And if the disruption is small or you fear a hot gangrene: then fill the wound with cloths and dust according to the size of the cut wound.
Which if a hot gangrene happens, then pour cloth into rose oil, and vinegar, and wine, and place over it.
The stiffness of the bone when it is without a wound and without cutting is, as you apply over the bone an ointment made from mill flour with egg white. And place a warm poultice over it. Then place a sphere under the splint of it, if you need to use one. Then take a hemp rope, that is, the circle which the Saracens hold, which is very long; and let its width be the breadth of one's hand, or about that. Then place a small doubled cloth over the swelling and apply a plaster. Then take a small splint from thin wood, of three finger's breadth in width, and similarly in length. Then wrap it around with cloth. Then wrap that cloth with the splint into place over the spot where the bleeding falls upon the fracture and the place. Then place the pad over the fracture as it is folded, and wrap it around his neck and under his healthy shoulder and under his ailing shoulder: and bring it back over all of it, and as you see that the bandage holds the fracture in its grip: and it is this, which is not hidden from you, and whole, so that the splint should not be removed from above the broken bone. And because of this, you must inspect the ailing person every day. Whenever therefore the bandage loosens and you see that the splint is already being removed: then correct it, and tighten the bandage.
Then place the patient's back on a pillow and put under his shoulder while he sleeps a small pillow at night, so that with him may be lifted up his support from beside himself. The fracture of the arm therefore is lifted by elevation of the shoulder.
Or bind his arm to his own neck: and do not loosen the bandage unless an accident happens in place of a boil or from an abscess until twelve days.
Then renew the plaster, if you see that his face turns to it. And add to the bandage, and let him go until he is restored and has healed.
And the fracture of the arm is indeed tightened and strengthened for the most part within twenty-eight days, and sometimes it happens in some men within a shorter time.
Of the restoration of the fractured Spoon.
The spatula is rarely broken and is used in a wide area, and it does not break except for its edges.
When therefore it breaks from a wide area and breaks in the middle of it, and is not known except by touch: then according to the figure of the break, try to prepare its repair. And reduce it back over its natural shape in every way possible with your mind, which is within your power.
Then place powdered millstone with the white of an egg, and gentle starch, and place a small amount on top from doubled cloth. Then place over it a broad splint made from thin board according to the size of the spatula or somewhat larger than it.
But if there is a concavity under the splint in some part of the spatula, then equalize it with gentle starch until the splint descends according to equality.
Then bind it from above with linen threads long and decent constriction, and the firmness of the splint, until it is not removed from its place.
And inspect the bandage every day. Whenever the bandage loosens, bind it again, and straighten the splint if it becomes dislodged from its place, and let the patient lie on their healthy side.
And indeed the spatula is restored in twenty days or in twenty-five days.
When therefore this number is completed, then loosen the bandage and you will be safe. For it is of bones whose disunion is not to be feared, nor their dissolution.
But if a splinter proceeds from the bone and pierces under the skin, then cut above it and remove it. And do as we said about a broken wing from septicating an abscess of hot pus, if anything happens from that.
On the treatment of a broken chest.
The chest sometimes breaks in its middle part, and it is rarely encountered. Its extremities are often broken and bruised from falls on them.
When its middle breaks downwards and a severe pain occurs to him, and difficulty in breathing, and coughing: and perhaps he vomits blood, and the concavity is shown in the broken bone, which does not remain hidden from sense. Its restoration is, that the weak person be laid on his back: and place soft cushions between his shoulders.
Then press his shoulders and bring the ribs together from both sides with his hands: and make them smooth in balance with each other, as much as possible that it is fitting, until the form of the bone returns, as it should.
Then place on top of him a poultice and plaster, and place above that an astel from a thin board of willow or hazelnut, or something similar to it in lightness, after you have wrapped it in cloth. Then smooth it out by binding it over the broken bone, so that it is not moved; and go with a bandage according to its roundness to the back several times, and bind it tight with a suitable knot.
Then consider the bandage at every hour, and whenever it becomes soft, bind it; and if necessity prompts to its removal because of itching, or corrosion which occurs in the place, or pain, or swelling: then hasten, and loosen it, and remove the poultice, and correct what has occurred with the method of its cure.
Then replace the plaster, if you see it is necessary for that; and apply a tight bandage to him until he is healed.
On the treatment of Ribs, when they are broken.
Know that a fractured rib does not occur except in the places which follow the back and are from the extremities of them from the anterior part. And bruising does not happen to them, unless because they are cartilaginous. And knowledge of it is not hidden from the sense during an investigation with fingers.
And its restoration is done in such a way that you equal the fracture with fingers according to what is possible until it equals the figure, as it should. Then apply poultices and bind the fractured bone with a splint if it needs that.
And if the rib fracture is inclined towards the inside: then a sick and severe pain occurs, and a puncture, just like that which occurs to one who has pleurisy: because the bone pierces the membrane, and also difficulty in breathing, coughing, and vomiting of much blood occurs: and this is difficult to cure.
And now the ancients have been ingenious in it with many devices: and among them are those who say, that you put foods of the infirm which generate inflammation and flatulence, so that the belly is inflamed, and extends, and pushes the fracture to the outside.
We however detest, that it not be a cause of speed in making a warm abscess, if it does not occur. But if it has already occurred, then you add to it.
And some of them said: a poultice should be placed on the place, then it should be sucked with strength, and it is similar in reasoning. However, there is fear that a poultice might attract excess humors to the place because of weakness of disposition.
And some of them said: it is necessary that the place be covered with wool that has already been soaked in warm oil, and that lint be placed between the ribs, until it is filled: so that there may be a bandage equal when you wind it around according to its roundness.
Then let the ailing person be cured with treatment through expectoration by means of food and medicine.
But if some grievous thing oppresses the ailing person, which they cannot bear, and their mouth is pained by an ulcerating puncture, and we fear for them: then it behooves us to cut open above the place, and uncover the broken rib. Then let us lay over it an instrument that preserves stitches, of which mention was made beforehand: and let us cut the mouth with ease, and cut out the ulcer itself.
Then let us bring together the two lips of the wound, if it is large with a suture: and let us treat it with plasters until it heals.
But if a hot pustule should occur meanwhile, then quickly, and pour powdered ashes into rose oil, and place it over the place: and let the ailing person be cured with what suits a pustule.
Meanwhile also a bandage will be stretched over the side above which it sleeps, until it heals.
Chapter On The Restoration of a Fracture of the Vertebra of the Back and Neck.
Indeed the bones of the neck sometimes come to be fractured, and rarely a chafing occurs: and similarly also with the vertebrae of the back. When therefore it happens to anyone, and you wish to know whether it will heal or not heal: then feel. For if you see their hands already softened and stiffened, and dead: and they cannot move them, nor stretch them out, nor constrict them: and when you constrict them with your fingers, and prick them with a needle, he does not feel it, and does not feel pain in them: then know that it is not healing, and in many dispositions is mortal.
But if he moves them, and feels constriction with your fingers and pain from a prick: then know that the marrow of the bone is saved, and that the ailing person will be healed with treatment.
If a similar condition should occur on the spine and you wish to know whether it can be cured or not, then examine his feet. If you see that they have been softened and that there occurs in them what occurs in the hands, and when he lies on his back, flatulence and vomiting occur without will; and when he is laid on his stomach, flatulence occurs without will; and when he is laid on his abdomen and wishes to urinate but cannot: then know that it is mortal. Therefore do not labor in its cure.
But if indeed nothing of that sort occurs to him, the condition is lighter.
And its cure is, as for the inflammation of a boil, to apply a warm poultice to the spine; thus, placing on the spine pounded rose oil alone, or with eggs that are well cooked.
Place over it several times a day the warm poultice until the hot boil is satiated; then place on the spot one of those plasters that are comforting, drying, and tie it on with a bandage: and command the sick person to use quietness and tranquility. And let him not sleep on the part where he finds pain until it is healed.
And if it occurs due to the bruising of a vertebra or something that has already separated from it, then it is necessary to make an incision on the skin and remove it. Then bring together the two edges of the wound, if it is large, with a suture. Then care for him with some ointment from unguents until he heals.
If however the last vertebra of the spine is broken and is at the end of the tail: then it is necessary to insert the finger of the left hand into the anus, and with the other hand, straighten the broken bone as much as possible, and make a second adjustment.
Then place over it [the instrument], and remove it; and care for the wound with the treatment which preceded, until it heals.
Of the restoration of an eye.
Chapter 11.
The bones of the eyes are rarely broken. But if they are broken, then the fracture is not unless there is comminution of their ends; and they split lengthwise and incline to the interior: and an aching pain occurs in the place, and a spot: and the leg that breaks becomes numb.
And indeed its restoration is that you stretch out your hand over it until you stand above the fracture so as to see what shape it has.
But if the fracture is only in its ends, then make it even with the fracture according to what you prepare for yourself from setting, until it is like its natural shape.
But if the fracture is lengthwise, or already inclines to the interior; then make the patient lie on his stomach until you prepare for yourself its restoration of that fracture. Therefore when you have made it even, place over it a poultice; then put over it a splint of wood or of skin, and bind tightly over where you do not fear the change of the fracture, nor the removal of the splint.
And make the concavity even with it and that which fills it, until it takes on the binding according to equality; and command the patient to sleep on his back or on his healthy side.
But if a hot abscess should happen to him, then cease from stretching it and its restoration, until the hot abscess settles; and place over it that which settles it, according to what came before, then return to its restoration; and bind it as is fitting.
If however it happens that a piece of bone is detached or comminuted, extract it.
On the restoration of fractures of the limbs.
The Adiutorium is that which is between the elbow and the head, a spatula.
Therefore if it is fractured, then its restoration is according to one of two manners.
One of them is: that you take a piece of wood flat and of middle thickness, in the shape which follows.
And two bands are tied to both ends.
Then it is suspended from a raised place, and the patient sits on a seat. Then he throws his fractured arm over the wood, until it reaches its elbow joint, where a notch is cut in the middle of the wood.
Then something heavy is suspended above it, so that the weight will stretch out the arm downward. Then the doctor equalizes the fracture with his hands together, until the fracture returns as it should.
And the other method is that the patient lies on his back over his own neck and hang his hand from his own neck with a band. Then order two servants to hold one of them by the parts above the fracture with their hands and have the other hold below it and each stretch as far as his own.
And if you wish the extension to be stronger, then tie one band below the fracture and another above it with another band, and have each of the two servants stretch as far apart as they can.
If the fracture is near the end of the shoulder, then it is necessary to place one band under the elbow joint and another under the fracture towards the wrist.
And similarly if the fracture is near the elbow, then it is necessary to place a band over that place and over the elbow itself.
Then equalize the fracture with ease without effort until when the fracture is equal as it should be and good treatment is applied to it, then tie it if no hot abscess occurs.
If a hot abscess occurs, then leave the bandage until the seventh day. And put over it a cloth that has been cut and soaked in vinegar and rose oil, until the abscess becomes quiet; then bind it. And the way of binding it is, as when you apply an ointment to a fracture.
Then put an inflation from a new cloth over the ointment. Then draw the arm over the assistance and place his hand open on his shoulder.
And put a cloth and bind over the assistance of the arm, so that the arm may stand in the place of joints; if it does not hinder you from doing anything or something is altered on your body from a broken bone.
But if you fear that something is altered on your body from a broken bone, then use splints; and it is as when you place over the fracture itself a splint which is wider and stronger than the other splints. And place between all the splint a breadth of a finger; and let the length of the splints be according to the fracture with an addition of three fingers from each part.
Then bind over the splints with the bandage which I said at the beginning of this chapter.
And it is as if your bandage were tighter on the place of the fracture. And as much as the fracture extends, the bandage should be looser.
If therefore you see the state of the splints and bandage, as we said, in the hour when you restore the member immediately, then do it; and if you fear a hot abscess, then leave the bandage and splints until the seventh day, as we said.
Then look at the ligature in all three days, lest there occur itching, or inflation, or that food should not reach the member because of the disposition of excess tightness of the bandage. Correct therefore that whole thing according to what we said.
If indeed it is above trust, so that nothing comes from it: then do not separate the ligature, unless after three days or more.
And let the sleep of the sick person be on his back, and his hands on his belly: and place under him a bolster full of wool evenly.
And consider at every hour of night or day, lest the figure of the broken member be loosed, or the ligament be softened. Therefore correct that whole thing with your skill.
And give the food to the sick person according to the order we have mentioned, so that the food is subtle at first until he begins to chew well: then it is necessary that you thicken his food.
For it is customary for the assistant and the leg to be set so that they are strong in forty days: then therefore it is necessary that it be released, and a bath be administered, and it be treated with ointments which correct that.
But if the fracture is foul and crushed: then you will not separate from it the ligament and splinters until fifty days, and even until two months.
On the restoration of a broken arm.
The arm is composed of two bones which are called the two humerus.
One of which is small, and it is that which follows the thumb. And the other is large, and it is that which is placed below the small one from the lower part.
Perhaps therefore only the larger humerus breaks, or the smaller one, and perhaps both break together.
When therefore the small upper humerus breaks, then its restoration is easy, and its healing is easier.
And when the lower humerus breaks, it is a bad fracture, and its healing is difficult.
And it is worse still, when both bones break together.
If therefore the bone that is fractured is a femur a little too high up, then it suits the physician for its restoration, to place a small splint with ease, until it fits.
If however the femur that is fractured is large one: then it is necessary to place a stronger splint.
And it is necessary to place the shape of the hand on its restoration, and its splint extended over a cushion. And let the thumb of the hand be above all the fingers higher, and let the lower joint of all the fingers, or the rest, be so; and let the weak person sit in a square on itself, and let the cushion be before him, or in direct line with him, in height, so that the weak person does not incur labor.
Then the assistant should stretch the arm from the lower part, either by hand or with a bandage. And another assistant should stretch similarly from above.
Then the physician should straighten the bone until he reduces it to its better shape as much as possible.
Which if there are splinters in the fracture of the bone, then try to reduce all fragments to their place with your strength.
If however there appears a separated splinter and it pierces the skin, and there is no hope for its restoration: then take it out from above, and remove it according to the method which we mentioned among those that were previously said.
If however the fracture is with a wound, I have already done for that singular chapter, so receive its treatment from there.
Which if it happens at the beginning of your restoration a warm abscess forms, then mix linen with cerote made from rose oil and white wax, and let it be in the middle between thickness and thinness: and bind the linen tightly on it with a subtle bandage, until when the abscess is pacified; then remove the cerote, and place a prepared poultice made from mill dust with egg white. Then place splints, and let the splint placed over the fracture itself be somewhat wider and stronger.
And know that the number of splints for the arm is six in most dispositions; whether the fracture be in one forearm or in both forearms together.
Then place your bandage over the place of the fracture more tightly and severely, and somewhat more to the higher or lower part of it: or put a softer bandage than it slightly, according to what was said at the beginning of this chapter.
And let the cloths that are wrapped over the fracture be soft, moist ones; and not hard, rough ones. And let the thread with which it is tied be linen of its own kind, medium between fine and thick, as we have mentioned.
And observe the member and the ligament after some days. But if anything happens that requires correction, such as itching occurring in the member: then you must, anoint the member with warmed water until that itching ceases, and leave the member not constricted until it ceases; thereafter repeat the bandaging.
But if the bandage is already softened and the bone removed and similar things have happened: then correct that whole thing with your skill, and observe again.
For if nourishment is prevented from reaching the member because of excessive bandaging, then it is necessary to loosen it somewhat and leave it for some days until nourishment flows back to it; then bind it.
But if none of the things we mentioned happen to the patient, then it is not necessary for him to be released until after twenty days, or about that.
Then the hand of the sick person shall be suspended from his neck. And let his neck be even and be guarded with his strength against shaken movements. And make him sleep on his back.
Know that this fracture is restored in thirty days, or possibly in thirty-two days; and perhaps it is restored in twenty-eight days. The whole thing is according to the dispositions of the humors and the dispositions of vitality.
Concerning the restoration of fractures of the hand.
Know that a fracture rarely occurs in the wrist and knuckles of the fingers. And it does not occur to him unless there is much rubbing against it.
When therefore a fracture or rubbing of the sole occurs, then it is necessary for the sick person to be seated in an equal chair: and before him should be a seat. Then place his hand over it stretched out.
Then let the servant stretch out the broken bones and let the doctor shape them until they are joined together with a good conjunction.
Then it is necessary for him to place a poultice and a compress, if a warm aposteme does not occur. Then place a bandage over it from above according to the size of the place: and you have already wrapped it in a linen cloth.
But if the fracture is near the lower part around the belly of the foot, then make a similar sphere out of cloth and order the sick person to bend over it with his fractured foot: afterwards let it be bound with a long cloth.
And the bandage should be of leather in which there is softness, so that the skin may adhere to the body of the foot; and bind it as is necessary.
But if the fracture is on the outside, then it is necessary to place a bandage over from above and another bandage from below on the foot; and let the hand be straight. Then place a splint, as the hand is revolved; and extend between the fingers with a ligament.
If there should happen a fracture of any of the joints of the fingers, then if it is the thumb, it will be set straight according to what is necessary. Then let a bandage be applied with the sole of the foot; and if it is an arm, place on it a splint straight, small so that the fracture may be elevated and not be moved.
But if the fracture is of the remaining fingers, such as the middle, and index, or ring, and little finger, then let it be set straight and bound with the sound finger that follows it; or all should be bound according to their connection; and it is better. Or place over it a small splint, as we said of the thumb.
And consider in your treatment and after the aforesaid arrangement you will apply hot fomentations; and place against it what is needed when something happens to it, according to how its story is repeated.
On the restoration of the fracture of the hip joint.
The bone of the hip joint is often broken and becomes known to sensation because it turns to the inner and back part.
And its restoration is done in this way, that a bandage is applied above the fracture and another bandage below the fracture. And the patient should be stretched out on his back.
Then let every bandage that is applied to him be extended to the other side according to equality; and this when there is a fracture in the middle of the bone.
But if it is a fracture near the root of the hip joint, then let the bandage be to the side of the groin so that the extension falls upwards and another bandage below the fracture.
And similarly if it is a fracture near the knee, then let the bandage be near the knee so that the extension falls downwards.
Then the physician should set the bone with both of his hands until he reduces it according to the similarity of its natural figure and join the bones together with a good connection.
Then it is necessary, that you put on a poultice and a bandage, if an abscess does not occur in the limb. But if it happens that an abscess occurs in the limb: then leave it for a few days until the warm abscess subsides, and then return to your treatment.
And the bandage of it should be such. It is necessary that you wrap something strong, such as a cloth or a bandage, over the broken limb, and double it back on itself two or three times, and let excess remain. Then wrap the leg so that the heel reaches down to the ankle.
And insert a long thread between the hip and the leg near the knee to the lower parts, so that the ends of the thread come from the upper part of both parts. Then wrap over the leg and thigh whatever excess comes out from the bandage.
Then place over the thigh at the place of the fracture small splints, and place one of them over the shinbone.
Then fill the spaces between the leg and thigh with soft cloths to equalize the bandage.
Then begin with a strong bandage from the middle over the place of the fracture three or four windings. And as much as you extend the bandage with ligatures from the place of the fracture, let your bandage be less tight and softer, and gentler.
Then turn to the two ends of the thread which you inserted between the thigh and leg. So bind with them all three splints that are on top. Then go back with the two ends of the thread to the lower parts until you reach the heel of the foot. And with both, tie also the ends of the splints from the other part, so that the bandage is not removed from its place.
Then leave the bandage as it is, so that it does not cause a member to be corroded, nor an abscess, nor swelling, and things like these. If something happens with him. Therefore apply a dressing to it and correct that which has happened from it all over again, just as we have said many times.
If however a splinter of bone is in the bone that pierces: then it must be equalized by you, if it is possible for you. And if not, find something over it and remove that which pierces, and care for him to whom the previous incision was made, until he heals.
And when this fracture of the hip occurs again, besides the splints being added to him, as we said in the aid and arm.
