However, in its restoration when thus broken, lameness does not occur in the patient. And if it is restored alone, besides a leg being applied to him, then it is necessary for him whose it is, to be lame forever.
And know that the hip gets inflamed after fifty days, or is increased a little, or diminished a little; all this is according to the diversity of temperaments and other dispositions.
On the restoration of a fractured patella of the knee.
Know that a fractured patella of the knee occurs rarely, but attrition occurs to it many times.
If however a fracture occurs to it, then only an opening or a cleft or a hole can occur in its parts. And it is with an injury and without an injury. And a dressing is applied to it all over with feeling.
And its restoration is such that what has been disrupted will be equalized with its parts using the fingers, until it is gathered and joined as possible according to equality and facility, and skill.
Then apply a plaster made from sesame flour or mill flour with egg white, and place over it a round splint if you need one, and bind it with suitable bandages on top.
Then consider all the arrangements which we have narrated in other fractures, such as suppurating empyema and similar ones; so, as to oppose every affliction with what is suitable for it, until it is healed.
About the restoration of a fractured leg.
Know that there are two bones in the leg, one thick and named by the name of the leg, and the other subtle and named Fibula.
And it happens to both types of fractures that happen to the two bones of the arm: and because of this, its restoration is done similarly to the restoration of the arm equally, and the operation is one.
Which if the two bones are broken at the same time, the leg turns to all parts.
And if the thinner bone is broken, the leg turns forward.
And if the thicker bone is broken, you will find it below: and it is one of those that are not hidden from you.
Then apply extension, equability, and the binding of the splints according to what is equal.
However it is necessary, if the fracture of the leg is a foul one, having many swellings: that there be less extension, and a milder alleviation by you.
And indeed in the leg there is something like an operation on the arm: and it is, that when you equal the splints, and complete all your operation, then take two strips of pine wood, which is administered in the making of sundials that are put under tables: and they are made from the palm of a spatula: and choose among them those whose thickness is small, nor are they minute: and their length should be according to the length of the leg from the knee down.
Then wind around each strip two wrappings according to its length. And place one part of the leg on one side, and the other on the other side: and they should be from the knee down to the foot.
Then bind two ligatures in three places from both ends and the middle. For when stricture is prevented, the urethra turns to the right and left, and stands in good position.
And now indeed a cradle made of wood should be administered according to the length of the leg, and the leg should be placed in it so that it may be preserved from movement.
And indeed it is most necessary to do this when there is a fracture along with the wound itself properly.
Then observe the leg every day and strive with utmost care to prevent it from becoming inflamed, swollen or suffering any other affliction. So if something of this sort happens, then apply to it whatever is necessary until it heals, if God so wills.
Of the fracture of bones of the foot and fingers.
Indeed a fracture does not occur in the heel completely.
Indeed a fracture occurs to the bones of the foot and fingers at times, and even a little fracture occurs to them; and it does not occur to them unless there is crushing in many dispositions.
But if a fracture occurs to the bones of the foot and you see those bones rising up on top of each other altogether, then let the patient put his foot on the prepared ground as one walking.
Then stand you and place your foot on what is raised from those bones; afterwards press or tread upon it; and equalize it until it returns to its places.
Then place a poultice and a compress on top of it; and place under the foot of the patient a small board with two flat heads.
Then bind it around with the bandage suitable for constriction, after you have wrapped it with cloths and other things which it needs.
When therefore three days or four have passed by from this point, then loosen the bandage. For you will find bones that are broken or fractured, or dislocated. So the operation for them is as you see.
But if some fingers are broken, then restore them and make them even as I told you about restoring the fingers of the hand. Then place a splint on your finger with the astel as long as the finger and a little wider: then place this board under the foot that I told you about, and tie it with a suitable string.
But if what is broken is made of two or three bones, or more, then place a splint as long as the finger on top of all the fingers, wrapped in linen cloth: and tie the board to the lower foot; and let it stand over one end of the board outside the belly of the foot, so that it holds well in place.
And it is fitting for you to remember not to forget all that I have said to you about the other fractures from accidents as we mentioned, and oppose every accident with what is needed.
Of the fracture of a woman's vulva, and of the breastbone of a man, and of the penis of a man.
When the vulva of a woman is broken, then make her sit up straight. Then lean her back a little to the part of her own back and hold it in place.
Then the midwife should fill the vulva with cotton until it is filled and it should be like a sphere inside the vulva.
Then the woman is shaken, and her back is lifted slightly. For that cotton will come out until it is before the vulva as a sphere, and the broken bone will return.
Then put a poultice on her back. And when she wants to urinate, remove the cotton easily, then let her do so; and replace it afterwards according to the manner in which you first inserted it, then return her to her own poultice.
He should make it seven days, or about that time, for indeed the jaw itself is restored.
And if you wish, take the bladder of a sheep, and tie a reed tube over its orifice, and insert the whole bladder into its vulva. Then blow through the tube with force, until the bladder is inflated inside the vulva, for indeed the fracture returns.
Then it will be filled with fat, and stand for a few days, as we have said, until it heals.
And when the bone of the pectoral muscle or woman is broken, then administer it for its restoration and that which we have said about the bones of the shoulder.
And the rectum is not concealed in these nearby fractures which are close together, especially for one who has some study or use.
And understand my book very well, for indeed the fracture itself signifies to you in many arrangements a straight way in its restoration and tightening of itself: therefore understand.
But in the rod of a man when it is broken, take the gullet of a goose: and insert the rod into it. Then put over it a bandage from cloth, and be wrapped up, and let go for three days, or about those, and he will heal, if God wills.
Concerning the restoration of fractures of bones when there is also a wound.
It is fitting for him to whom a fracture occurs with a wound, especially if it is great, such as the hip bone or assistant bone or similar to that, to speed and to let blood immediately: if the conditions of letting blood are attested to you, as we have previously mentioned.
But if the wound lets out blood, then you must hasten to its excision, so that you powder ground zeek over it, if another is not present for you.
Then begin to restore the fracture on that very same day; and do not postpone it if no suppuration occurs.
If, however, a warm suppurating lesion occurs, then abandon the restoration of it until the ninth day, until the suppuration subsides. And do not approach it on the third and fourth days at all, for you would cause bad things to happen to it.
But if the fractured bone is prominent above the skin and visible: then you must endeavor to reduce it, and straighten it out with your hands with ease and a little extension.
But if its extension and alignment by your hands is not prepared: then reduce it with this instrument. And the instrument is made of iron, whose length is the quantity of seven fingers, or eight: and its width is according to the size of the wound. And because of that the physician must take from them three or four according to the quantity as he needs it for that sake of cure in every kind of fracture.
And it should be rounded, and there should be a small thickness in it, so that it does not double back upon itself under compression at the time of the operation: and it should have an acute end, with which there is a edge on its extremities. And its upper part should be to the thickness from its middle up to the lower parts.
And this is its form.
It is necessary in this operation that the acute end of it be curved over the extremity of the prominent bone: and push it with one force until when the bone returns and becomes straightened with some equality.
Therefore, strive to straighten the ends of the fracture mutually.
But if the ends of the fractured bone are fine and do not receive it with good acceptance, then you must cut the end of that bone until the instrument can have power over it.
If you truly cannot reduce them with what we have described entirely, then cut into it with the instrument that suits it from those which we mentioned: or saw it with any saw that is possible for you.
Then smooth out what remains on the bone from roughness and small scabs.
Therefore when the bone is reduced and the sick person after reduction finds a severe pain hurting him, then you will know that the bone has not returned to its natural place.
So do that thing. For you will be giving great aid to the sick person, when your restoration which you are doing for the bone is completed.
Then immerse a cloth in black wine of vinegar. And especially if it be in summer. And do not place over the wound a cloth that is greasy, nor anything in which oil is, so that it will not cause to happen in itself decay and corruption.
Then administer theriac at the hour of completion of your restoration of the bone. And leave the found wound thus, so that you cut with scissors a hole in the bandages according to the size of the wound.
And beware with all care lest you close the wound with the fracture. For many times foolish doctors have done this and caused to happen over their sick patients either death, or corruption, or gangrene, that is, putrefaction. And let your stitch be soft, gentle, and different from the stitches of other fractures.
For if the wound is bad or a great wound and you fear over it some evil accidents that we have narrated, and you find pain in the place suspended: then it is not necessary to put theriac on it. And make bandages from hard cloths over the place of the theriac and tie them on it.
When however it is a day or two after and you see the wound beginning to suppurate, as it were being generated with pus: then take off from above that cloth which you placed over it with wine. Then administer linseed and ointments with which we are accustomed to heal wounds, as the unguent of four remedies, and similar things.
And it is fitting for you to untie the bandage. And consider the wound every morning and evening until it becomes firm and heals.
And it is necessary that the member be prepared in such a way that the pus may flow from it downward with ease.
But if many days pass over the wound and it does not become firm, nor is the pus shed from it: then know that there are small splinters of bone. Therefore it is necessary to examine the wound with a probe. As many as are separated from them, remove them and take out those things. And what are not separated from them and pierce the member, and cause pain to occur, try to cut them off and remove them in any way that is possible for you.
But if there occurs to the wound a gangrene or ecchymosis, that is an ulceration, or some other kind of corruption and putrefaction: then it is necessary to apply to each of them with what cure suits it, as has been told about it in its own chapter.
And from what I have said, and in which you will make present to yourself the mind when a bone is broken and protrudes over a member, such as the os coxae, and assistive ones, and similar ones from large members, it is that you should not attempt to bring it into place, for death often occurs from this, nay, leave it alone until it becomes putrid.
For perhaps it will fall on its own after twenty days or thirty.
Then therefore treat the wound, if it seems good to you; and otherwise, leave it.
On the treatment of swelling in a track of a fracture.
This swelling often occurs in the path of healing a fracture. Especially near the joints: and it is disfigured by the shape of the limb. And perhaps it prevents the limb from performing its natural movement.
Therefore consider then, if the swelling is recent: administer to it medicines which have an astringent quality, such as aloe, and frankincense, and myrrh, and resin of the tree called sarcocolla, and acacia, and similar things.
Thus take some of them, or all of them; and prepare them with astringent wine, or with egg white, or with vinegar; and place them on the swelling in a compress, and bind them over it with a good bandage.
And leave the bandage, nor loosen it for many days. Then loosen it, and apply another one, until the swelling is removed.
Or bind over it a thin lead plate. For lead has the property of removing all that is feared from the limbs.
But if the swelling has already turned to a stone and is hard and presses urgently to be removed: then make an incision over it from above down, and cut out the superfluous part that protrudes; or rasp it with some rasps until it is removed: and care for the limb until it heals.
On the treatment when it is restored, and the member remains after that subtle, not according to its natural form.
When a bone fracture is restored and the member remains after it subtle, weak: then that does not happen unless for many causes. One of these is the multitude of solutions to ligaments and ligation thereof not according to what is fitting: either on account of excess tightness of the ligaments, until it prohibits nourishment from flowing into the member; or on account of the abundance of superfluous embrocation at an improper time: or on account of the scarcity of blood in the body of the infirm person, and its weakness.
And the cure for this is to feed the infirm person, and to make their body tender, until blood multiplies in them. And administer a warm bath. And bring joy and gladness over him.
Then place pitch on the member, so that it draws pitch to itself as a great nourishment. And apply its ointment with warm water, until nourishment flows and returns to its natural shape.
On the cure of broken bones when they are set crookedly.
When it happens that a member already restored after its cure is bent and protrudes with the broken bone, or swells: and becomes disfigured in that form of the member, but the member is not prevented from performing its natural function: then you should not believe his words who thinks that the member must be broken anew.
And already there have been many foolish doctors and restorers who did it in our land.
And indeed this operation is very reprehensible, leading to death or a great fear, so that it may be mortal.
But if the stiffness and knotting are recent: then it is necessary that they be fomented with warm water, in which have been decocted softening herbs, such as the leaves and root of hawthorn, and meliloth, and similar things. And make a poultice for him with a softening plaster, such as well-made diachylon.
A poultice of the juice or sap of the elder tree root should be taken and pounded with chicken fat or sesame oil, and applied thereto.
Figs should also be taken and rubbed with pigeon dung, and similar things which are called from medicines diminishing consolidation.
And sometimes the knot is loosened by frequent, old, and gentle friction made with moist hands. And motion should be given to the limb in every direction at all hours.
But if the curvature has already proceeded and is strong, and has turned into a stone, and necessity for its cure with iron has been provoked: then it is necessary that what lies above it be sawed off, and the continuity of the bone be loosened, and what superfluously remains from the knot or bone be cut out with delicate scalpels; and ease be given to it with solicitude and effort.
About the treatment in dislocations.
A dislocation is the removal of some joint from its place. Wherefore it is forbidden from its motion and the figure of the member is spoiled. And it causes great pains and severe injuries to the sick person.
Therefore when a dislocation occurs in someone, it is necessary to hasten to restore it, nor should delay be made at all.
For if it is delayed, the place becomes inflamed and perhaps the reduction of the dislocation as well.
On account of this therefore it is not necessary to move or stretch it in the hour of its inflammation. For very often it causes spasms and hurting pains.
But when that happens, then it is necessary to hasten to bleed the sick person. Then let him be left until the inflammation subsides.
Then anoint the limb with warm water and oil. Then it should be returned with ease.
And every member should be dressed back with him whose recollection comes to its place.
I have indeed arranged the chapters on dislocations, even according to what preceded in fractures, from the upper part of the body down to its lower part.
On the care for the dislocation of the lower jawbone.
The two jawbones are rarely dislocated unless very slightly.
And the dislocation of both occurs in either of two ways. For they are either removed from their places with slight removal and become slightly relaxed. Or they are dislocated with complete, full dislocation until they become relaxed up to the chest, until the patient's saliva runs and he cannot hold it, nor can he close his mouth, and his tongue is rolled back around his speech.
When however its dislocation is slight, then it returns in many positions by itself with ease.
If however the dislocation is complete, then it must be reduced quickly and not delayed at all. And it is necessary for the minister to hold the head of the patient and for the physician to insert his thumb of one hand into the root of the jawbone's dislocation between his teeth, if the dislocation is on one side. Or he should insert both of his thumbs at once if the dislocation is from two sides. And the rest of his fingers should be outside to apply pressure.
And command the patient to clean his jawbones and clear them for eating on every side. And the physician should apply pressure to the jawbone and push it until it returns to its place.
And if the reduction of it is difficult, especially if both jawbones are together then administer a poultice with warm water and oil until the reduction of both becomes easy: nor delay the reduction of both altogether as we said.
When therefore they return and congeal, and the mouth of the ailing person is closed and does not soften: then place on top of them woolen pads with wax made from wax, and rose oil.
Then it will be bound with ease with soft bandages.
And let the patient lie on his back and his head be placed upright between two pillows, so that he may not move it to the right or left nor try to masticate anything. Nay, let a gentle draught be given him until when the pains are removed and the jaw is loosened: then let him eat what he wills, and it will be administered with ease.
He should not struggle at the operation of his mouth during eating and drinking and gaping until the jaw is loosened and healed.
But if the reduction of the two jaws is difficult when they are dislocated at some time and do not return to their place: then many times fevers and constant sweating occur, and perhaps the patient's belly will be dissolved, and perhaps he will vomit pure bile.
When therefore you see that, then know that it is a disaster, and he often dies to whom that occurs within ten days.
On the reduction of the dislocation of the collarbone and the extremity of the shoulder.
The collarbone indeed is not dislocated from the inner side because of its continuity with the chest. And it becomes dislocated sometimes to the outside and this is made known by sense.
And its restoration is that the patient lie on his back and stretch out his arms. Then bind the place with your palm a strong constriction, for it will return.
Then put on top of it a plaster and woolen pads, and tie it.
But his extremities which follow the elbow and are connected with it, do not become dislocated unless rarely. When a joint is dislocated on some day: then it must be reduced and adjusted according to what we said, and what is prepared for you.
Then place over it a poultice, and a stricture. And command the patient to adhere to quietness and rest until they are healed.
And when this same cure is applied, the arm is reduced when it becomes separated from its place also.
Concerning the reduction of a dislocated shoulder.
Know that the shoulder does not dislocate except in three ways.
One of which is, that it dislocates to the upper part towards the lower. The second is, that it dislocates to the breast side. And perhaps it dislocates to the upper part of the shoulder, and that happens rarely.
And it does not dislocate towards the back because of the blade. And it does not dislocate towards the front because of the nerves.
And indeed it dislocates and comes out towards the lower side towards the armpit: especially in those whose flesh is scarce. For it comes out quickly in them and returns quickly.
But in those whose flesh is abundant, it happens contrary to that, namely because it comes out with difficulty and returns with difficulty.
And perhaps it happens to some people by a blow or a fall, and the shoulder is inflamed with a warm inflammation, and it is thought because of that, that it has already become dislocated.
Therefore you must experience that, until you are sure of it, and then proceed to its cure.
And a dislocation is recognized when it is towards the lower side towards the armpit: and distinguish between a dislocated shoulder and a healthy one. For you will find among them a manifest difference, and you will find in the head of the arm a concavity, and under the little bony point near the touch of the head of the arm as if it were an egg: and the infirm person cannot reduce his hand to his ear, nor move it with all movements.
And similarly, if it is dislocated towards the chest, or upwards: then you will find a manifest touch, nor is it hidden.
And indeed an easy reduction of this dislocation occurs when it is recent, or the child is weak.
And its reduction is, that the assistant lifts his hand up to the upper part. Then place your thumbs under the bony point of it, and lift the joint with force up to its place: and the assistant lifts his hand and extends it upwards. Then set it back down, for indeed it will quickly return itself.
But if it does not return through what we said, and there have been many days since the dislocation occurred: then it is necessary that the infirm person be bathed in hot water, and an embrocation be administered which softens and soothes, as if roots of celery, and fenugreek, and melilot were to be boiled in water, and it should be given.
Then the infirm person is placed on his back: and a ball of wool or flax is placed under the bony point of him which is equal between softness and tightness.
Then the doctor places his heel on the ball, and lifts the head of the arm with force, and draws the hand of the infirm person downwards.
And if you wish, reduce him according to this method: and it is, that you make the man who is taller than the infirm person be present, and stand at his side, and insert his arm under the bony point of the infirm one: and lift the bony point of him upwards, until the infirm one is suspended in the air. And another attendant should draw the patient's hand down towards his lower intestines. But if the patient is light, then it is necessary that something else be hung with him to weigh him down, for the dislocation returns immediately.
And it is also reduced according to another method, which is that a long wooden beam be placed on the ground, with its top being rounded in shape, like a mortar cover, and not too thick nor too thin. Then cloths are placed under the patient after cloth have been put on top of the wooden beam, and the patient stands upright according to the length of the beam.
Then his hand is extended downwards from the opposite side, and his body is extended with force on the other side, for the joint returns to its place quickly.
But if its reduction is difficult through all that we have said, then use this cure: which is to take a piece of wood whose length is two arm-lengths, and width four fingers, and thickness two fingers; and whose top is rounded, so that it may facilitate its entrance into the deep crevice, in this shape.
Then soft cloths are tied on top of the rounded wood so that it does not hurt the sick person.
Then it is placed under the patient's crevice, so that his hand or arm may be extended over the wood down to the lower part; and the wood should be tied above the assistant and the arm and the extremity of the hand.
Then his arm is placed over the step of the ladder in breadth, and his hand is extended downwards, and the rest of his body is left suspended on the other side. For the joint enters immediately.
When therefore its entrance is completed in whatever way it may be possible, then it is necessary to place under the crevice a ball of wool equal in size. Then place an ointment prepared from flour of the mill with frankincense and egg white over his shoulder all around, as it rolls off on top.
Then a cord is to be bound under the navel with some decent stricture: and the ligament is to be rolled over the plaster above; and its hand is to be joined to its neck, and it is to be left, not moving its hand for seven days.
And food should be put before the sick person in small amounts until the limb is comforted; for it will be more quickly on its way to healing.
Then a larger [one] should be put in place after seven days, or five, and it should be exercised with movement. Therefore if it is strengthened and not softened, then it is already healed.
If the joint is dislocated many times, either because of humidity that happens to it, or for another cause: then it is necessary, that a cautery with three holes be administered into it, according to what was mentioned earlier in the chapter on cautery.
But if this is done entirely, and the ligament is dissolved after seven days, and the joint does not hold firm, and the plaster and stricture are applied to it many times, and still it does not hold firm and falls, and cannot lift itself up higher: then you should know that its nerves which are at the head of the humerus have already been cut, or stretched, or softened; and you should know that the joint will never be fixed in its proper place.
But the dislocation which occurs towards the breast and nipple, or to the backside, is reduced with a push and stretching with hands: until it returns, and there is administered into it the remaining stricture and care, until it heals.
And if heaviness occurs in the member and slowness in its movement after healing: then the patient should make use of a bath many times, until that heaviness is alleviated and it returns to its original nature.
On the cure of the dislocation of the elbow.
You should know that the joint of the elbow is dislocated with difficulty, and similarly it returns to its place with difficulty also; and this very joint is dislocated in all parts, and especially to the front or back.
And its dislocation is not hidden from you: because it hangs under sight, and under touch, however it may be dislocated in shape. And when you join the dislocated arm with the sound one, it is made clear to you. For the joint swells and the arm cannot be straightened nor can it touch its own shoulder.
And you must hurry to reduce the dislocation at once, before a hot abscess occurs.
For if a hot abscess occurs, its reduction becomes difficult and it will hardly ever be cured, especially when it is in the back. For it is worse than all kinds of dislocations and has a more severe pain, and death often accompanies it.
And its restoration, when it can be reduced from them, is for his hand to be stretched out with both hands, and his arm to be extended, and the doctor's hands to be above the elbow and below it, and he himself should push the joint with the tips of his fingers together, or with the root of his palm, until it returns to its place.
Or indeed the dislocation is in the front: then it returns so that he doubles his hand with his own hand until it strikes the elbow which is dislocated with the root of his palm.
Which if the dislocation does not obey to reduction, then administer a strong and forceful stretching, so that two servants extend his arm, and also hold him, so that he may not be moved during the stretching.
Then let his arm be turned back to its original position after a doubled and long cloth or wide bandage has been wrapped over his hand.
And when the doctor touches the joint with his hand, he should anoint it with oil so that it may be among those things which assist in the smoothness of the joint with ease. Then let him push the joint with a strong thrust until it returns.
And after reducing it, a poultice should be placed on top of it, with astringency and drying with egg white: and it should be tightened with a suitable bandage. And the arm is suspended from the neck of the patient: and let him rest for several days.
Then it should be dissolved. Therefore if the joint is strengthened in its place, then loosen the bandage from it and let it go.
And if you see the joint not being well tightened, then reapply the poultice and bandage, and let it be for several days until it is tightened. Then loosen it.
But if heaviness occurs to him after the reduction of the joint and it is disposed in motion: then give an ointment for bathing, and subtle friction, and compression, until it becomes smooth, or place over the inflamed joint the fat of a ram's tail. Then it should be bandaged, and let it be on her day and night. Then remove it, and introduce him into a bath. When therefore he sweats, then rub the joint with tempered friction. Then reapply over it a plaster like once, and twice, and third time with the entrance of the bath, until it becomes smooth.
And if you wish, place over it cow dung, moist, warmed with butter, and bind tightly on it.
Do that several times. For indeed it becomes smooth, and returns to its proper condition.
Chapter on the cure of a dislocation by Almahassem.
The hand of Mahassem is frequently dislocated, that is, the joint which is between the arm and the hand.
And the reduction of his dislocation is easy. On the contrary, to other joints. However it is necessary, that there be swiftness in the reduction of its dislocation within an hour when it dislocates, before the place becomes inflamed; or a warm swelling occur in it.
And the reduction of its dislocation is, that you place the arm of the infirm on a table, and the attendant stretch out his hand to hold it; and the physician put his palm over the prominence of the joint, and push it until it returns.
However it is necessary to consider if the palm has already receded towards the belly of the hand; then let the infirm person place the back of his hand on the table by extension and reduction: and if the dislocation is proceeding towards the back of the hand, then the position of his hand should be internally on the table, that is, the inner part of his hand should be placed on the table so that the hand of the physician falls upon that very prominence of the joint.
So if it returns immediately, that is well; and if not, then bind it with a plaster to soothe the swelling: and let him go, nor do you repeat it.
For if it does not tolerate, nor can you effect a reduction after the passing of days, however the joint remains according to its own tortuosity: and no harm comes to the infirm person, except that his hand is softened, and he cannot bind anything: then know that the nerves are severed or bruised.
It is not therefore a condition in it, except to be bound with a cautery near its case. And perhaps that does nothing good.
So when Almahasseem does not return, then place over him a plaster as we have narrated. Then let it be bound and let go for five days: afterwards it shall be loosened and let go, and the hand exercised.
But if it be deprived of its motion and something serious occurs in it: then temper it with warm water and sometimes friction until it is tempered.
On the treatment of the dislocation of fingers.
When a finger is dislocated in any direction, or towards the back of its palm or belly: then stretch out the finger and push the dislocation with your thumbs until it returns.
Then support the head of the finger and hang it towards the side to which it is dislocated, and let it go for two days.
Then hang him up and stretch him until it is straight on that day itself.
When therefore night comes, bind it according to its own narrative, and cease not to release it by day, and exercise it with motion, and bind it at night.
Do that for a few days until it is tightened.
And do the same with him if he is dislocated to the belly of the hand: and bind him to that side, and perform your first operation with him until it heals.
And do the same with him when he is dislocated in other parts.
On the treatment of the dislocation of the back vertebra.
When any of the vertebrae of the back or neck are completely dislocated, or many of them are removed from their places: then there is no cure for it, because death approaches the sick person.
And the sign of it is that the sick person's body issues pus without his will, nor can he hold it back: and many times some of his limbs become soft on him, or both his legs, or both his arms, or one of them: or a vertebra is removed from its place.
For often it happens that they are removed, which often takes place in four parts.
Therefore what is removed to the back is called Humpbackness.
Its cure however is, as you should consider then, if humpbackness happens from childhood: then there is no cure for it, nor is there any healing from it.
In those things however which occur by chance or stroke or similar ones, the ancients have already spoken in length about methods of treatment. Of which a great many do not yield with a good outcome.
Indeed I have now shortened from it because of the little that justifies against its much by my proximity to intention and through my explanation with which I explained it and formed its instrument in a different way than they showed it and presented it.
Therefore, I say that in the swelling which occurs from the front part, there is no cure and no healing from it.
And similarly in that which occurs to both sides as well.
And they do not get cured from them, unless that which occurs on the back properly, with this that I say and it is, so that the weak one stretches over his face over a flat surface near the wall: and a wet cloth is spread under him, so that his chest may not be hurt.
Then a straight and fixed piece of wood is put in a hole on the ground at the end of the platform, near his head: and another piece of wood near his feet at the other end of the platform: and the servant holds the stick and it is not fixed in the hole. And another servant hold the other one.
Then wrap over the head of the sick one and under his cheek a strong soft bandage: and stretch out the end of the bandage to the wood that is by his head: and bind it to it.
Then tie another bandage above his armpits and over his knees, and at the crooks of his elbows. Then gather all the bandages together and bind them to the other wood that is by his feet.
Then all the servants stretch out to the part of the wood with the bandage and they should not be removed from their places where they are fixed: but however they should not be fastened, as we said.
And the physician should place his palms strongly upon the splint until it returns (to its normal shape): or place a board on top of it. Then he attaches his feet to the board until it returns.
But if it does not return with this treatment, then take a board whose length is about three arms. And an opening in the wall which we said is near the sick person, a place into which the end of the board enters. Then place the middle of the board on the splint. And the physician should place his feet on the other end. And he should bind it well until the splint is constricted and returns to its place.
Then if you wish to do it with a lathe that is wrapped around your hand: and indeed, you fix into the ground near the head of the sick person at the last step two pieces of wood, whose length is one arm, and under his head in the last step two pieces of wood.
And let the length between all the wood be one palm: and thus it is made at every place a hole through which the lathe runs: and let every piece of wood be firmly fixed to the earth very much, so that it may not move at all.
And a round piece of wood is inserted: and the lathe is in which the tendon is turned around, in two holes of two pieces of wood.
And at its end is a hole in which the wood is fixed, whose length is with that which turns together: and similarly in two other pieces of wood.
Then bind the bandages which you bound on the chest of the sick person with the lathe that is at his head: and the bandages which you bound on his legs with the lathe that is at his feet.
Then stand beside every lathe the servant who turns his hand with an iron stick, with which the lathe is turned: and the physician should shape the splint, as we have said.
This is the form of a saw which follows.
Then after Spondilus has healed and the place has been made level, you must put on a drying plaster with egg white. Then dress it. Then place above the plaster a splint of wood whose width is three fingers, or about that. And its length shall be as much as covers the gibbous place and above the healthy part of Spondilus.
Then it will be bound with the bandage which we mentioned. And the patient should be nourished with gentle food until he is healed.
But if something remains elevated in the place at the end of healing, then it is necessary to administer medicines that soften and calm, with the application of the board which we mentioned over a long time. And now lead is applied to it.
And sometimes a prominence occurs in the last vertebra of the back and it is thought to be a dislocation, and that bone which now protrudes. Therefore it should not be presumed on with this medicine, lest perhaps death will result.
About the cure of a dislocated Ancah.
Know that the dislocation of Ancah and the dislocation of the shoulder joint does not occur except for a dislocation alone; nor do they suffer what other joints suffer from a small displacement and hollowing.
And indeed the Ancah joint is dislocated in four parts. Which is because it dislocates both forward and backward.
And indeed it dislocates most of all to the inside, and little to the front and back.
And the cure of its dislocation to the inside is this: because when you join the sick leg of the infirm with a healthy one, it is longer; and his knee is more prominent than that of the healthy one, and he cannot bend down his foot at the groin: and the place which follows the groin is inflamed, on account of the head of the hip having reached there.
And the sign of that in whom a dislocation occurs outward is, that are his accidents contrary to those accidents.
And the sign of that in whom a dislocation occurs forward is, because he straightens out the healthy leg according to its measure: yet he does not double it without pain that is in the knee; and if he attempts to walk, he cannot put his foot forward over it; and his urine is retained, and his thigh becomes inflamed, and at walking is a bruise on his heel.
And the sign of that in whom a dislocation occurs backward is, that he does not straighten out his knee, nor can double it until he doubles his thigh; and his leg is also shorter on one side: and his thigh is softened, and there is a prominence at the head of the hipbone.
The reduction, however, of this kind of dislocation is to consider then, if the dislocation is old, which already becomes stale with having it. Then if you administer a reduction to him and he remains according to his condition: then there is no treatment at all for it. Therefore it should not be supposed that it is completely incurable.
But in him whose dislocation is recent, and is of one of the four kinds of dislocations, then hasten to this, that you turn the joint and extend it inward and outward, and move it right and left: perhaps it will return, and he will not need any other treatment.
But if it does not return in this way, then it is necessary that a strong instrument be prepared, and the leg be extended from below; or with his own hands, or with a bandage, which is to be tied over his leg above the knee: and another assistant extend his hand from above, so that he may insert his hands under the prominences of his thigh. Then let him bind it with a soft bandage over the head of the hipbone, and hold the other end of the bandage.
And let its extension be either from the front of the groin to the furca [or] from the back to the back of the torso.
And let the extension of all be vice versa one, until the sick person is lifted with his body from the earth, and remains hanging.
For this kind of extension is a common form of the four kinds of forms.
Therefore if the dislocation returns with him to what I said, it is good; and if not, then it is necessary for all species of that which I will say about proper cure.
Indeed in its own reduction when the dislocation is to the innards, it is necessary that the sick person be placed supine over his healthy side. Then he should place a bandage on top of the femur's root, in that which is between the head of the hip and the place that is under the groin. Then extend the bandage upward from the front of the groin to the higher part of the body, to the part of the furca.
Then let another strong assistant take hold of his arms and reduce the swollen place of the sick hip, and extend it to the outside with a violent extension. For now it is returning to its place.
And this kind of treatment is simpler than the other kinds of cures in which this member is reduced.
But if it is lifted above you and does not obey you for entering with this kind of treatment entirely: then it is necessary that the two feet of the sick person be bound together at once with a strong, gentle bandage, over the two heel bones, and over the two knees; and let the length of each one from its own comparison be four finger's breadth in quantity; and let the sick leg be extended more than the other by the quantity of two fingers.
Then the sick person should be suspended over a wooden beam in his house; and let its length from the ground be two arm's breadth.
Then command a strong youth to protect the head and pelvis of the patient: and let another young man support him; and let another youth push with all his strength, protecting the hip with force, for the joint returns to its place quickly.
But if it is his own reduction and there is a dislocation to the outside, it is necessary that the patient lie on a platform, just as we have said about one having a hump. And let the youth stretch the ligament over his own weak leg and over his chest.
Then two sticks are placed, one by his feet and the other by his head. Then a stick is placed in the middle of the platform, firmly fixed: over which damp cloths are wrapped, so that the patient is not hurt; and let the stick be between his hips, so that he does not bend down at extension.
Then the attendants stretch out in their direction, and the doctor adjusts the reduction with his own hand.
If then it submits to reduction, it will be good.
And if not, place over it a board, and press down upon it, just as we said about a hump, equally. However, it is necessary that the patient lie on his healthy side.
And when the dislocation is to the front, then it is necessary that the weak leg be stretched quickly; and this is according to the same account on a platform: and the doctor places the palm of his right hand over the groin of the patient. Then he compresses it with the other hand, and with that compression place him extended to the lower part towards the knee.
And if the dislocation is to the back: then it is not necessary that the patient be extended downwards, and be lifted up from the earth; nay, it is necessary that he be placed on a hard surface, just as it also has to be done if his thigh bone is dislocated outwards, according to what we said about his lying on two thighs: and it is over his face, and stretched ligaments: just as we said recently.
And it is necessary that a compression be administered with a board even over the place to which the joint protrudes, when reduction is completed as is fitting.
And the sign of its return is not hidden from you: and it is, that you stretch out the two legs of the weak one: and when you see them equal, and the weak one tightens his leg and expands it without excuse: then know that the limb has already returned as is fitting: then join together the two thighs, and apply a poultice, and bind with a bandage, that is, a spiral: so that the joint does not move to any part: and let the weak one rest quietly for three days, or four.
Then dissolve the bandage and poultice, and compare the leg with the other: then if you see them equal in state, then know that the dislocation is already fixed: so release the weak one to walk.
And if you see something of softening: then return and apply a poultice to it, and bind it according to your first stricture: and leave it even for three days.
Then dissolve that and make it delay in walking on both sides for some days, until it is comforted well.
¶ On the Dislocation of the Knee.
The knee is dislocated in three ways. It is dislocated towards the outside, towards the inside, and towards the lower part, that is, towards the back.
And it does not become dislocated towards the front.
And the sign of its dislocation is that you order the patient to place his leg against his hip. But if it does not adhere with the hip then know that the knee is dislocated.
And the restoration of all modes of its dislocation is that you make the patient sit up straight, so that he can already stretch out his leg if he can over it, and attach your back to his face: and place his foot between your feet.
Then bind his knee with your palms and place them straight between your fingers over his knee. Then apply both sides of his knee with the flat of your hand with force. And let another attend to stretch out his foot until his knee comes back to its place.
And the sign of recovery is, that the thigh adhere with the hip in calmness, not painfully.
Then apply a poultice to him and bind his thigh with his hip. Then bind them together with a bandage for three days or four. Then loosen them.
And the sick person should not walk for a few days until he is healed.
But if his return to place is refused to you, then apply a strong extension with bands of which the preceding narration was spoken in the cure of the knee, until it returns.
The treatment for the cure of dislocated calcaneus.
The heel is sometimes removed with a small displacement, and sometimes it becomes dislocated according to its completion. And its dislocation occurs either towards the inside or towards the outside.
And the sign of its dislocation is, that you see the heel swollen, proceeding to the side towards which it is dislocated.
But the cure of its removal is easy return: and it is, that he be extended with ease by hand, and adjusted, until it returns.
But in its cure when it is dislocated according to completion, it is necessary that you make the sick person sit upright: and have a strong attendant hold him from behind his back, at the midpoint of himself.
Then take hold with your right hand his foot from the upper part of it, and with your left hand from the lower part of his foot at the place of the heel.
Then pull the foot towards you with your right hand. Then with the left hand two fingers.
Then stretch out with the left hand and push the foot's chest with the right hand towards the knee without effort.
Do that twice as we have described.
Then push the foot's chest to the knee for a third time, and you draw out the bandage.
If therefore it returns in one way, or in two ways according to this method, and you see the foot equalized, it is good; and if not, then repeat the procedure on it, since it will return.
But if reduction is prevented from happening as we have described, then make the patient lie on his back on the ground and place a sturdy peg into the earth so that it falls between his thighs: and you meanwhile have wrapped a cloth over him so that the patient will not be hurt.
Then let the attendant take hold of his thigh; afterwards let the attendant straighten out another foot, or with his hands, or with a bandage that ties over the ankle of the foot. Then let the attendant stretch himself against the stretching of his own limb; and the peg shall be standing between the two thighs of the sick man holding him so that his body is not pulled towards the lower parts during the stretching.
Then let the doctor equalize the dislocation with his hands and the attendant shall hold the healthy leg at the lower part. For the dislocation returns quickly.
For when the dislocation returns and your treatment is declared successful: then apply a plaster and stuffing, and bind it firmly with bandages, and tie the foot with a bandage to the leg.
And you must beware or be afraid of the nerves which are above the posterior calcaneum, so that the bandage is not very tight over them and hurts them.
Then let it go for two days, or three. And if the bandage becomes loosened, tie it again; afterwards untie it on the third day, or fourth: and forbid the sick person from walking for forty days. If indeed he is able to walk before that space of time, no security is created lest a dislocation be dissolved over him, and be corrupted, and not receive cure after that.
If it happens to him that a hot aposteme forms: then it is necessary for you to administer to him on his seat the thing of which our story went before in another place concerning treatment and anointing until it is removed.
On the dislocation of the fingers of the foot, and its cure.
It is necessary for him to be aligned with what has been dislocated from them with a few extensions without effort; and indeed that is not difficult, nay rather easy.
But if the dislocation is in certain knots of the foot or heel: then it is necessary for the patient to sit down his foot on a place level with the earth, or on a board: and he stands as one walking.
Then you stand, and put your foot on that which protrudes from those joints. Then step on it with your foot with force until it returns and you see the joint itself now aligned, and no longer appears to protrude.
Then place under his foot a board that takes up the whole foot with two ends. Then bind it with a suitable bandage for three days. Then loosen it and make him rest from walking for many days until it is bound and secured from slipping back into dislocation.
On the kinds of dislocations with a wound or fracture.
When something happens from that and you are attempting its cure and restoration, death often ensues; and on account of this it is not to be presumed over its cure, which is of this kind, except by one who is wise in the art through long practice, merciful, pious, slow, not rash or presumptuous: and that he should make use of medicinal remedies which calm hot apostemes only; and let the patient be released according to divine judgment. Unless you know, or hope for salvation from perdition with gentleness of the affliction, and where something appears that gives hope. Therefore be able to reduce it at once in the beginning of the thing, before a warm abscess occurs.
So if the member returns, as we wish, then administer a regimen that will calm the abscess; and care for the wound with it that is suitable to it from dry ointments.
Which if the dislocation is with fracture, and happens in bones separated from each other, then try to remove them, and do in that what we said about simple afflictions from what preceded in their places.
And beware according to your power, and guard yourself from entering the way of fear: according to what my preceding warning has been to you.
For it is better for you with your Creator, and more lasting to your glory, and safer to your body among them at your time.
